The Hanafi Theory of Fasād and Its Historical Context
Historical and philosophical discussion of Hanafi fasād theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi theory of fasād, distinguish it from buṭlān, and explain its historical role in Ottoman legal adaptation to modern transactions.

Core Idea
Fasād is a distinct Hanafi intermediate category between validity and voidness, historically flexible enough to accommodate modern contracts.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa discusses the Hanafi theory of fasād (voidability) and its historical application in Ottoman law. He contrasts it with the concept of buṭlān (voidness) found in positive law and other schools, and explains how the Hanafi approach, though restrictive in some ways, was considered moderate. The passage sets the stage for understanding how later jurists adapted these principles to new economic realities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key distinction
Recognize that Hanafi jurisprudence distinguishes between fasād (voidability) and buṭlān (voidness). Fasād allows a contract to produce some effects until annulled, while buṭlān makes it legally nonexistent from the start.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.
2
Position the Hanafi school
Note that the author describes the Hanafi position as 'moderate' compared to other schools that either treat all invalid conditions as void or are more lenient.
The text states 'mutawassiṭan bil-nisbati ilā ghayrihi'.
3
Understand the practical consequence
Because many conditions are considered 'voidable' (mufsid), many contracts become fasid. This creates legal complexity but also flexibility.
The passage says 'aṣbaḥat ḥawādith al-fasād fī al-'uqūd kathīratan.'
4
Analyze historical adaptation
In the late Ottoman period, new rights (intellectual property, insurance) required legal recognition. The Hanafi theory of fasād allowed jurists to adapt by considering customary practices ('urf) to uphold such contracts.
The text refers to 'amthāl al-ḥuqūq' that were not previously known.

الفصل السابع والخمسون
موقف القانون من الفساد، ورأينا في الموضوع ١/٥٧ - الفقه القانوني لم يعرف نظرية الفساد التي أسسها الاجتهاد الإسلامي مثلما عرف نظرية البطلان كما سنرى .
والاجتهاد الحنفي الذي جاء بنظرية الفساد وكان عليه قضاء الدولة العثمانية، قد كان من الاجتهادات التي ضيَّقت من حرية سلطان الإرادة في الشروط العقدية، وإن كان يعتبر متوسطاً بالنسبة إلى غيره كما تقدم (ف ٣/٢ و ١٢/٤ وما بعدها).

ومن ثم كان الشرط الذي لا يجوز اشتراطه في العقد يُعتبر مفسداً
ولما كانت هذه الشروط كثيرة أصبحت حوادث الفساد في العقود كثيرة أيضاً.
وفي أواخر العهد العثماني اتسعت في الدولة التجارة الخارجية مع أوروبة. وتطورت أيضاً أساليب التجارة الداخلية والصنائع. وتولدت في العصر الحديث أنواع من الحقوق لم تكن معهودة كامتياز المؤلف والمخترع وكل ذي أثر فني جديد في استثمار كتابه أو مخترعاته أو آثاره الفنية، مما بالملكية الأدبية والصناعية واحتاج أصحاب هذه الحقوق والامتيازات إلى بيعها والتنازل عنها لغيرهم من القادرين على استثمارها. وظهرت في التجارة الخارجية أيضاً حاجة قوية إلى عقود التأمين التي عرفت باسم

Chapter Fifty-Seven: The Position of Law on Voidability, and Our Opinion on the Subject 1/57 - Positive law did not know the theory of voidability that Islamic legal reasoning established, as it knew the theory of voidness, as we shall see.
And the Hanafi legal reasoning that produced the theory of voidability, and upon which the Ottoman judiciary relied, was among the juristic efforts that restricted the freedom of the will's authority regarding contractual conditions, although it is considered moderate compared to others, as previously mentioned (F 3/2 and 12/4 onwards).
For the contract.
Hence, a condition that is impermissible to stipulate in a contract is considered voidable.
And since these conditions are many, cases of voidability in contracts also became many.
In the late Ottoman era, foreign trade with Europe expanded in the state. Methods of internal trade and industries also developed. Modern times generated types of rights that were not previously known, such as the copyright of an author, the patent of an inventor, and every holder of a new artistic work in the exploitation of his book, inventions, or artistic works, which are part of literary and industrial property. The owners of these rights and privileges needed to sell and assign them to others capable of exploiting them. In foreign trade, there also emerged a strong need for insurance contracts, which became known as

Commentary
This passage is a historical and philosophical discussion of the Hanafi concept of fasād (voidability). The author notes that positive law (qānūn) did not develop a distinct theory of fasād the way Islamic jurisprudence did; instead, it mainly recognized voidness (buṭlān). The Hanafi school, which was the basis of Ottoman judiciary, introduced the theory of fasād. This theory was more restrictive of contractual freedom regarding conditions than some other schools, but it was moderate compared to others. Because many conditions were considered invalidating, instances of fasād became frequent. In the late Ottoman period, with increased foreign trade and the emergence of new rights (intellectual, industrial property, insurance), the need arose for legal adaptation. The passage sets the stage for discussing how the Hanafi theory of fasād was later reformed to accommodate modern transactions.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād): The Hanafi concept of voidability — a contract that is defective but not void, and may produce some legal effects until annulled.
البطلان (al-buṭlān): Voidness — a contract that is legally nonexistent from the start and produces no effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the Hanafi theory of fasād relative to other schools?
A) It was the most restrictive
B) It was the most lenient
C) It was considered moderate
D) It was identical to Shafi‘i

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi theory of fasād provides a flexible intermediate category that allowed Ottoman jurists to accommodate modern contracts, demonstrating the school's adaptability.

Hanafi Contract Law and the Emergence of the Penal Clause
Historical analysis of Hanafi fasād theory and emergence of penal clause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how economic modernization in the late Ottoman era led Hanafi jurists to consider the permissibility of the penal clause (al-sharṭ al-jazā’ī) as a response to the limitations of the theory of corruption (fasād).

Core Idea
Economic necessity drove Hanafi law to adapt: the theory of corruption gave way to the penal clause to protect against delay damages.

This passage explores the historical tension between the classical Hanafi theory of fasād—which renders many stipulated conditions corrupting—and the evolving needs of a modernizing economy. By understanding this evolution, you will see how Hanafi fiqh is not static but adapts pragmatically to address new forms of harm, such as loss from delay in performance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tension between fasād theory and modern needs
Recognize that the Hanafi theory of fasād, as applied in Ottoman courts, restricted the freedom to stipulate conditions in contracts. This created a problem when economic modernization required more flexible contractual tools.
The passage notes that Hanafi law was 'moderate relative to others' but still led to many instances of fasād.
2
Trace how fasād restricted stipulations
Understand that any condition not permissible in a contract was considered mufsid (corrupting), making the contract fāsid. This meant that many necessary commercial safeguards could not be enforced.
3
Recognize the economic changes demanding adaptation
Identify the specific developments: foreign trade, intellectual property, insurance (sūkira), istisnā‘ contracts, and supply contracts. These required timely performance and financial guarantees.
The value of time increased, making delay particularly harmful.
4
See why mere specific performance is insufficient
The passage argues that ordering specific performance does not compensate for losses caused by delay, such as idle factories or falling prices. Thus, a new remedy was needed.
5
Understand the emergence of the penal clause
People began stipulating financial penalties for delay, which became known as the penal clause (sharṭ jazā’ī). The passage shows that this was a pragmatic adaptation to fill a gap in the classical system.
The term is borrowed from foreign jurisprudence (Clause pénale).

الفصل السابع والخمسون
موقف القانون من الفساد، ورأينا في الموضوع ١/٥٧ - الفقه القانوني لم يعرف نظرية الفساد التي أسسها الاجتهاد الإسلامي مثلما عرف نظرية البطلان كما سنرى .
والاجتهاد الحنفي الذي جاء بنظرية الفساد وكان عليه قضاء الدولة العثمانية، قد كان من الاجتهادات التي ضيَّقت من حرية سلطان الإرادة في الشروط العقدية، وإن كان يعتبر متوسطاً بالنسبة إلى غيره كما تقدم (ف ٣/٢ و ١٢/٤ وما بعدها).
للعقد.
ومن ثم كان الشرط الذي لا يجوز اشتراطه في العقد يُعتبر مفسداً
ولما كانت هذه الشروط كثيرة أصبحت حوادث الفساد في العقود كثيرة أيضاً.
وفي أواخر العهد العثماني اتسعت في الدولة التجارة الخارجية مع أوروبة. وتطورت أيضاً أساليب التجارة الداخلية والصنائع. وتولدت في العصر الحديث أنواع من الحقوق لم تكن معهودة كامتياز المؤلف والمخترع وكل ذي أثر فني جديد في استثمار كتابه أو مخترعاته أو آثاره الفنية، مما بالملكية الأدبية والصناعية واحتاج أصحاب هذه الحقوق والامتيازات إلى بيعها والتنازل عنها لغيرهم من القادرين على استثمارها. وظهرت في التجارة الخارجية أيضاً حاجة قوية إلى عقود التأمين التي عرفت باسم السوكرة (۱) لضمان الأخطار على البضائع المشحونة، ولاسيما في النقل البحري. واتسع مجال عقود الاستصناع في التعامل بطريق الإيصاء على المصنوعات مع المعامل والمصانع الأجنبية وكذا عقود التعهد بتقديم اللوازم والأرزاق والمواد الأولية إلى الدوائر الحكومية والشركات والمعامل والمدارس، مما عقود التوريد وكل ذلك يعتمد على المشارطات في شتى صورها.
وقد ازدادت أيضاً قيمة الزمن في الحركة الاقتصادية. فأصبح تأخر أحد المتعاقدين أو امتناعه عن تنفيذ التزاماته في مواعيدها المشروطة مضراً بالطرف الآخر في وقته وماله أكثر مما قبل.
فلو أنَّ متعهداً بتقديم المواد الصناعية إلى صاحب معمل تأخر عن تسليمها إليه في الموعد المضروب لتعطّل المعمل وعماله. ولو أن بائع بضاعة لتاجر تأخر في تسليمها حتى هبط سعرها لتضرّر التاجر المشتري بخسارة قد تكون فادحة وكذا تأخر الصانع عن القيام بعمله في وقته، وكل متعاقد إذا تأخر أو امتنع عن تنفيذ عقده في موعده.
ولا يعوّض هذا الضرر القضاء على الملتزم بتنفيذ التزامه الأصلي، لأنَّ هذا القضاء إنما يضمن أصل الحق لصاحبه وليس فيه جبر الضرر التعطل أو الخسارة، ذلك الضرر الذي يلحقه من جراء تأخر خصمه عن وفاء الالتزام في حينه تهاوناً منه أو امتناعاً .
وهذا قد ضاعف احتياج الناس إلى أن يشترطوا في عقودهم ضمانات مالية على الطرف الذي يتأخر عن تنفيذ التزامه في حينه.
ومثل هذا الشرط يسمى في اصطلاح الفقه الأجنبي: الشرط الجزائي
Clause pénale
(۱) انظر ما تقدم ف (٢٤/٤٦ .
Chapter Fifty-Seven: The position of law on corruption, and our opinion on the subject 1/57 — legal jurisprudence did not know the theory of corruption that Islamic ijtihad established, just as it knew the theory of nullity as we shall see. And the Hanafi ijtihad that brought the theory of corruption and upon which the Ottoman state’s judiciary was based was among the ijtihad that restricted the freedom of will’s authority in contractual conditions, although it is considered moderate relative to others as previously mentioned (F 3/2 and 12/4 and after). For the contract. Hence the condition that is not permissible to stipulate in the contract is considered corrupting. And since these conditions were many, incidents of corruption in contracts also became many. In the late Ottoman era, foreign trade with Europe expanded in the state. Methods of internal trade and industries also evolved. In the modern era, types of rights emerged that were not customary, such as the copyright of the author and inventor and every owner of a new artistic work in exploiting his book, inventions, or artistic works, which are literary and industrial property, and the owners of these rights and concessions needed to sell them and relinquish them to others capable of exploiting them. Also in foreign trade, a strong need appeared for insurance contracts known as “secur” (1) to guarantee risks on shipped goods, especially in maritime transport. The field of istisna’ contracts expanded through ordering manufactured goods with foreign factories and workshops, as well as contracts of undertaking to supplies, provisions, and raw materials to government departments, companies, factories, and schools, which are supply contracts, all relying on stipulations in various forms. The value of time in economic activity also increased. Delay by one of the contracting parties or his refusal to fulfill his obligations at their stipulated times became more harmful to the other party in his time and wealth than before. If a supplier of industrial materials to a factory owner delayed delivering them at the appointed time, the factory and its workers would be idle. Similarly, if a seller of goods to a merchant delayed delivery until the price fell, the merchant buyer would suffer loss that might be enormous, as would a craftsman delaying his work at its time, and every contracting party if he delays or refuses to execute his contract on time. The judiciary does not compensate this harm by ordering the obligor to fulfill his original obligation, because this order only guarantees the original right to its owner and does not compensate the damage of idleness or loss, that damage inflicted by the delay of his opponent in fulfilling the obligation on time due to negligence or refusal. This doubled people’s need to stipulate in their contracts financial guarantees against the party who delays fulfilling his obligation on time. Such a condition is called in the terminology of foreign jurisprudence: the penal clause (Clause pénale). (1) See above, F 24/46.
Source pages p. 715

Commentary
This passage is a historical and philosophical discussion of how Hanafi fiqh’s theory of corruption (fasād) in contracts, which was applied in Ottoman courts, restricted the freedom to stipulate conditions. The author notes that while Hanafi law was moderate relative to other schools, it still led to many instances of contract corruption. With economic modernization—expansion of foreign trade, new intellectual property rights, insurance, istisna’, and supply contracts—the need for timely performance grew. The passage highlights the inadequacy of mere specific performance as a remedy for delay, since it does not compensate for lost time and opportunity. This created a demand for a penal clause (shart jiza’i) to be stipulated in contracts, a concept borrowed from foreign jurisprudence (Clause pénale). The author is describing the historical pressure that led to juristic adaptation, showing that Islamic law is not static but responds to evolving societal needs.

Key Terms
نظرية الفساد (nazariyyat al-fasād): The Hanafi theory that certain prohibited conditions in a contract render it defective (fāsid) rather than void (bāṭil), allowing possible correction.
الشرط الجزائي (al-sharṭ al-jazā’ī): A contractual condition stipulating a financial penalty for delay or non-performance, intended to guarantee timely execution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Hanafi jurists in the late Ottoman era need to consider the penal clause (الشرط الجزائي)?
A) Because the theory of corruption was too lenient
B) Because mere specific performance did not compensate for damages from delay
C) Because foreign laws forced them to adopt it
D) Because it was originally part of classical Hanafi fiqh

Key Takeaway
Hanafi contract law evolved in response to economic modernization, allowing the penal clause as a necessary remedy for delay damages.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Penal Clauses and the Hanafi Theory of Voidness: From Remedy to Barrier
Economic need for penal clauses and Hanafi theory of voidness as a barrier and a remedy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi theory of voidness (fasād) in relation to contractual stipulations, identify its original purpose, and compare it with the broader approaches of other schools, applying the principle of maslaha in modern fatwa.

Core Idea
The Hanafi theory of voidness was a flexible remedy, not a barrier—but later narrowed due to partisanship.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' explores the evolution of contractual conditions in Islamic law. It highlights how economic changes increased the need for penal clauses to compensate for delays, yet the Hanafi school's traditional rules on voidness (fasād) became a hindrance. The author traces the original intention of the Hanafi theory—to save contracts from total nullity—and contrasts it with the broader freedom allowed by the Hanbali school and early jurists like Ibn Shubruma. This lesson will guide you through the historical transformation and its relevance for contemporary fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify Economic Changes
List the economic factors (e.g., increased value of time, expansion of istisna' and supply contracts) that created a need for penal clauses. Recognize that simple enforcement of the original obligation does not compensate for delay-related losses.
Penal clauses are financial guarantees against delay or non-performance.
2
State the Original Purpose of Hanafi Fasād
Explain that Hanafi ijtihad developed the theory of voidness to prevent contracts from falling into absolute nullity (bāṭil) by using the intermediate category of fāsid (voidable). This allowed reform rather than automatic invalidation.
Fasād was innovative: it distinguished degrees of violation.
3
Contrast with Opposing Schools
The Hanafi school remained restrictive in the range of prohibited conditions. Compare with the Hanbali school and the views of Ibn Shubruma and Qadi Shurayh, which granted wider freedom to stipulate based on the principle of autonomy of will (sulṭān al-irāda).
These schools prioritized the general permissibility of contracts.
4
Analyze the Transformation
Trace how the originally flexible theory became a problem: the Hanafi school's partisanship and narrow juristic thinking prevented adopting wider views from other schools. The Majalla did not incorporate the broader approaches, leaving the Hanafi rules in place despite economic needs.
What was meant as a remedy became a barrier.
5
Evaluate Implications for Fatwa
Assess the practical outcome: modern civil codes in many Muslim countries adopted the broader approach, allowing penal clauses and similar conditions. Consider how a mufti today should weigh the Hanafi position against maslaha (public interest) and other school opinions.
The author implies a need to transcend strict taqlīd.

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه

(السوكرة) (۱) لضمان الأخطار على البضائع المشحونة، ولاسيما في النقل البحري. واتسع مجال عقود الاستصناع في التعامل بطريق الإيصاء على المصنوعات مع المعامل والمصانع الأجنبية وكذا عقود التعهد بتقديم اللوازم والأرزاق والمواد الأولية إلى الدوائر الحكومية والشركات والمعامل والمدارس، مما عقود التوريد وكل ذلك يعتمد على المشارطات في شتى صورها.

الفصل (٥٧) موقف القانون من الفساد
وهكذا تضافرت الأسباب والدواعي وتمخضت العوامل الزمنية في تطور الحياة الاقتصادية العامة، فولدت حاجة ملحة إلى حرية في أساليب التعاقد أوسع مما كان في قواعد المذهب الحنفي الذي كان هو المذهب المعمول به في قضاء الدولة.
وكانت بعض قواعد نظريتي البطلان والفساد في هذا المذهب بحدودها القديمة وخاصة فساد العقد في كثير من الشروط العقدية حائلاً دون الاستجابة إلى هذه الحاجة .
لذلك ظهر الضيق في حرية التعاقد الفساد في الشروط وما بسبب تفرضه قواعده من قيود مع ازدياد حاجة التعامل التجاري والصناعي إلى سعة مجال الاشتراط في الحقوق والالتزامات التعاقدية .
٢/٥ - وقد كانت العصبية المذهبية وضيق التفكير الفقهي إذ ذاك حائلين دون التماس العلاج مما في صيدليات المذاهب الفقهية الأخرى ومستودعاتها من فقه خصيب ونظریات سمحة لا تضيق عن حاجة تشريعية إذا ضاق عنها المذهب الواحد، فلم تأخذ جمعية المجلة في الشروط العقدية بغير المذهب الحنفي من المذاهب التي وشعت في حرية الشروط العقدية توسعة لم تأت القوانين الحديثة بأكثر منها؛ كالمذهب الحنبلي، و مذهب ابن شُبْرمة والقاضي شُريح، مما تقدم بيانه في بحث سلطان الإرادة من نظرية العقود

Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Supports in Fiqh.

(Insurance) (1) to cover risks on shipped goods, especially in maritime transport. The scope of istisna contracts expanded through ordering manufactured goods with foreign factories and enterprises, as well as contracts to supply provisions, rations, and raw materials to government departments, companies, factories, and schools, constituting supply contracts, all of which rely on stipulations in their various forms.
It was called.
The value of time also increased in economic activity. So the delay of one contracting party or his refusal to fulfill his obligations at the stipulated times became more harmful to the other party in terms of his time and money than before.
For example, if a supplier of industrial materials to a factory owner delays delivery at the appointed time, the factory and its workers would be idled. And if a seller of goods to a merchant delays delivery until their price falls, the buyer merchant would suffer loss that might be severe, likewise the delay of a craftsman in performing his work on time, and any contracting party who delays or refuses to execute his contract on time.
The judiciary does not compensate this harm by ordering the obligor to fulfill his original obligation, because this order only secures the original right for its owner and does not include compensation for the harm of idleness or loss, that harm which befalls him due to the delay of his opponent in fulfilling the obligation on time, whether due to negligence or refusal.
This has multiplied people's need to stipulate in their contracts financial guarantees against the party who delays in fulfilling his obligation on time.
Such a condition is called in the terminology of foreign fiqh: the penal clause

Chapter (57) The Position of Law on Voidness
Thus the causes and motives converged and temporal factors in the development of general economic life crystallized, giving birth to an urgent need for freedom in methods of contracting wider than what was in the rules of the Hanafi school, which was the school applied in the state judiciary.
And some rules of the theories of nullity and voidness in this school, with their old limits, especially the voidness of a contract due to many contractual conditions, were an obstacle to responding to this need.
Therefore, constriction appeared in the freedom of contracting due to voidness in conditions and what its rules impose of restrictions, alongside the increasing need of commercial and industrial transactions for a wide field of stipulation in contractual rights and obligations.
2/5 - And narrow partisanship and limited juristic thinking at that time were obstacles to seeking remedy from the fertile fiqh and generous theories in the pharmacies and storehouses of other schools of fiqh, which are not too narrow for legislative need if a single school becomes too narrow. So the Society of the Majalla did not adopt, regarding contractual conditions, other than the Hanafi school from among the schools that widened the freedom of contractual conditions, a widening that modern laws have not surpassed; such as the Hanbali school, and the school of Ibn Shubruma and Qadi Shurayh, as was explained earlier in the discussion on the autonomy of will in the theory of contracts.

(1) Thus the theory of voidness turned into a problem after it had been a remedy. For the Hanafi ijtihad founded the theory of voidness to erect a barrier against the contract falling into the abyss of nullity whenever it violated its prescribed system, as in some schools. So the Hanafi ijtihad distinguished between types and degrees of violations, and restricted nullifying violations to a narrow scope. This was the original idea from the genius of Hanafi ijtihad in juristic logic. Among its good features was that it considered a stipulation as corrupting (mufsid) to an originally valid contract, not void (mubtil). But the circle of these prohibited conditions

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' discusses the evolution of contractual conditions in Islamic law, particularly in the Hanafi school, in response to modern economic needs. The author opens by describing the economic changes that increased the value of time and made delays harmful. He explains that merely ordering specific performance does not compensate for losses caused by delay, leading to the need for 'penal clauses' — stipulations for financial compensation in case of delay. He then notes that the Hanafi school's traditional rules on voidness (fasād) were narrow and hindered the freedom needed for modern commerce. The author argues that the Hanafi theory of voidness was originally a flexible tool to avoid outright nullity, but with time it became restrictive due to partisanship. He suggests that other schools, like the Hanbali, and early scholars like Ibn Shubruma and Qadi Shurayh, offered wider freedom in stipulations, which modern laws have adopted. The author emphasizes the irony: the theory of voidness, meant as a remedy, became a problem. This discussion is part of the broader theme of 'supporting guarantees' (mu'ayyidāt shar'iyya) and the need to adapt fiqh to changing circumstances.

Key Terms
الشرط الجزائي (al-sharṭ al-jazā'ī): A contractual stipulation that imposes a financial penalty on a party who delays or fails to perform their obligation on time, intended to compensate the other party for harm caused by delay.
نظرية الفساد (naẓariyyat al-fasād): The Hanafi legal theory that distinguishes between void (bāṭil) and voidable (fāsid) contracts, allowing a fāsid contract to be rectified rather than automatically nullified.
سلطان الإرادة (sulṭān al-irāda): The principle of autonomy of will in contract law, meaning parties are free to stipulate conditions as they see fit, within the limits of Shariah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the original purpose of the Hanafi theory of voidness (fasād) in contracts?
A) To make all contracts void if they contain any condition.
B) To prevent contracts from falling into total nullity by allowing reform of defective contracts.
C) To follow the laws of modern European countries.
D) To restrict the use of penal clauses.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi theory of voidness, originally a flexible remedy, became a barrier due to partisanship, but the existence of broader views in other schools allows modern fatwa to adapt to economic needs.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Problem of Fasād and Ottoman Legal Reform
Analyzing the historical problem of fasād and Ottoman legal intervention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the historical problem of fasād in Hanafi contract law and evaluate the Ottoman legislative solution that combined Hanafi and Hanbali approaches.

Core Idea
The Hanafi theory of fasād saved contracts from nullity but created too many restrictions, forcing reform by borrowing Hanbali principles.

The Hanafi school’s intermediate category of fasād was a brilliant innovation to prevent contracts from becoming void due to minor defects. Yet over time, the wide scope of prohibited conditions became an obstacle to commercial and industrial growth. This lesson examines how the Ottoman state addressed this tension by introducing liberal principles into procedural law, effectively synthesizing the Hanafi distinction between nullity and corruption with the Hanbali emphasis on freedom of stipulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Recognize why the Hanafi theory of fasād, despite its advantages, created practical difficulties for economic transactions.
2
Understand Hanafi Fasād Theory
Analyze how the Hanafi ijtihad distinguished between bāṭil (null) and fāsid (corrupt) and the scope of prohibited conditions.
3
Compare with Hanbali Approach
Contrast the Hanbali position: a narrow circle of prohibited conditions but harsh nullity for any violation.
4
Analyze the Ottoman Solution
Study Article 64 of the Civil Procedure Law (1332 AH) and its three principles expanding contractual freedom.
5
Evaluate the Synthesis
Assess the wisdom of combining Hanafi’s distinction between nullity and corruption with Hanbali’s liberal view on stipulations.

وهكذا تضافرت الأسباب والدواعي وتمخضت العوامل الزمنية في تطور الحياة الاقتصادية العامة، فولدت حاجة ملحة إلى حرية في أساليب التعاقد أوسع مما كان في قواعد المذهب الحنفي الذي كان هو المذهب المعمول به في قضاء الدولة.

Chapter (57): The Position of Law on Fasād. Thus the causes and motives combined and the temporal factors in the development of general economic life produced an urgent need for greater freedom in methods of contracting than existed in the rules of the Hanafi school, which was the school applied in the state's judiciary. Some rules of the theories of nullity and fasād in this school, in their old limits, especially fasād of the contract due to many contractual conditions, were an obstacle to responding to this need. Therefore, constriction appeared in freedom of contracting due to fasād in conditions and the restrictions imposed by its rules, with the increasing need of commercial and industrial dealings for a wide scope of stipulations in contractual rights and obligations. 2/5 - And sectarian fanaticism and narrowness of juristic thinking at that time were obstacles to seeking remedy from what is in the pharmacies of other juristic schools and their repositories of fertile jurisprudence and generous theories that do not restrict a legislative need if one school restricts it. So the codification committee did not adopt in contractual conditions other than the Hanafi school from the schools that broadened freedom of contractual conditions in a way that modern laws have not exceeded; like the Hanbali school and the school of Ibn Shubruma and Qadi Shurayh, as mentioned previously in the discussion of the authority of will in the theory of contracts. (1) Thus the theory of fasād turned into a problem after it had been a remedy. Indeed, the Hanafi ijtihad founded the theory of fasād to establish a barrier preventing the contract from falling into the abyss of nullity whenever it violated its prescribed system, as in some schools. The Hanafi ijtihad distinguished between types and degrees of violations and restricted nullifying violations to a narrow scope, so it was the original idea from the genius of Hanafi ijtihad in juristic logic. It was good that it considered a stipulation as making the contract corrupt (mufsid) not void (mubtil), but the circle of prohibited conditions in Hanafi ijtihad is still wide, and freedom of stipulation is narrow, although it is moderate in that among other schools as previously explained in the discussion of the authority of will. Therefore, the Ottoman state felt an urgent need to enact a new legal text expanding freedom of contracting, contractual conditions, and the validity of the subject matter of the contract, as long as the Majalla - which is like the general Islamic civil code - did not solve this problem because it did not adopt from outside Hanafi ijtihad. 3/57 - However, the Ottoman government did not wish to confront the Majalla al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya with direct amendment, so it resorted to the Law of Civil Procedure which has no connection to this subject. It replaced one of its articles dealing with some trial procedures (article 64) with a new article inserted in its place by a law issued on 15 Jumada al-Akhira 1332 AH, which established the following three legal principles: 1 - Everything customarily traded of assets, usufructs, and rights is considered a valid subject matter for contracting, like every valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) and also things that will come into existence. 2 - Every agreement or stipulation is considered binding on the contracting parties unless it violates public morals, public order, a specific legal text, the provisions of land laws, endowment laws, or personal status laws such as inheritance, succession, capacity of contracting parties, and family rights. These are six excepted areas whose rulings are considered mandatory and may not be agreed contrary to. 3 - If the contracting parties agree on the original points - i.e., the essential aspects - And it is observed that the Hanbali ijtihad severely tightened the theory of nullity, considering every violation of imperative texts in the prescribed system of contracting and every prohibited condition as a cause for nullity of the contract. But it narrowed the circle of prohibited conditions, thus opening a wide field for freedom of stipulation. As for the Hanafi ijtihad, it narrowed the scope of the theory of nullity by establishing the theory of fasād. But it made the circle of prohibited conditions wide, resulting in an embarrassing constriction in freedom of contracting. Each of the two ijtihads has a positive aspect. If one were to take from Hanafi ijtihad its theory of distinguishing between nullity and fasād, and from Hanbali its theory of the authority of will and contractual conditions, the result would be a wiser and more spacious civil system in contracts.
Source pages p. 717

Commentary
This passage is a historical and comparative analysis of the Hanafi theory of fasād (contractual corruption) and its practical impact on Ottoman law. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that the Hanafi school, though it innovated the intermediate category of fasād to avoid outright nullity (bāṭil), nonetheless maintained a wide scope of prohibited stipulations, which constrained commercial freedom. As economic life evolved, this became a problem. The Ottoman state, rather than amend the Majalla directly, inserted a new article into the Civil Procedure Law (art. 64) laying down three liberal principles: (1) all customarily traded items, including future assets, are valid subjects; (2) agreements are binding unless they violate public order, specific laws, endowments, or personal status; (3) essential terms govern. Zarqa contrasts the Hanbali approach (narrow prohibited conditions but harsh nullity) with the Hanafi (wide prohibited conditions but only corruption). He suggests that combining Hanafi’s distinction between bāṭil and fāsid with Hanbali’s liberal approach to stipulations would yield an ideal civil system.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād): A category of contractual defect in Hanafi law where the contract is defective in a non-essential attribute (e.g., a prohibited condition) but can be rectified; it is an intermediate status between ṣiḥḥa (validity) and buṭlān (nullity).
البطلان (al-buṭlān): Nullity; the contract is void ab initio and cannot be rectified.
سلطان الإرادة (sulṭān al-irāda): The principle that the will of the contracting parties is the primary source of contractual obligations, limited only by public order, morals, and mandatory legal provisions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The Civil Procedure Law was the only law that could be amended.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi theory of fasād, while ingenious, created practical constraints that required a legislative synthesis with Hanbali principles to achieve contractual freedom.

The First Principle: Expanding Valuation via Custom
Legal principle expansion from Sharia to custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the first legal principle that expands the basis of valuation (taqawwum) from Sharia permissibility to custom ('urf) and identify its practical consequences for contracts.

Core Idea
Custom, not Sharia permissibility, now determines what counts as valuable property in modern law.

In classical Hanafi fiqh, property is valuable (mutqawwim) only if its benefit is permissible in Sharia. Modern legislation has introduced a first principle: the custom of people ('urf) becomes the basis for valuation. This expands the range of objects that can be the subject of valid contracts, including wine and intangible rights. In this lesson, we examine the principle, its scope, and its exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Classical Hanafi Condition
Recall that in Hanafi fiqh, property is mutaqawwim only if the benefit from it is permissible (halal) in Sharia. This excludes wine, pork, etc. for Muslims.
2
State the New Principle
The first principle replaces Sharia permissibility with 'urf al-nas (custom of people) as the basis for valuation. Whatever people customarily consider valuable is treated as such.
This is a modification (ta'dil) of the classical rule.
3
List Examples from the Passage
The text provides examples: wine, abstract rights (water rights, air rights), debts, and modern intangibles like industrial property, literary property, and business names.
These are now considered valid subject matters for contracts.
4
Note the Exception
Narcotics like hashish and opium remain non-valuable because they are prohibited by specific legal texts (nass) and thus excluded even under custom.
The second principle (not covered here) handles items prohibited by law.

الفصل (٥٧) موقف القانون من الفساد يعتبر العقد ،تاماً، ولو لم تذكر النقاط الفرعية ثم عند الاختلاف في تلك النواحي الفرعية تعينها المحكمة بحسب ماهية القضية. ٤/٥٧ - ١ - تحليل المبدأ الأول: فالمبدأ الأول من هذه المبادىء قد جاء تعديلاً لبعض أسباب البطلان فيما يتعلق بمحل العقد فإنه جاء بتوسيع قابلية المحل للتقويم، وهذه القابلية هي من شرائط الانعقاد العامة، فجعل في ذلك عرف الناس هو الأساس في التقوم، بينما كان أساسه في نظر فقهائنا هو حل الانتفاع شرعاً؛ فالخمر كانت تعتبر من الأموال غير المتقومة في حق المسلمين، فلا ينعقد بيعها وشراؤها، ولا يضمن متلفها ، فأصبحت في حكم هذه المادة مالاً متقوّماً ومحلاً صالحاً للتبايع وسائر العقود (٢) . (1) أما في حق غير المسلمين فتعتبر الخمر مالاً متقوّماً شرعاً، فيجوز تعاقدهم عليها. فيضمن المسلم ما أتلفه من الخمر لغير المسلم، لأن الإسلام أوجب معاملتهم بما [تنعقد] يدانين. وهذا من مزيد سماحة الشريعة الإسلامية (ر ) رد المحتار ١٠٤/٤). (۲) بخلاف الحشيش والأفيون ونحوهما من المواد المخدّرة التي يمنعها القانون بنص خاص، فإنها بقيت غير متقومة بالنظرين الشرعي والقانوني وفقاً للمبدأ الثاني. فلا بيوعها، بل تجب مصادرتها ويعتبر اقتناؤها جريمة يعاقب القانون عليها . فنظرية عدم التقوم في المال موجودة أيضاً في النظر القانوني، لكن مبناها فيه هو المنع القانوني من تداول المال لا التحريم الشرعي. وسنرى تفصيل ذلك في فصل الأموال من الجزء الثالث الذي أسميناه المدخل إلى نظرية الالتزام العامة». Chapter (57) The Law's Stance on Corruption. A contract is considered complete even if the subsidiary points are not mentioned, then when there is disagreement about those subsidiary aspects, the court determines them according to the nature of the case. 4/57 - 1 - Analysis of the First Principle: The first of these principles has come as a modification of some causes of nullity relating to the subject matter of the contract, for it has expanded the eligibility of the subject matter for valuation, and this eligibility is among the general conditions of formation. So it made in that custom of people the basis for valuation, whereas its basis in the view of our jurists was the permissibility of benefit by Sharia. For wine was considered from non-valuable property with respect to Muslims, so its sale and purchase are not valid, and one who destroys it does not guarantee it. Then it became, by virtue of this article, valuable property and a valid subject for sale and other contracts (2). (1), (2) Likewise, abstract rights such as the right to water for lands, the right to ascend and ride in building floors, and debts in the debts, and privileges in some modern legal rights such as industrial property rights, literary property rights of authors in printing and publishing, and privileges for issuing periodical newspapers and magazines, and the names of these newspapers and the names of well-known commercial shops that people today desire to buy their names from their owners to benefit from their fame. (1) As for with respect to non-Muslims, wine is considered legally valuable property, so their contracting upon it is permissible. So a Muslim who destroys wine belonging to a non-Muslim guarantees it, because Islam obligated dealing with them by what [they profess?]. And this is from the further tolerance of Islamic Law (see Radd al-Muhtar 4/104). (2) Unlike hashish, opium, and similar narcotic substances that the law prohibits by specific text, for they remain non-valuable in both the Sharia and legal views according to the second principle. So their sales do not validly occur; rather they must be confiscated and their possession is considered a crime punishable by law. So the theory of non-valuation in property also exists in legal view, but its basis in it is the legal prohibition of circulation of property, not Sharia prohibition. And we will see its detail in the chapter on property in the third part which we have named 'Introduction to the General Theory of Obligation'. (See also the book 'Property and Contract Theory' by Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra, p/4). So all that has become, by virtue of this legal article, eligible for sale and other contracts. And it is noted that most of the results branching from this principle are easy to derive from the rules of Islamic jurisprudence, except contracting on wine and similar Sharia prohibitions.

Commentary
This passage begins by introducing the law's stance on corruption in contracts, stating that a contract is considered valid even if subsidiary details are omitted; the court will determine them in case of dispute. Then it analyzes the first principle, which modifies the classical Hanafi conditions for the subject matter of a contract. In Hanafi fiqh, the subject matter must be valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), which requires that the benefit from it is permissible in Sharia (e.g., wine is not permissible for Muslims). The modern law, however, shifts the basis from Sharia permissibility to custom ('urf al-nas): whatever people customarily consider valuable is treated as such. This allows wine to become a valid subject matter for contracts, and also extends to abstract rights like water rights, easements, debts, and modern intangible assets such as industrial property and literary rights. The footnote clarifies that for non-Muslims, wine already was valuable in Sharia, so this expansion mainly affects Muslims. It also distinguishes narcotics like hashish and opium, which remain non-valuable due to legal prohibition, thus following both Sharia and law. The principle concludes by noting that most of its results align easily with Islamic fiqh, except for wine and similar explicitly prohibited items. This is a significant departure from classical Hanafi rules, but the author acknowledges it as a modification by modern legislation.

Key Terms
التقوم (at-taqawwum): The legal quality of being considered valuable property (مال متقوم) that can be the subject of contracts and compensation.
عرف الناس (urf al-nas): Customary practice of people, used as the basis for determining whether something is considered valuable in modern law.
المال المتقوم (al-mal al-mutaqawwim): Property that is legally considered valuable and thus can be sold, bought, or guaranteed if damaged.

Key Takeaway
The first principle substitutes custom for Sharia permissibility as the criterion for valuation, expanding the scope of valid contract subject matters but excluding items prohibited by specific law.

The Second Principle: Validating Conditional Contracts and Negative Obligations
Expansion of contractual freedom by modifying nullity and corruption rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the second principle of contractual modification and apply it to identify which conditional contracts and negative obligations are now valid under modern law.

Core Idea
The contract is the law of the parties; conditions are valid unless they violate public order.

This passage introduces the second principle, which significantly expands contractual freedom beyond classical Hanafi limits. It overcomes the strict Hanafi rules that voided conditional contracts and negative obligations, and abolishes the category of 'corrupting conditions' in commutative contracts. The guiding maxim is that the contract is the law of the contracting parties, with only six exceptions related to public order and morals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching principle
Recognize the legal maxim 'al-'aqdu shari'at al-muta'aqidayn' (the contract is the law of the contracting parties) which is the foundation for expanding contractual freedom.
This maxim is stated explicitly at the beginning of the passage.
2
Analyze the modification of nullity causes
Note that classical Hanafi fiqh voided contracts suspended on a condition (ta'liq) and negative obligations (e.g., promises not to build). The second principle now validates these by agreement.
Examples include sale, lease, and agreements not to compete.
3
Analyze the modification of corruption causes
Understand that the principle abolishes the 'corrupting condition' (shart mufsid) which previously invalidated commutative contracts. Now all conditions are valid unless they fall under six exceptions.
The six exceptions are public order, public morals, and others mentioned generally.
4
Apply the principle to a scenario
Given a contractual condition (e.g., 'I sell you this car if you pass your exam'), determine its validity under the second principle.
Check if it violates any of the six exceptions; if not, it is valid.

Likewise, contracting on negative obligations such as not building higher than a certain limit in front of a neighbor, not operating one's factory, not trading in a specific commodity by a trader to reduce industrial or commercial competition. Commentary
This passage analyzes the second principle, which modifies the Hanafi rules on nullity and corruption in contracts. It begins with the overarching legal maxim that 'the contract is the law of the parties', emphasizing freedom of contract. The principle first addresses nullity: in Hanafi fiqh, contracts that are suspended on a condition (like future uncertain events) are void. The modern law now allows such conditional contracts by agreement. Also, negative obligations (promises not to do something) which were previously void in Hanafi ijtihad are now valid and binding. The second part deals with corruption: the principle abolishes the concept of a 'corrupting condition' (shart mufsid) that used to vitiate commutative contracts. Instead, all conditions are valid unless they contravene six exceptions: public order, public morals, etc. Finally, it states that accordingly, the law now permits parties to agree on various terms. The passage marks a significant shift from classical Hanafi limitations on contractual freedom.

Key Terms
التعليق على الشرط (at-ta'liq 'ala ash-shart): Suspending the effectiveness of a contract on a future uncertain condition, e.g., 'If you come, then I sell you this.'
الالتزامات السلبية (al-iltizamat as-salbiyyah): Negative obligations: promises not to perform a certain act, such as not building or not competing.
الشرط المفسد (ash-shart al-mufsid): A condition that corrupts a commutative contract under Hanafi law, e.g., a condition that benefits one party without reciprocal obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A corrupting condition (shart mufsid) in a commutative contract.
C) A negative obligation not to compete.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
The second principle expands contractual freedom by allowing conditional contracts and negative obligations, and abolishing the corrupting condition, based on the maxim that the contract is the law of the parties.

Second Principle: Contractual Freedom Overrides Hanafi Nullity Rules
Explaining a legal principle: contractual freedom overrides Hanafi nullity rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a stipulation contrary to the default legal effect of a contract (muqtada al-'aqd) is valid under the second principle and state the six exceptions that limit contractual freedom.

Core Idea
The contract is the law of the parties—stipulations are valid unless they violate public order, morals, or other specific prohibitions.

This passage presents the second principle of contractual freedom, which significantly modifies traditional Hanafi rules on nullity and voidability. Where the classical Hanafi school considered conditions suspending a sale, negative obligations, or penalty clauses as void or voiding, this principle—rooted in the maxim 'al-'aqd shari'at al-muta'aqidayn'—makes them valid and binding, subject only to six exceptions. You will understand how this shift expands contractual autonomy while maintaining essential Islamic limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Traditional Hanafi Rule
In classical Hanafi fiqh, any stipulation that contradicts the default legal effect of a contract (muqtada al-'aqd) renders the contract void (fasid) in commutative contracts. This includes conditions that suspend ownership transfer or impose negative obligations.
The passage refers to this as 'shart mufsid'.
2
Understand the Second Principle's Modification
Under the second principle, such stipulations are no longer automatically void. Instead, they are valid and binding unless they fall under one of six exceptions (public order, public morals, etc.). This restores the parties' freedom to tailor their contract.
The principle is derived from the authority of the will (sultan al-irada) and broader Islamic legal maxims.
3
Recognize the Abolished Causes
The principle explicitly abolishes 'al-shart al-mufsid' (corrupting condition) as a cause of voidability. Causes of nullity, such as suspension on a condition, are also made permissible.
Examples from the passage: conditional sales, negative obligations (like not raising a building), and penalty clauses.
4
Apply to Penalty Clauses
The principle validates penalty clauses (al-shart al-jaza'i) as enforceable, citing the precedent of Qadi Shurayh. This is a departure from earlier Hanafi reluctance.
Penalty clauses are now common in modern contracts as liquidated damages.
5
Note the Boundary: Six Exceptions
The principle is not absolute. It excludes contracts on prohibited items (like wine) and any stipulation violating public order, public morals, etc. These six exceptions preserve the inviolable limits of Islamic law.
The passage only names two explicitly; the other four are derived from general legal theory.

As a result, they apply between the parties unless they agree in the contract otherwise (see: folio 7/39, 8/42, and 3/47). So if the seller and buyer agree that ownership of the sold item does not transfer to the buyer until full settlement of the deferred or installment price, or that the seller is not liable if the sold item perishes in his possession before delivery, then it is according to their agreement—whereas such stipulation was voiding of the contract in the view of Hanafi ijtihad, because the basis therein is that the rulings of contracts are mandatory, so stipulating contrary to the contract requirement voids it except where its violation is permitted. Commentary
This passage discusses the second principle of the 'Theory of Legal Supports' in fiqh contracts. The author explains that modern legal developments have modified the traditional Hanafi positions on causes of nullity (batalan) and voidability (fasad) in contracts. The key idea is that the contract is the law of the contracting parties, subject only to limited exceptions. Previously, Hanafi law considered certain conditions—like suspending a sale on a future event or imposing negative obligations—as void. Now, such stipulations are valid and binding. Similarly, the 'corrupting condition' (shart mufsid) that once vitiated commutative contracts is abolished; all conditions are valid unless they violate public order, public morals, or other specific prohibitions. This principle also validates penalty clauses for breach of contract, citing the precedent of Qadi Shurayh. The author notes that while Hanafi rules were initially restrictive, other Islamic schools (madhahib) allow greater contractual freedom, and thus the principle can be accommodated within Islamic fiqh by adopting broader views.

Key Terms
سلطان الإرادة (sultan al-irada): The authority of the will in creating and extinguishing rights; the principle that contractual parties may freely determine their obligations.
الشرط المفسد (al-shart al-mufsid): A condition that renders a commutative contract void (fasid) because it contradicts the contract's essential requirements.
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-'aqd): The default legal effects that the law assigns to a contract, which the parties may deviate from by agreement.

Key Takeaway
The second principle modifies Hanafi causes of nullity and voidability, validating stipulations that contradict muqtada al-'aqd unless they violate one of six exceptions (public order, public morals, etc.).

Default Contractual Provisions Are Non-Mandatory
Analyzes the second principle that legal provisions for contracts are default rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that default contractual provisions are non-mandatory to evaluate the validity of stipulations in a contract and distinguish between the Hanafi position and broader Islamic legal thought.

Core Idea
Default contract rules are non-mandatory; parties may agree otherwise unless a mandatory text prohibits deviation.

In this lesson, we explore the second principle of Islamic contract law: the statutory provisions that govern named contracts are not inherently binding. Rather, they serve as default rules that the contracting parties can modify by mutual agreement. This principle challenges the strict Hanafi view that any stipulation contrary to the contract's inherent requirement (muqtada al-'aqd) invalidates the contract. Understanding this principle opens the door to greater contractual flexibility within the broader framework of Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its scope
State the principle as given: legislative provisions for contracts are non-mandatory (غير إلزامية). Determine what type of provisions it covers – the default rules that the law provides for named contracts.
The principle applies unless a mandatory legal text (نص قانوني آمر) prohibits deviation.
2
Understand the default rule mechanism
These default rules are organized by legislation and ijtihad for the convenience of parties. They apply when parties do not agree otherwise. If parties stipulate contrary terms, those terms prevail, provided no mandatory text bars them.
The result (نتيجة) is that default rules are established between parties as long as they don't agree to the contrary.
3
Analyze the Hanafi counter-position
The Hanafi school holds that stipulations contrary to muqtada al-'aqd (the inherent requirement of the contract) invalidate the contract, unless an explicit exception permits it. This is because they consider contractual provisions to be mandatory (إلزامية).
Contrast this with the second principle which treats them as non-mandatory.
4
Examine the examples
Apply the principle to two examples: (1) agreement that ownership does not transfer until full payment, or exemption of seller from liability before delivery – valid under the second principle but invalid in Hanafi. (2) Penal clause (الشرط الجزائي) for delay – validated by the precedent of Judge Shuraih.
These examples show the practical difference between the two approaches.
5
Connect to broader legal maxims
Relate this principle to the autonomy of will (سلطان الإرادة). Recognize that the Hanafi school is restrictive, but other Islamic schools allow greater freedom of stipulation, providing a wider framework within Islamic jurisprudence.
This principle aligns with the broader maxim that contracts are governed by the intention of the parties.

Chapter (57) The position of law regarding corruption in named contracts, contrary to what legislation and jurisprudence have decided regarding provisions in each contract (1) unless a mandatory legal text prohibits its violation. So the principle regarding those legislative provisions for contracts is that they are non-mandatory; rather, legislation and ijtihad organize them in advance for the contracting parties to rely upon and to dispense with detailing them in the contract. The result is that they are established between the contracting parties as long as they do not agree in the contract to the contrary (see: sections 7/39, 8/42, 3/47). Performance: If the buyer and seller agree that ownership of the sold item does not transfer to the buyer until all deferred or installment price is paid, or that the seller is not liable if the sold item perishes in his possession before delivery, then that is according to what they agreed. Such a stipulation was considered invalidating of the contract in the view of Hanafi ijtihad, because the principle is that contractual provisions are mandatory, so stipulating a condition contrary to the contract's requirement invalidates it except where its permissibility is explicitly stated. Also included in the scope of this principle is the undertaking of financial guarantee as a penalty for default or delay in fulfilling an obligation, which is called in modern legal terminology: the penal clause (clause penale) as previously stated in section 1/57. This penal clause is based on the school of Judge Shuraih, for something similar was narrated from him. As previously explained (see: section 18/42). This second principle with all its consequences is at the very core of the principle of the autonomy of will. Commentary
This passage presents the second principle, which establishes that the statutory default rules for contracts are not mandatory; parties can agree to deviate from them unless a mandatory legal text explicitly prevents such deviation. The author contrasts this with the strict Hanafi position, which generally considers stipulations contrary to the contract's requirement (muqtada al-'aqd) as invalidating. Examples include agreements on delayed transfer of ownership or exemption of the seller from liability before delivery. The principle also validates penalty clauses (shart jaza'i), citing Judge Shuraih's precedent. This principle is an expression of the autonomy of will. Where the Hanafi school is restrictive, other Islamic schools allow greater freedom, providing a broader framework within Islamic jurisprudence.

Key Terms
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-'aqd): The inherent legal requirements and effects that a contract produces by default under the law.
الشرط الجزائي (al-shart al-jaza'i): A penal clause stipulating a financial penalty for default or delay in performance.
سلطان الإرادة (sultan al-iradah): The autonomy of will; the principle that parties are free to shape their contractual obligations within legal bounds.

Key Takeaway
Default contractual provisions are non-mandatory; parties may stipulate otherwise unless a mandatory legal text prohibits deviation.

The Third Principle: Ignorance of Subsidiary Matters Does Not Invalidate
Explains the third principle with examples and comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the third principle to determine when ignorance (jahalah) in a contract does not invalidate it, and distinguish valid from void scenarios.

Core Idea
Ignorance of subsidiary terms does not invalidate a contract if the essential aspects are known or determinable.

This lesson introduces a key principle in Hanafi legal philosophy that relaxes the strict rule against ignorance (jahalah) in contracts. While classical Hanafi fiqh held that any uncertainty in a material term could invalidate a contract, the third principle (codified in Majalla Article 64) recognizes that if the parties agree on the basic aspects or a method to determine them, subsidiary ignorance is tolerable. This reflects a pragmatic balance between legal certainty and commercial necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify basic vs. subsidiary aspects
First, distinguish the essential elements of the contract (price, subject matter) from secondary details (exact payment date, precise quality).
Basic aspects must be known or determinable; subsidiary aspects may be left uncertain.
2
Check if basic aspects are known or agreed upon a scale
If the parties agree on the subject matter and a method to fix the price (e.g., market price), then the basic aspects are considered known.
Examples: sale at market price, murabahah with reference to a capital list.
3
Apply the principle to validate or void
If basic aspects are known and agreed, ignorance in subsidiary aspects does not invalidate the contract. If basic aspects are unknown or no method agreed, the contract is void.
The same article 64 that validates also voids when basics are unknown.
4
Consider the court's role in subsidiary disputes
If parties dispute subsidiary terms, the court determines them using experts, based on the agreed basic framework.
This gives practical enforceability to contracts with flexible terms.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

C - Analysis of the third principle: As for the third principle, it came as an amendment to another cause of corruption, which is ignorance (jahalah). It does not consider ignorance harmful in the formation and validity of a contract if it is in a subsidiary aspect of it, and the basic aspects are known and agreed upon from the time of contracting, or the two contracting parties agree on a suitable basis for determining them later. An example of that is if two persons make a sale and agree that the price of the sold item is its market price, or the price that will settle for it that day, or he buys it as murabahah with an increase of ten percent on his capital according to the statement of capital that will reach the seller. So the basic aspects in all these examples are agreed upon: the sold item is known and the price is agreed upon a scale for its determination, so there is no harm in not knowing the amount of the price at the time of contracting. This ignorance was considered invalidating of the contract in Hanafi ijtihad, and voiding in some other ijtihads. But this third principle in the article /64/ under discussion has sufficed for the validity of the contract by the knowledge of the basic aspects if the two parties agree on them. It has made the determination of the subsidiary aspects, in case of dispute between the contracting parties, to the court, so they are determined with the knowledge of experts, taking from those agreed-upon bases as a scale for determining the disputed subsidiary aspects. This principle is also strong in foundation and is acceptable in the rules of Hanbali ijtihad, whose jurists approved the sale based on the prevailing price, as previously explained in the theory of contracts (see: section 16/42). As for if some of the basic aspects in the contract are unknown, not agreed upon, nor on a suitable basis for determining them, then according to this principle they remain a corrupting factor in the contract according to the legal ruling in Hanafi ijtihad. That is as if the sold item is unknown, or they make a sale or partnership on the condition that they agree on the price or profit share later; this is a void contract according to the same article /64/ because it did not validate...
Source pages p. 722

Commentary
This passage explains the third principle, which addresses ignorance (jahalah) as a cause of contract nullification. It holds that ignorance is not harmful if it relates only to subsidiary aspects while the essential elements are known and agreed upon, or if the parties have a mechanism to determine them later. Examples include sales with a market price or murabahah with a reference to a capital list. The principle overrides the stricter Hanafi view that previously considered such ignorance invalidating. Hanbali school is cited as accepting this approach. However, if the basic aspects themselves are unknown (e.g., unknown subject matter or agreement to decide price later), the contract remains void. The principle thus balances flexibility with legal certainty.

Key Terms
الجهالة (al-jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty about a material term in a contract, potentially causing invalidity.
النواحي الأساسية (al-nawahi al-asasiyyah): Essential aspects of a contract such as the subject matter and price criteria.
النواحي الفرعية (al-nawahi al-far'iyyah): Subsidiary or secondary aspects that do not affect the contract's core validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the third principle, which of the following contracts would be considered valid?
A) Sale of an unspecified car with price to be fixed later by the seller alone.
B) Partnership where profit shares are determined by a fixed ratio but capital is left completely undefined.
C) Sale of a known commodity with price set as 'the market rate at delivery'.
D) Murabahah where the cost price is unknown and no reference is provided.

Key Takeaway
Ignorance in subsidiary matters does not invalidate a contract as long as the essential aspects are known or a method for their determination is agreed upon.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

8 min
The Third Principle: Subsidiary Ignorance Does Not Invalidate Contracts
Explaining the third principle of subsidiary ignorance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that subsidiary ignorance does not invalidate a contract, distinguishing between essential and subsidiary ignorance, and identify when a contract remains valid despite uncertainty in peripheral terms.

Core Idea
Subsidiary ignorance is tolerated; only fundamental ignorance voids a contract.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi contract law: ignorance of peripheral details does not necessarily invalidate a contract. The great Hanafi jurist Mustafa al-Zarqa explains how this principle was codified in Ottoman law and later influenced modern civil codes. You will learn to distinguish between essential and subsidiary ignorance and see how courts resolve disputes over subsidiary terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ignorance
Distinguish between fundamental ignorance (جهالة أساسية) – lack of knowledge about core elements like subject matter or price basis – and subsidiary ignorance (جهالة فرعية) – uncertainty about peripheral details like exact price when a valid basis is agreed.
The Hanafi school treats these differently; only fundamental ignorance invalidates.
2
State the principle
Understand that subsidiary ignorance does not harm contract formation or validity if the essential aspects are known and agreed upon, or if the parties agree on a valid basis for determining them later.
This principle is embodied in Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Procedure.
3
Work through the examples
Analyze the examples given: market price, future price, and murabaha with a capital statement. In each, the essential aspects (subject matter and price measure) are agreed, so the contract is valid despite unknown exact price.
These examples demonstrate a practical application where the principle facilitates commerce.
4
Apply the exception
Recognize when ignorance is fundamental: if an essential aspect (e.g., subject matter, profit ratio) is left to future agreement with no basis, the contract remains void. This preserves the original Hanafi ruling against uncertainty.
Footnote: Many mistakenly think Article 64 abolished all invalidity; it only abolished subsidiary ignorance and condition-based invalidity.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Shar'i Supports in Fiqh
7/57 - C - Analysis of the Third Principle:
As for the third principle, it came as an amendment to another cause of invalidity, namely ignorance (jahala). For it did not consider ignorance harmful in the formation and validity of a contract if it is in a subsidiary aspect of it, while the essential aspects are known and agreed upon from the time of contracting, or the two contracting parties agreed on a valid basis for determining them later.
.
Example of that: If two persons buy and sell and agree that the price of the sold item is its market price, or the price that will settle for it on that day, or he buys it as a murabaha with a ten percent increase on its capital according to the capital statement that will reach the seller. So the essential aspects in all these examples are agreed upon, for the sold item is known and the price is agreed upon a measure for its determination, so there is no harm in not knowing the amount of the price at the time of contracting. Such ignorance was considered invalidating for the contract in Hanafi ijtihad, and voiding in some other ijtihads, but this third principle in Article 64 under discussion has sufficed for correcting the contract with the knowledge of the essential aspects if the two parties agree upon them. And it has made the determination of subsidiary aspects, in case of disagreement between the contracting parties, to the court, so they are determined by the knowledge of experts, taking from those agreed bases a measure for determining the disputed subsidiary aspects.
This principle is also strong in foundation and is accepted in the rules of Hanbali ijtihad, whose jurists approved the sale on the basis of what the price settles at, as previously explained in the theory of contracts (see: F 16/42).
As for if some of the essential aspects of the contract are unknown, not agreed upon, nor upon a valid basis for determining them, then according to this principle they remain invalidating for the contract according to the shar'i ruling in Hanafi ijtihad. That is as if the sold item is unknown, or they buy and sell or form a partnership on the condition that they agree on the price or on the profit share later, then this contract is void according to Article 64 itself, because it does not correct contracts that include ignorance except what is subsidiary ignorance, and what is agreed upon in essential aspects or upon a valid basis for determining them later (1).
8/57 - The Stance of the New Syrian Civil Code:
Indeed, our Syrian Civil Code, which was issued while we were printing this book, was taken by its drafters from the new Egyptian Civil Code, which was also implemented in Egypt this year (1949 CE), and it is derived from a set of foreign civil codes. We will soon see that foreign jurisprudence is void of a theory of invalidity corresponding to our fiqh theory.
Hence our civil code, and likewise its Egyptian origin, came devoid of any trace of the theory of invalidity, except that it came supporting the three principles that Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Judicial Procedure previously mentioned brought us. (a) Articles 83 and 132 of this new civil code affirmed the first principle concerning the valuation of assets and their ability to be contracted.
For Article 83 stipulated: 'Everything not excluded from trade by its nature or by law is valid to be the subject of financial rights.'
And Article 132 stipulated the validity of the subject of obligation being a future thing, so it included the validity of selling something nonexistent that will come into existence and what is analogous, like fruits before their seasons, even embryos in their mothers' wombs.
(b) Articles 148 and 149 thereof included the second principle concerning freedom of conditions.
(1) Most of our jurists today think that Article 64 of the mentioned Code of Procedure has completely abolished the theory of invalidity. This is a mistake, for it only abolished invalidity due to conditions absolutely except what violates public order, and also abolished invalidity due to subsidiary ignorance. So fundamental ignorance remains according to the original fiqh ruling as invalidating for contracts.
Source pages p. 722

Commentary
This passage presents the third principle of the theory of shar'i supports in fiqh, which addresses ignorance (jahala) as a cause of contract invalidity. The principle holds that subsidiary ignorance—i.e., lack of knowledge about peripheral details—does not harm the formation or validity of a contract if the essential aspects are known and agreed upon from the start, or if the parties agree on a workable basis to determine them later. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that this principle modifies the earlier strict Hanafi stance that considered any ignorance harmful. He provides examples: agreeing to a market price, a future determined price, or a murabaha sale based on a forthcoming capital statement. In these cases, the core terms (subject matter and price basis) are agreed, so ignorance of the exact price is not fatal. The principle also empowers courts to resolve disputes over subsidiary details by referring to expert opinion based on the agreed foundations. Al-Zarqa notes that this principle is also accepted in Hanbali fiqh, which permits sales contingent on a future price settlement. However, if an essential aspect (like the subject matter itself or the profit ratio) is left to future agreement, the contract remains void according to Hanafi jurisprudence, as per Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Procedure. The passage then discusses the new Syrian Civil Code, which—though not adopting the full theory of invalidity—implicitly supports the three principles of Article 64. The code acknowledges the validity of future objects (Article 132) and freedom of conditions (Articles 148-149), thereby aligning with the principles that subsidiary ignorance does not invalidate contracts. A key clarification is made in the footnote: many jurists mistakenly think Article 64 completely abolished the theory of invalidity; in fact, it only abolished invalidity due to conditions (except those contrary to public order) and due to subsidiary ignorance. Fundamental ignorance remains a cause of invalidity.

Key Terms
الجهالة (al-jahāla): Ignorance; lack of knowledge regarding a contractual term or object. Here distinguished into subsidiary (فرعية) and fundamental (أساسية).
النواحي الأساسية (al-nawāḥī al-asāsiyya): Essential aspects of a contract that must be known and agreed upon for validity, such as the subject matter and the basis for determining the price.
النواحي الفرعية (al-nawāḥī al-farʿiyya): Subsidiary or peripheral details of a contract, ignorance of which does not invalidate if the essential aspects are agreed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third principle described in the passage, which type of ignorance does NOT invalidate a contract in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Ignorance of the subject matter of the contract
B) Ignorance of a subsidiary detail when the essential aspects are agreed
C) Ignorance of the price when no basis for determination is agreed
D) Ignorance of the profit ratio in a partnership

Key Takeaway
Subsidiary ignorance is harmless; only fundamental ignorance voids a contract in Hanafi fiqh.

The Three Principles of Contract Validity in Modern Civil Codes
Comparative analysis of three principles from Ottoman and modern codes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three principles from Article 64 of the Ottoman Procedure Code and explain how they are reflected in the Syrian and Egyptian civil codes.

Core Idea
Modern codes affirm three core contract principles but omit the nuanced Islamic theory of invalidity.

In this lesson, we examine how modern civil codes, though derived from foreign models, have adopted three fundamental principles of contract validity that parallel Islamic fiqh. These principles concern the subject matter of contracts, freedom of conditions, and the sufficiency of agreement on essential terms. The author shows that while the codes lack a explicit theory of fasād, they implicitly support these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three principles from Article 64
First, locate the three principles as described: (1) the subject matter must be valuable and legally tradeable, (2) freedom of contract subject to public order, (3) agreement on essential terms completes the contract, with courts deciding subsidiary details.
These principles are the foundation of contract law in both Islamic and modern systems.
2
Map each principle to the modern code articles
For each principle, find the corresponding articles in the Syrian civil code: Articles 83 and 132 for the first, Articles 148 and 149 for the second, and Article 96 for the third.
The author cites these articles verbatim in Arabic.
3
Analyze the scope of adoption
Note that the codes adopt the principles but do not incorporate the full Islamic theory of invalidity (fasād). The author emphasizes that foreign jurisprudence lacks such a theory.
This is a key point for understanding the comparative gap.
4
Evaluate the common misconception
Recognize that many jurists mistakenly believe Article 64 abolished the entire theory of invalidity. The author clarifies it only abolished invalidity due to conditions or subsidiary uncertainty, leaving fundamental uncertainty as a cause of invalidity.
This correction is vital for accurate legal analysis.
