Chapter 57: The Position of Law Regarding Invalidity. Contracts that include uncertainty except where the uncertainty is subsidiary and the fundamental aspects are agreed upon or there is a valid basis for determining them later. 8/57 - The position of the new Syrian civil code: Indeed, our Syrian civil code, which was issued while we were printing this book, was taken by its authors from the new Egyptian civil code that was also implemented in Egypt this year (1949 CE), and it is derived from a group of foreign civil codes. We will soon see that foreign jurisprudence is devoid of a theory of invalidity corresponding to our jurisprudential theory. Consequently, our civil code, and likewise its Egyptian original, came devoid of any trace of the theory of invalidity, except that it came supporting the three principles that were brought to us by Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Legal Procedure previously mentioned. A – Articles 83 and 132 of this new civil code affirmed the first principle concerning the valuation of assets and their capacity for contracting. Article 83 stipulated: 'Everything not excluded from dealings by its nature or by law may be a subject of financial rights.' Article 132 stipulated the permissibility of the subject of an obligation being a future thing, so it included the validity of selling a non-existent thing that will come into existence and what is in its meaning, such as fruits before their seasons, even embryos in their mothers' wombs. B – Articles 148 and 149 of it included the second principle concerning freedom of conditions. (1) Indeed, the majority of jurists among us today think that Article 64 of the aforementioned Code of Procedure has completely abolished the theory of invalidity. This is a mistake; rather it abolished invalidity by condition absolutely except what violates public order, and also abolished invalidity due to subsidiary uncertainty. Thus, fundamental uncertainty remains according to the original juristic ruling rendering contracts invalid. Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Endorsements in Jurisprudence. Article 148 stated the principle: 'The contract is the law of the contracting parties.' Article 149 stated: 'The contract must be performed according to what it contains in a manner consistent with what good faith requires.' The civil code also excepted matters relating to inheritance rules, public order, and public morals from agreements contrary to them. See Articles 137 and 146. And it organized – in its last section – the real estate provisions that cannot be agreed otherwise, and left the provisions of Islamic Sharia for the rest of personal status matters such as marriage, divorce, and others except what it contained of some rulings regarding capacity of persons, their naming, domicile, and so on; these it undertook to organize in Articles 31-53. C – Article 96 of it included the third principle, stating: 'Agreement on all essential matters is sufficient for the completion of the contract. Then if a dispute arises between the parties on subsidiary detailed matters, the court decides on them according to the nature of the transaction, provisions of the law, custom, and rules of justice.' Thus, all three legal principles contained in Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Procedure have been fulfilled by the civil code, which is the natural place for these texts. (1) 9/57 – Our Opinion on the Theory of Invalidity: The sum of the statement is that the theory of invalidity in Islamic jurisprudence is solid in structure, and it is inherently among its valuable useful merits that it uniquely possessed in the system of legislative endorsements, making it a beneficial station on the path to nullity. (1) The appropriate place for it in the past was indeed the Majallah, which was the Islamic civil code; for these three principles are an amendment to the provisions of the Majallah, but the Ottoman government inserted them surreptitiously into the Code of Procedure, as we have pointed out.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal analysis explaining the position of modern civil codes – specifically the Syrian and Egyptian codes – towards the Islamic juristic theory of invalidity (فساد). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a Hanafi scholar, first outlines that these codes imported from foreign models lack a dedicated theory of invalidity. However, they implicitly affirm three key principles from the Ottoman procedural law (Article 64) which align with Islamic fiqh: (1) the subject matter of contracts must be valuable and legally tradeable (Articles 83 and 132), (2) freedom of contract with exceptions for public order and morals (Articles 148 and 149), and (3) agreement on essential terms suffices for contract formation, with courts supplying subsidiary details (Article 96). The author corrects a common misconception that Article 64 abolished the entire theory of invalidity; rather, it only eliminated invalidity due to conditions or subsidiary uncertainty, leaving fundamental uncertainty as a cause of invalidity according to fiqh. In the final section, the author gives his personal opinion: the Islamic theory of invalidity is structurally sound and a unique advantage of Islamic jurisprudence, providing a beneficial intermediate stage before nullity. He notes that the Majallah (Ottoman civil code) was the natural home for these principles, but the Ottoman government improperly inserted them into the Procedure Code.

Key Terms
فساد (fasad): Invalidity, a defect in a contract that prevents it from being fully effective but may be curable, as distinct from absolute nullity (بطلان).
جهالة أساسية (jahala asasiyya): Fundamental uncertainty about a core element of the contract (e.g., subject matter or price), rendering it invalid (فاسد).
جهالة فرعية (jahala far'iyya): Subsidiary uncertainty about non-essential details, which does not vitiate a contract under Hanafi law and modern codes.
نظام عام (nizam 'amm): Public order, a set of fundamental principles and interests that cannot be contravened by contractual agreements.
المادة ٦٤ (al-mādda 64): Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Legal Procedure, which introduced three principles of contract validity but was often misinterpreted as abolishing the entire theory of invalidity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It fully adopts the theory and incorporates all its details.
B) It completely rejects the theory and contains no trace of it.
C) It does not explicitly include the theory but implicitly supports its three core principles.
D) It replaces the theory with a stricter nullity framework.

Key Takeaway
Modern civil codes adopt three key principles of contract validity from Islamic law but lack the nuanced theory of fasād, which provides a beneficial intermediate stage between validity and nullity.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Uniqueness of the Islamic Theory of Invalidity (فساد)
Author's opinion on the unique value of the Islamic theory of invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the author's argument that the Islamic theory of invalidity (fasād) is a unique and valuable feature of Hanafi jurisprudence, and evaluate its absence in modern codes.

Core Idea
Islamic fiqh uniquely offers an intermediate 'invalid' stage, a beneficial station before nullity.

In this lesson, we explore the author's personal opinion on the theory of invalidity. He argues that the fasād theory is a solid structure and a distinct advantage of Islamic jurisprudence. Unlike modern civil codes that only recognize valid or void contracts, Islamic law provides a middle ground where contracts can be rectified. This lesson also examines why the Ottoman Majallah was the natural home for these principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's claim of uniqueness
The author states that the theory of invalidity in Islamic fiqh is 'متينة المبنى' (solid in structure) and a 'مزاياه القيمة المفيدة' (valuable useful merit) that makes it unique compared to other legal systems.
This is the core thesis of the author.
2
Analyze the 'beneficial station' metaphor
The author describes fasād as a 'محطة نافعة' (beneficial station) on the path to nullity. This means that instead of immediately voiding a contract, Islamic law allows for correction, preserving the contract's purpose where possible.
This contrasts with the binary valid/void approach of modern codes.
3
Connect to the Majallah
The author argues that the Majallah (the Ottoman civil code based on Hanafi fiqh) was the appropriate place for these principles. The Ottoman government mistakenly inserted them into the Procedure Code rather than the civil code.
This historical note shows the author's preference for codifying fiqh principles in their proper context.
4
Evaluate the practical implication
In practice, the absence of the fasād theory means courts in modern civil systems lack a mechanism to treat contracts as temporarily invalid and allow correction, leading to harsher outcomes.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf of the dispute.

Chapter 57: The Position of Law Regarding Invalidity. Contracts that include uncertainty except where the uncertainty is subsidiary and the fundamental aspects are agreed upon or there is a valid basis for determining them later. 8/57 - The position of the new Syrian civil code: Indeed, our Syrian civil code, which was issued while we were printing this book, was taken by its authors from the new Egyptian civil code that was also implemented in Egypt this year (1949 CE), and it is derived from a group of foreign civil codes. We will soon see that foreign jurisprudence is devoid of a theory of invalidity corresponding to our jurisprudential theory. Consequently, our civil code, and likewise its Egyptian original, came devoid of any trace of the theory of invalidity, except that it came supporting the three principles that were brought to us by Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Legal Procedure previously mentioned. A – Articles 83 and 132 of this new civil code affirmed the first principle concerning the valuation of assets and their capacity for contracting. Article 83 stipulated: 'Everything not excluded from dealings by its nature or by law may be a subject of financial rights.' Article 132 stipulated the permissibility of the subject of an obligation being a future thing, so it included the validity of selling a non-existent thing that will come into existence and what is in its meaning, such as fruits before their seasons, even embryos in their mothers' wombs. B – Articles 148 and 149 of it included the second principle concerning freedom of conditions. (1) Indeed, the majority of jurists among us today think that Article 64 of the aforementioned Code of Procedure has completely abolished the theory of invalidity. This is a mistake; rather it abolished invalidity by condition absolutely except what violates public order, and also abolished invalidity due to subsidiary uncertainty. Thus, fundamental uncertainty remains according to the original juristic ruling rendering contracts invalid. Chapter Seven: The Theory of Legal Endorsements in Jurisprudence. Article 148 stated the principle: 'The contract is the law of the contracting parties.' Article 149 stated: 'The contract must be performed according to what it contains in a manner consistent with what good faith requires.' The civil code also excepted matters relating to inheritance rules, public order, and public morals from agreements contrary to them. See Articles 137 and 146. And it organized – in its last section – the real estate provisions that cannot be agreed otherwise, and left the provisions of Islamic Sharia for the rest of personal status matters such as marriage, divorce, and others except what it contained of some rulings regarding capacity of persons, their naming, domicile, and so on; these it undertook to organize in Articles 31-53. C – Article 96 of it included the third principle, stating: 'Agreement on all essential matters is sufficient for the completion of the contract. Then if a dispute arises between the parties on subsidiary detailed matters, the court decides on them according to the nature of the transaction, provisions of the law, custom, and rules of justice.' Thus, all three legal principles contained in Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Procedure have been fulfilled by the civil code, which is the natural place for these texts. (1) 9/57 – Our Opinion on the Theory of Invalidity: The sum of the statement is that the theory of invalidity in Islamic jurisprudence is solid in structure, and it is inherently among its valuable useful merits that it uniquely possessed in the system of legislative endorsements, making it a beneficial station on the path to nullity. (1) The appropriate place for it in the past was indeed the Majallah, which was the Islamic civil code; for these three principles are an amendment to the provisions of the Majallah, but the Ottoman government inserted them surreptitiously into the Code of Procedure, as we have pointed out.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal analysis explaining the position of modern civil codes – specifically the Syrian and Egyptian codes – towards the Islamic juristic theory of invalidity (فساد). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a Hanafi scholar, first outlines that these codes imported from foreign models lack a dedicated theory of invalidity. However, they implicitly affirm three key principles from the Ottoman procedural law (Article 64) which align with Islamic fiqh: (1) the subject matter of contracts must be valuable and legally tradeable (Articles 83 and 132), (2) freedom of contract with exceptions for public order and morals (Articles 148 and 149), and (3) agreement on essential terms suffices for contract formation, with courts supplying subsidiary details (Article 96). The author corrects a common misconception that Article 64 abolished the entire theory of invalidity; rather, it only eliminated invalidity due to conditions or subsidiary uncertainty, leaving fundamental uncertainty as a cause of invalidity according to fiqh. In the final section, the author gives his personal opinion: the Islamic theory of invalidity is structurally sound and a unique advantage of Islamic jurisprudence, providing a beneficial intermediate stage before nullity. He notes that the Majallah (Ottoman civil code) was the natural home for these principles, but the Ottoman government improperly inserted them into the Procedure Code.

Key Terms
محطة نافعة (mahatta nāfi'a): A beneficial station; the author's metaphor for the faasād stage as a useful intermediate point before nullity.
المجلة (al-Majalla): The Ottoman civil code (Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah) based on Hanafi fiqh, in effect from 1877 to 1926.
دساً (dassan): Sneakily inserted; the author's description of how the Ottoman government placed the principles into the Procedure Code.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what was the proper place for the three principles from Article 64?
A) The Ottoman Code of Legal Procedure
B) The Majallah (civil code)
C) The Syrian civil code
D) The Egyptian civil code

Key Takeaway
The Islamic theory of invalidity is a unique and valuable feature that provides a beneficial intermediate stage, distinguishing Hanafi contract law from modern civil codes.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Hanafi Theory of Fasād: A Defense Against Hardship
Defending the Hanafi theory of fasād and its legal benefits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi theory of fasād (invalidity) differs from butān (nullity) and evaluate the claim that fasād causes hardship in contractual stipulations.

Core Idea
Fasād is a middle ground, not a source of hardship; the real rigidity is in the Hanafi mandatory view of contract terms.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa defends the Hanafi theory of fasād against the criticism that it leads to legal hardship. He argues that the theory itself is a beneficial innovation, providing a 'useful station on the path to nullity' (محطة نافعة في طريق البطلان). The difficulties in practice arose not from fasād but from the school's strict view of contractual mandatory provisions. This lesson unpacks his defense and the practical benefits of distinguishing fasād from butān.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal categories
Distinguish between fasād (invalidity) and butān (nullity) in Hanafi fiqh. Fasād applies to defects in non-essential elements; butān to defects in essences.
The author emphasizes that fasād is a 'station on the path to nullity'.
2
Understand the criticism
Some claimed that the theory of fasād caused hardship because many contracts became invalid due to prohibited conditions.
This criticism is linked to the Hanafi view that most contractual provisions are mandatory.
3
Follow the author's rebuttal
Al-Zarqa argues that the hardship came from the narrow freedom of stipulations (i.e., the mandatory nature), not from the theory of fasād itself. Without fasād, those conditions would cause absolute nullity.
He uses analogies: cold air (theory) vs. accompanying cold (strict conditions).
4
Examine the ijtihadi nature
The theory of fasād is based on ijtihad and can be adjusted according to legislative need and public interest.
This flexibility allows for correction and partial effects.
5
Assess practical examples
In Ottoman family law, marriages with defects (e.g., to mahrams, without witnesses, underage) are fasid, not batil. If consummated, they establish lineage and 'iddah.
This shows the practical benefit of distinguishing degrees of invalidity.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Sanctions in Fiqh. Article 148 stated the principle that the contract is the law of the contracting parties. Article 149 stated that the contract must be executed in accordance with what it contains in a manner consistent with good faith. The civil code also excepted matters related to inheritance rules, public order, and public morals from agreements that violate them. See Articles 137 and 146. It regulated in its last section the property rulings that cannot be agreed otherwise, and left to Islamic Sharia rulings the rest of personal status matters of marriage, divorce, and others, except for some rulings that came concerning the capacity of persons, their naming, domicile, and so on; these it undertook to regulate in Articles 31-53. C) Article 96 included the third principle, stating that agreement on all essential matters is sufficient for the completion of the contract, and if a dispute arises between the parties on detailed subsidiary matters, the court decides them according to the nature of the transaction, the provisions of law, custom, and rules of justice. Thus, all three legal principles contained in Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Civil Procedure have been fulfilled by the civil code, which is the natural place for these texts. (1) 9/57 - We have seen in the theory of invalidity: The gist of the matter is that the theory of invalidity in Islamic jurisprudence is solid in structure and in itself is one of its valuable beneficial merits, unique in the system of legislative sanctions, making it a useful station on the path of nullity. (1) The appropriate place for it previously was the Majalla, which was the Sharia civil code, for these three principles are an amendment to the rulings of the Majalla, but the Ottoman government inserted them into the Code of Civil Procedure, as we indicated. 768 : Chapter (57) The Law's Stance on Invalidity As for the difficulty observed in its application before the existence of Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Civil Procedure, it did not arise from the theory of invalidity itself, but from the narrowness of freedom of contractual stipulations in the Hanafi school, which holds that the original ruling in most contractual provisions is mandatory, so stipulating contrary to them in the contract invalidates it. Moreover, were it not for the theory of invalidity, such a stipulation would nullify the contract, as in some other schools. The theory of invalidity was created to be relief, not hardship. But the theory of the prohibited invalidating condition carried its evils. People between them were like one who sits in the cold open air and gets sick; the sickness came from the accompanying cold, not from the open air, or like one who takes a lot of beneficial treatment and it harms him; the harm comes from the excess, not the type of treatment! We have shown that the theory of invalidity is based on ijtihad and is subject to modification by ijtihad in its causes and rulings according to legislative need and interest. We also saw how the application of the theory of invalidity to the system of litigation had the best effect in judicial principles (see: f 8/54), even foreign judiciary did not dispense with the idea of distinguishing between an incomplete flawed claim that is correctable and a void claim that is illegitimate from the start, so it is rejected outright and not accepted for correction; they applied the theory of invalidity in litigation practically, even if they did not know it by this name. The Ottoman Family Law Law in our country benefited from applying the theory of invalidity in marriage, according to the opinion of those who distinguish in marriage between invalid (fasid) and void (batil) in terms of effect, even if they are the same in non-conclusion. The aforementioned law considered marriage contrary to its legal and statutory conditions: invalid (fasid) - if by consummation it establishes lineage of the child and a waiting period for the woman despite its non-conclusion, such as marriage to mahrams in the view of Abu Hanifa, marriage without witnesses, and marriage below the age stipulated by law. 769
Source pages p. 724

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical discussion defending the Hanafi theory of fasād (invalidity) as distinct from butān (nullity) in Islamic law, particularly in light of the Ottoman civil code (Majalla) and modern legal principles. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by showing how the Ottoman civil code incorporated three key principles: freedom of contract (article 148), mandatory execution in good faith (article 149), and the court's power to settle subsidiary terms (article 96). He notes that these principles originally belonged in the Majalla but were inserted into the procedural law. He then turns to the theory of fasād, summarizing that it is a solid and beneficial feature of Islamic law, providing a middle station between complete validity and absolute nullity. The core of the passage addresses the criticism that the theory of fasād caused hardship. Al-Zarqa argues that the hardship came not from the theory itself, but from the Hanafi school's strict view that most contractual provisions are mandatory, so stipulating against them invalidates the contract. Without the theory of fasād, such stipulations would render the contract entirely void, as in some other schools. Thus, fasād actually provides relief by allowing the contract to be corrected or partially effective. He uses analogies: the cold air (theory) is not the cause of illness, but the accompanying cold (strict conditions); beneficial treatment in excess causes harm, not the treatment itself. He emphasizes that the theory of fasād is based on ijtihad and can be adjusted according to legislative need and public interest. He gives examples from litigation and family law: in Ottoman family law, marriages that violate conditions (like marriage to mahrams according to Abu Hanifa, or without witnesses, or underage) are considered fasid (invalid) rather than batil (void), so if consummated, they establish lineage and impose a waiting period. This shows the practical benefit of distinguishing between degrees of invalidity. The passage thus defends the Hanafi position as flexible and pragmatic, contrary to allegations of rigidity.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād): Invalidity that is not absolute nullity; a defective contract that can be corrected or produce partial effects.
البطلان (al-butān): Absolute nullity; a contract that is void from inception with no legal effects.
الشرط الممنوع المُفْسِد (al-shart al-mamnūʿ al-mufsid): A prohibited condition that renders the contract fasid (invalid) but not batil.
العقد شريعة المتعاقدين (al-ʿaqd sharīʿat al-mutaʿāqidayn): The contract is the law of the contracting parties; a principle of freedom of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the theory itself.
B) From the narrow freedom of contractual stipulations in the Hanafi school.
C) From the Majalla's provisions.
D) From the Ottoman Code of Civil Procedure.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi theory of fasād is a beneficial legal tool that mitigates hardship by allowing correction of defective contracts, contrary to the misconception that it causes rigidity.

The Legal Theory of Fasād vs. Bāṭil
Analyzing the Hanafi theory of fasād and its practical benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between fāsid and bāṭil contracts and evaluate its application in modern family law legislation.

Core Idea
The Hanafi theory of fasād preserves partial effects in deficient contracts, offering legislative flexibility unobtainable under blanket nullity.

This passage unpacks the Hanafi legal theory of fasād as a middle category between valid and void, rebutting criticisms that the theory creates hardship. By distinguishing contracts that are fundamentally void from those merely deficient, the theory allows limited legal effects—such as lineage and waiting period—where other schools would deny all consequences. The author argues that this ijtihādī tool should inform modern codification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criticism and its rebuttal
Find the complaint that the Hanafi theory of fasād caused difficulty. Note that the author attributes the difficulty not to the theory itself but to the narrow view on contractual stipulations.
The author uses analogies (cold air, beneficial treatment) to separate the theory from its misapplication.
2
Grasp the core distinction: fāsid vs. bāṭil
Understand that fāsid contracts are not validly concluded but can produce partial effects (e.g., lineage, iddah) under certain conditions, whereas bāṭil contracts are absolutely null with no effects. This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.
Other schools (e.g., Shāfiʿī) often treat all defects as buṭlān.
3
Analyze the practical example in marriage
Look at how the Ottoman Family Law applied this distinction: a marriage lacking conditions (e.g., without witnesses, to a mahram) is fāsid—if consummated, it establishes lineage and iddah. A marriage between a Muslim woman and a non-Muslim is bāṭil and produces no effects.
This solves the theoretical paradox of attributing effects to a void contract.
4
Evaluate the legislative benefit
Consider the author's conclusion: the theory allows flexibility and relief (faraj) by enabling correction and partial validity. It also aligns with the principle of freedom of contract and can be adapted via ijtihād.
The author urges modern codification to adopt this theory with modifications according to legislative need.

Chapter (57): The Position of Law Regarding Invalidity (Fasād).

As for the difficulty noticed in its application before the existence of Article 64 of the Ottoman Code of Civil Procedure, it did not arise from the theory of invalidity itself, but from the narrowness of freedom of contractual stipulations in the Hanafi school, which considers that most contractual provisions are mandatory, and stipulating contrary to them in the contract renders it invalid.

However, without the theory of invalidity, such a stipulation would have nullified the contract entirely, as in some other schools. The theory of invalidity was established to be a relief, not a hardship. But the theory of the prohibited invalidating stipulation carried its negative aspects. People were between them like one who sits in the cold open air and falls ill; the illness came from the accompanying cold, not from the open air, or like one who overuses a beneficial treatment and is harmed; the harm is from excess, not the type of treatment!!

We have explained that the theory of invalidity is ijtihādī (based on independent reasoning) and can be modified by ijtihād in its causes and rulings according to legislative need and interest. We also saw how the application of the theory of invalidity to the system of lawsuits had the best effect on judicial principles (see vol. 8, p. 54), so much so that foreign judiciary did not dispense with the idea of distinguishing between a deficient, defective lawsuit that can be corrected and a void lawsuit that is fundamentally illegitimate, so it is rejected outright and not subject to correction. They applied the theory of invalidity in lawsuits practically, even if they did not know it by this name. The Ottoman Family Law benefited from applying the theory of invalidity in marriage, according to the view that distinguishes in marriage between invalid (fāsid) and void (bāṭil) in terms of effect, although they are alike in non-conclusion. The said law considered marriage contrary to its legal and statutory conditions:
- Invalid (fāsid) – if it establishes, by consummation, lineage of the child and a waiting period for the woman despite its non-conclusion, such as marriage to mahram relatives in the opinion of Abū Ḥanīfa, marriage without witnesses, and marriage below the age stipulated by law.
- Void (bāṭil) – if it has no effect, such as pure zinā, with which no lineage is established, like the marriage of a Muslim woman to a non-Muslim.

See: Family Law, Statement of Reasons for Nullity and Invalidity of Marriage, and Articles 52-58 and 76 thereof.

This is a wise distinction that helps remove theoretical contradiction in establishing some effects for a contract described as void, and it is based on expanding the meaning of 'invalid' to include the non-concluded. The point of difference between it and 'void' is the establishment of some effects of a valid act or the absence of any. So it is worthy for modern codification to pay attention to the theory of invalidity with modification of its causes according to the principle of freedom of contractual will, and it can benefit greatly from it.
Source pages p. 725

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical analysis of the Hanafi theory of fasād (invalidity) and its distinction from buṭlān (nullity). The author begins by addressing a criticism: the difficulty in applying the theory was due to the Hanafi school's restrictive view on contractual stipulations, not the theory itself. Without this theory, stipulations contrary to default rules would render contracts completely void, as in other schools. Thus, fasād serves as a middle ground, preserving some effects.

The author uses analogies to illustrate that problems attributed to fasād actually stem from misuse, not the theory. He emphasizes that fasād is ijtihādī and can be adapted. He then highlights the practical benefit in procedural law: distinguishing between a deficient lawsuit (which can be corrected) and a void one (which is rejected outright) – a distinction also used in Western law implicitly.

Focusing on family law, the Ottoman Family Law applied this distinction in marriage. A marriage contrary to conditions can be fāsid (e.g., marriage to a mahram in Abū Ḥanīfa's view) – it is not concluded, but if consummated, it establishes lineage and a waiting period. In contrast, absolutely void marriages (e.g., Muslim woman to non-Muslim) have no effects even after consummation. This resolves the paradox of attributing some effects to a void contract.

The author concludes that modern legislation should adopt this theory, modifying its causes in line with freedom of contract.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād): A type of invalidity where a contract is not concluded but may produce some effects (like lineage and iddah) under specific conditions, distinguishing it from absolute nullity.
البطلان (al-buṭlān): Absolute nullity; a contract that is fundamentally illegitimate and produces no legal effects whatsoever.
نظرية الفساد (naẓariyyat al-fasād): The legal theory that recognizes a category of fāsid contracts that are not valid but can have partial effects, allowing flexibility in legislation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a fāsid marriage from a bāṭil marriage in Hanafi law?
A) Fāsid marriages require witnesses; bāṭil do not.
B) Fāsid marriages can produce lineage and iddah after consummation; bāṭil marriages have no effects.
C) Fāsid marriages are valid if conditions are later fulfilled; bāṭil are always invalid.
D) Bāṭil marriages are those without a guardian; fāsid are those without witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi theory of fasād is a sophisticated legal tool that distinguishes between absolute nullity and remediable invalidity, allowing partial effects in deficient contracts to prevent total hardship.

The Hanafi Bāṭil and Fāsid: A Comparative Analysis with French Nullity Theory
Comparative analysis of nullity theories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Hanafi classification of void (bāṭil) and defective (fāsid) contracts with the French law concepts of absolute and relative nullity, and apply the distinction to identify the legal effects of a defective contract.

Core Idea
The Hanafi fāsid is not void: it produces some effects, mirroring the French relative nullity.

In this lesson, we explore the sophisticated Hanafi distinction between a void contract (bāṭil) and a defective contract (fāsid). The author of our text argues that this distinction is not only theoretically rigorous but also finds a striking parallel in modern French law's division of nullity into absolute and relative. Understanding this comparison sharpens your ability to classify contracts accurately and appreciate the practical consequences of a defective agreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories in Islamic law
From the text, note how the author distinguishes between void (bāṭil) and defective (fāsid) contracts. The void contract has no legal effects; the defective one may have some.
The example given: marriage of a Muslim woman to a non-Muslim is void (bāṭil) and no lineage is established.
2
Map to French law's absolute and relative nullity
The author states that absolute nullity (Nullité absolue) corresponds exactly to bāṭil, while relative nullity corresponds to fāsid. Understand the basis: missing pillar/condition vs. defect in description.
3
Analyze the legal effects
For absolute nullity/bāṭil, the contract is legally non-existent. For relative nullity/fāsid, some effects may be recognized, such as the possibility of annulment upon petition.
The text hints at the wisdom of this distinction: it resolves contradictions in establishing some rulings for a defective contract.
4
Apply to a modern codification scenario
Consider how a legislator might use the Hanafi concept of fāsid to craft nuanced rules for defective contracts, allowing partial enforcement.
The author recommends modern codification benefit from the theory of fasād.

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal analysis. The author begins by concluding the discussion on the theory of legal confirmations (نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية) and then transitions to a comparison with foreign law. He first reiterates the distinction in Islamic fiqh between a void contract (باطل) and a defective contract (فاسد), emphasizing that a void contract has no legal effects (e.g., no lineage from illicit intercourse). البطلان النسبي (al-buṭlān al-nisbī): Relative nullity in foreign law, analogous to the Hanafi concept of fāsid (defective) — can be annulled upon petition.

Comparing Nullity Theories: Islamic Law and Foreign Law
Comparative legal analysis of nullity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute and relative nullity in foreign law, and compare them with the Hanafi concepts of bāṭil and fāsid.

Core Idea
Absolute nullity is identical in both systems; relative nullity parallels the Hanafi fāsid contract.

Islamic legal theory is often compared with other legal systems to clarify its unique contributions. In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, a leading Hanafi jurist, presents the French theory of nullity and shows how it aligns with or diverges from Islamic jurisprudence. The comparison reveals a striking agreement on absolute nullity but a nuanced difference in how defective contracts are treated. For the Hanafi mufti, understanding these parallels and distinctions is essential when advising on contracts in a modern context, especially when parties may be influenced by foreign legal concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of nullity
Read the passage to find the two categories: absolute nullity (al-buṭlān al-muṭlaq) and relative nullity (al-buṭlān al-nisbī). Note their causes and consequences as described.
Absolute nullity arises from missing an essential element; relative nullity from defects of consent.
2
Compare absolute nullity with Islamic law
The author states that foreign absolute nullity conceptually matches the Islamic bāṭil (void) contract. List the shared features: no legal effect, no ratification, no court annulment needed, any interested party can raise it, no statute of limitations.
This agreement stems from the common idea that the void is legally non-existent.
3
Analyze relative nullity and its Hanafi counterpart
Relative nullity applies to contracts with defects like fraud, error, duress, or incapacity. In Islamic law, such contracts are not absolutely void but fāsid (voidable). Note that the Hanafi school allows ratification (ijāza) for certain defects, which is absent in foreign relative nullity.
The Hanafi fāsid contract can be rectified by subsequent ratification, while foreign relative nullity leads to annulment without retroactive cure.
4
Apply the comparison to a mufti's scenario
Consider a contemporary case: a contract made under duress. According to foreign law, it is relatively void and can be annulled. According to Hanafi fiqh, it is fāsid and may be ratified if the duress ceases. A mufti must explain this difference to a questioner.
The practical difference affects whether the contract can be saved or must be terminated.

نظرية البطلان في الفقه الأجنبي
عرض ونقد ومقارنة بنظريتي البطلان والفساد
في الفقه الإسلامي
١/٥٨ - نرى من المفيد - بعد بحثنا هذا في نظريتي البطلان والفساد الفقه الإسلامي - أن نلم بعرض موجز لنظرية البطلان في الفقه الأجنبي نخلص منه إلى مقارنتها بما رأيناه من ذلك في فقهنا الإسلامي، ويكون هذا ختام البحث في نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية.

البطلان المطلق Nullite absolu .
والبطلان النسبي Nullite relative .
- فأما البطلان المطلق، فنظريته لديهم متفقة كل الاتفاق البطلان في الفقه الإسلامي بالفكرة والقواعد والأسباب والنتائج، حتى لتكاد تكون في أحكامها نسخة طبق الأصل عما في الفقه الإسلامي:
فالعقد الباطل بطلاناً مطلقاً عندهم أيضاً، وإن وُجد فعلاً، هو معدوم اعتباراً، أي غير منعقد وسبب هذا البطلان فقدان أحد مقومات العقد أو بعض شرائطها. ويترتب على ذلك :
- أنَّ العقد الباطل لا يفيد حكمه وآثاره ولا يقبل الإجازة، ولا يحتاج إلى إبطال من قبل الحاكم.
- وإذا تمسك به تلقاء أحد الطرفين وادعى بطلب تنفيذه فللقاضي من نفسه أن يقرر البطلان ويرد الدعوى، لأنّ العقد في الواقع معدوم فليس بحجة أمام القضاء وكذا لكل ذي مصلحة غير الطرفين المتعاقدين كالوارث، والدائن أن يتمسك بهذا البطلان.
- والدفع بالبطلان المطلق في وجه المدعي لا يسري عليه حكم التقادم إلى غير ذلك مما سلف بيانه في نظرية البطلان.

٣/٥٨ - أما البطلان النسبي في الفقه الأجنبي فهو أن ينعقد العقد على حالة من حالات شوائب الإرادة العقدية التي تسمى عيوب الرضا، كالتدليس والغلط والإكراه وكذا حالة نقص الأهلية، كما في عقد الصغير المميز، فيكون كل ذلك سبباً لاتصاف العقد عندهم بهذا البطلان النسبي.
فالعقد هنا في حالة البطلان النسبي منعقد صحيح وموجب الجميع وجائبه الالتزامية بين العاقدين في نظر التشريع، ولكنه قابل للإبطال بين العاقدين، ما لم يمس هذا الإبطال حقاً قد اكتسبه غيرهما في المعقود عليه.
Chapter Fifty-Eight: The Theory of Nullity in Foreign Law – Presentation, Critique, and Comparison with the Theories of Nullity and Invalidity in Islamic Jurisprudence
1/58 – We see it beneficial, after our research here on the theories of nullity and invalidity in Islamic jurisprudence, to present a brief overview of the theory of nullity in foreign law, concluding with a comparison with what we have seen of that in our Islamic jurisprudence. This will be the conclusion of the research on the theory of legal rectifications.
2/58 – Division of nullity into absolute and relative in foreign law: French legal scholars divide nullity of contracts into two types:
Absolute nullity (Nullite absolue) and Relative nullity (Nullite relative).
- As for absolute nullity, their theory completely agrees with the nullity in Islamic jurisprudence in concept, rules, causes, and consequences, to the extent that its provisions are almost an exact copy of what is in Islamic jurisprudence:
A contract that is absolutely void, even if it exists in reality, is considered legally non-existent, i.e., not concluded. The cause of this nullity is the absence of one of the essential elements of the contract or some of its conditions. This entails:
- That the void contract produces no legal effect and does not accept ratification, and does not require annulment by the judge.
- If one party insists on it and claims a request for its execution, the judge may on his own pronounce the nullity and dismiss the case, because the contract is in reality non-existent and thus not an argument before the judiciary. Likewise, any interested party other than the two contracting parties, such as the heir or creditor, can assert this nullity.
- The defense of absolute nullity against the plaintiff is not subject to the statute of limitations, and so on, as previously explained in the theory of nullity.
This complete agreement between the two Islamic and foreign jurisprudential theories in this regard arises, in our view, from the agreement on the idea that the void is legally non-existent in the eyes of the law. The legal consequences of non-existence thereafter become rational necessities that opinions differ on only within the limits of some subsidiary matters outside the essential consideration of the void contract, such as the limits of protecting the right of a third party acquired in good faith on the object after the void contract, like the right of a pledgee from a buyer who bought invalidly.
3/58 – As for relative nullity in foreign law, it is that the contract is concluded with some flaw in the contractual will called defects of consent, such as deceit, error, and coercion, as well as a state of incomplete capacity, as in the contract of a discerning minor. All of that causes the contract to be characterized by this relative nullity.
Here, in the case of relative nullity, the contract is concluded and valid, producing all its binding effects between the contracting parties in the eyes of the law, but it is subject to annulment between the two parties, unless this annulment infringes upon a right that a third party has acquired in the subject matter.
Source pages p. 727

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal analysis. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a renowned Hanafi jurist, presents the theory of nullity in foreign (French) law and compares it with Islamic law, specifically the Hanafi perspective. He divides nullity into absolute and relative. For absolute nullity, he notes a striking similarity: the contract is legally non-existent, cannot be ratified, does not require court annulment, and any interested party can raise it without statute of limitations. This agreement stems from the common foundational idea that the void is legally nonexistent. For relative nullity, the foreign law treats it as a valid contract affected by defects like fraud or incapacity, voidable only between parties unless third-party rights intervene. In Hanafi fiqh, such defects (e.g., coercion) render the contract voidable (fasid) but not absolutely void; the comparison highlights differences in how Islamic law handles these situations, particularly the role of annulment and the protection of third parties.

Key Terms
البطلان المطلق (al-buṭlān al-muṭlaq): العقد الذي لا ينعقد أصلاً لفقد ركن أو شرط، فيكون معدوماً اعتباراً
البطلان النسبي (al-buṭlān al-nisbī): في القانون الأجنبي: العقد منعقد صحيح لكنه معيب بعيوب الرضا أو نقص الأهلية، قابل للإبطال بين المتعاقدين
عيوب الرضا (ʿuyūb al-riḍā): شوائب في الإرادة العقدية كالتدليس والغلط والإكراه

A) Both allow the void contract to be ratified.
B) Both consider the absolutely void contract as legally non-existent.
C) Both require a court annulment for absolute nullity.
D) Both distinguish between void and voidable contracts.

Key Takeaway
Absolute nullity is identical in both systems; relative nullity in foreign law is comparable to the Hanafi fāsid contract, but Islamic law offers greater flexibility through ratification.

Critique of Relative Nullity in Foreign Law from a Hanafi Perspective
Comparative critique of foreign nullity classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique the foreign concept of relative nullity using the Hanafi distinction between void (bāṭil) and annullability.

Core Idea
Relative nullity is not nullity at all; it is mere annullability, a concept alien to Hanafi fiqh where a contract is either valid or void.

This passage compares Islamic and foreign legal theories of nullity. The author first affirms that absolute nullity (bāṭil) is the same in both systems: the contract is legally non-existent. He then explains the foreign concept of relative nullity, where a contract is valid until annulled by the affected party. Finally, he delivers a sharp critique: relative nullity is not true nullity but annullability, which has no equivalent in Hanafi fiqh. This lesson unpacks that critique and its implications for a mufti evaluating modern legal terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of nullity in foreign law
Read the author's description of absolute and relative nullity. Note the key characteristics: absolute nullity makes the contract void ab initio; relative nullity leaves it valid but annullable at the request of a party with defective consent.
Absolute nullity corresponds to Islamic bāṭil, but relative nullity has no Islamic parallel.
2
Compare the legal effects of the two nullities
From the passage, list the effects of each: absolute nullity - no need for judicial declaration, any interested party can assert it; relative nullity - contract is binding until annulled, only the affected party can seek annulment, and the contract can be ratified.
The difference lies in who can invoke nullity and whether the contract is considered existing before annulment.
3
Locate the author's critique
Find the paragraph beginning with 'نقد هذا التقسيم الأجنبي' (Critique of this foreign classification). Identify the author's central objection: that relative nullity contains no shadow of true nullity; it is merely annullability (qābiliyyat al-ibṭāl).
The author insists that annullability is distinct from nullity.
4
Contrast with Hanafi categories
Recall that Hanafi fiqh categorizes contracts as ṣaḥīḥ (valid), bāṭil (void), or fāsid (defective). A fāsid contract is also void of effect unless certain conditions are met, but it is not merely annullable. Compare fāsid with relative nullity: both require a judicial intervention?
In Hanafi, a fāsid contract is void ab initio in terms of transferring ownership, but if the defect is cured, it may become valid. Relative nullity presupposes initial validity.
5
Derive practical impact for a mufti
Consider a modern contract labeled 'voidable' or 'subject to annulment'. The mufti must apply Hanafi criteria: if the contract is inherently defective (e.g., gharar, riba), it is bāṭil or fāsid, not merely annullable. Relative nullity is a separate legal construct that does not affect the intrinsic validity in Islamic law.
A mufti should not equate 'annullability' with 'nullity' when giving fatwas.

Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Nullities in Jurisprudence
-
That the void contract does not produce its ruling and effects, and is not subject to ratification, and does not require annulment by the judge.
- And if one party clings to it and claims seeking its execution, the judge may himself declare the nullity and reject the claim, because the contract is in reality non-existent, so it is not a proof before the judiciary. Similarly, any interested person other than the two contracting parties, such as the heir and the creditor, may assert this nullity.
-
And the defense of absolute nullity against the plaintiff is not subject to prescription, etc., as previously explained in the theory of nullity.
And this complete agreement between the Islamic and foreign legal theories in this regard arises, in our view, from the agreement on the idea that the void is considered non-existent in the eyes of the law. So the legal consequences of non-existence thereafter become logical necessities that do not differ except within the limits of some subsidiary matters beyond the intrinsic consideration of the void contract, such as the limits of protecting a third party's right acquired in good faith in the object after the void contract, like the right of the pledgee from a buyer by a void purchase.
Ref: al-Mujaz fi al-Iltizamat by Professor al-Sanhuri, pp. 165-179. 3/58 -
As for relative nullity in foreign law, it is that the contract is concluded in a state of defects of contractual will called defects of consent, such as fraud, error, duress, and also the case of lack of capacity, as in the contract of a discerning minor. So all that is a reason for the contract to be characterized by this relative nullity according to them.
Thus, the contract here in the case of relative nullity is concluded, valid, and fully binding between the contracting parties in the eyes of the law, but it is subject to annulment between them, as long as this annulment does not affect a right that another has acquired in the object of the contract.
And the difference between the two nullities, absolute and relative, according to them is the same difference between absolute nullity and validity except for this susceptibility to annulment. So the contract in the case of relative nullity remains valid and effective until the judge rules its annulment upon the request of the person whose will was tainted by a defect of consent in that contract, i.e., upon the request of the one for whom the right of annulment is to protect his consent and interest, like the defrauded buyer. And the contract thereafter is subject to ratification, so it is finally concluded by its ratification, and his right to annul it falls, even if his ratification is implicit, as if he voluntarily executes the contract after knowledge of the cause of nullity.
As for the other party, he has no right to demand annulment because the contract is binding on him. Likewise, the judge, when a claim for execution of the contract is brought before him, may not declare annulment on his own without a request from the interested party, like every private right of a person that is not adjudicated without the request of its owner. Hence it is called relative nullity because it is presumed relative to one of the two contracting parties.
And that is different from the case of absolute nullity, where the contract is non-existent in legal reality, not to be relied upon. So if execution is sought, anyone harmed by the execution may assert the nullity, and the judge may declare it on his own, as previously mentioned.
·
Ref: al-Mujaz fi Nazariyyat al-Iltizamat by Professor al-Sanhuri, pp. 163/166, and Colin and Capitant, Droit civil français, vol. 2, pp. 259-260.
4/58 - Critique of this foreign classification:
It is clear from that that relative nullity in foreign law does not contain any meaning of nullity, neither much nor little; rather, there is not even a shadow of the reality of nullity in it. All it contains is mere annullability granted by law to one of the parties to protect his private right.
And it is obvious that mere annullability is one thing, and nullity is another. So
Source pages p. 728

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal analysis of the concept of nullity in foreign (non-Islamic) law, specifically the distinction between absolute nullity (bāṭil) and relative nullity (qābil li al-ibṭāl). The author first reaffirms the Islamic position: a void contract (bāṭil) is legally non-existent, produces no effects, cannot be ratified, and does not require a judicial annulment; any interested party may assert its nullity, and the judge may declare it ex officio. He notes that Islamic and foreign law agree on absolute nullity as the legal non-existence of the contract.

Then he explains the foreign concept of relative nullity: it arises from defects of consent (fraud, error, duress) or lack of capacity. In this case, the contract is considered valid and binding until one party (the one whose will was defective) seeks annulment. The other party cannot demand annulment, and the judge cannot annul it on his own. The contract may be ratified, implicitly or explicitly, after which the right to annul is lost.

Finally, the author critiques this foreign classification, arguing that relative nullity is not truly nullity but mere 'annullability' (annulabilité). He insists that annullability is a distinct concept from nullity, as the contract remains fully valid until annulled. This critique underscores the precision of Islamic legal terminology, where a contract is either valid (ṣaḥīḥ) or void (bāṭil), with no intermediate category. The passage is significant for students of usul al-iftaa as it demonstrates how to critically evaluate non-Islamic legal categories through the lens of Islamic jurisprudence.

Key Terms
البطلان المطلق (al-buṭlān al-muṭlaq): Nullity that renders the contract legally non-existent from its inception, producing no effects and requiring no judicial declaration.
البطلان النسبي (al-buṭlān al-nisbī): In foreign law, a contract that is valid but can be annulled at the request of a party whose consent was defective; the author critiques this as not being true nullity.
قابلية الإبطال (qābiliyyat al-ibṭāl): Annulability: the legal possibility of having a contract annulled by a court, which the author distinguishes from actual nullity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main flaw in the foreign classification of 'relative nullity'?
A) It does not require a judicial annulment.
B) It is actually a form of absolute nullity.
C) It is not true nullity but mere annullability.
D) It applies only to contracts of minors.

Key Takeaway
Absolute nullity in foreign law corresponds to Hanafi bāṭil, but relative nullity is not true nullity; it is annullability, a concept with no equivalent in Hanafi fiqh.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
Voidness in Hanafi and Western Law: A Critical Comparison
Comparative critique of Western voidness classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish absolute voidness from relative voidness, apply the Hanafi critique to Western legal categories, and classify contracts as void, suspended, or binding using Hanafi terminology.

Core Idea
Relative voidness is not voidness; it is a loss of binding force, captured in Hanafi as suspension (tawaqquf) or khiyar.

Western law divides voidness into absolute and relative. But does 'relative voidness' truly mean the contract is void? In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa argues it does not: it is merely a contract that can be annulled at the request of one party—essentially a loss of binding force (luzum). In Hanafi fiqh, such situations are better understood as suspension (wuquf) or as options (khiyar). This lesson sharpens your ability to evaluate legal classifications and apply precise Hanafi terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Western binary classification
The author describes two types: absolute voidness (batal mutlaq) and relative voidness (batal nisbi). Note the legal effect of each: absolute voidness means the contract is non-existent; relative voidness means it remains valid until annulled by the court at the request of the party whose consent was defective.
Relative voidness is compared by Western jurists to a 'voidable' contract.
2
Understand the Hanafi critique
The author argues that relative voidness contains no true voidness. The contract is fully valid and effective; only its bindingness (luzum) is affected. He contrasts this with absolute voidness, where the contract has no legal existence and can be challenged by anyone or by the judge ex officio.
The critique is methodological: the term 'voidness' is misapplied.
3
Map relative voidness to Hanafi concepts
The author identifies two Hanafi equivalents: (1) tawaqquf (suspension) for cases like the sale of a discerning minor (mawquf pending guardian's approval), and (2) khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) for contracts where one party retains a right to cancel. Both involve a contract that is valid but not absolutely binding until ratification or expiry of the option.
The key Hanafi insight: these are not defects in the contract's existence, but in its binding force.
4
Draw the conclusion about true voidness
The author concludes that in correct fiqh usage, voidness is only one type—absolute voidness—which renders the contract legally non-existent. All other situations that Western law calls relative voidness are either suspension or loss of bindingness.
This clarifies the terminological boundaries of 'batalan' in Hanafi legal theory.

يجوز أن يوصف العقد هنا بالبطلان ولو نسبياً، لأن العقد القابل للإبطال صيانة لحق خاص هو عقد قائم كامل الوجود معتبر من كل الوجوه، منتج أحكامه. أما قابليته للإبطال حماية لأحد الطرفين وصيانة لحقه الخاص فإنما تسلبه قوته الإلزامية فقط، ولا تسمه بالبطلان قط. أفلا يرى أن قابلية الإبطال للعقد الملزم قد تثبت لأحد الطرفين أو لكليهما بالشرط، كما إذا اشترط أحد المتبايعين حق إلغاء البيع إلى مدة معينة، فإنه يثبت له هذا الحق (۱) ، ويكون البيع قابلاً للإلغاء بإرادة المشترط دون توقف على قضاء القاضي بالإلغاء، ومع ذلك يعتبر العقد قبل إلغائه صحيحاً من كل وجه . وليس مقبولاً في المنطق الحقوقي وصفه بأي لون ألوان البطلان ولم يعد الفقه الأجنبي هذه الحالة من صور البطلان النسبي رغم قابلية الإبطال القائمة في العقد بمقتضى الشرط.

Chapter (58): Theory of Voidness in Foreign Law. The difference between absolute and relative voidness among them is the same as the difference between absolute voidness and validity, except for this susceptibility to annulment. Thus, in the case of relative voidness, the contract remains valid and effective until the judge annuls it at the request of the person whose will was tainted by a defect of consent in that contract, i.e., at the request of the one for whom the right of annulment was established to protect his consent and interest, like the deceived buyer. After that, the contract is subject to ratification, becoming final upon its ratification, and his right to annul it lapses, even if his ratification is implicit, such as if he voluntarily executes the contract after learning of the cause of voidness. As for the other party, he has no right to request annulment because the contract is binding on him. Likewise, the judge, when litigation for enforcement is brought before him, may not decide annulment on his own motion without the request of the party with interest, just like any special right of a person that is not adjudicated without the request of its owner. Hence it was called relative voidness, because it is considered with respect to one of the two contracting parties. This is contrary to the case of absolute voidness, for therein the contract is non-existent in the legal realm, it is not considered; if its enforcement is sought, then anyone harmed by enforcement may assert voidness, and the judge may declare it on his own motion, as mentioned earlier. · Refer to the summary in 'Theory of Obligations' by Professor Sanhuri, vol. 163/166, and Colin et Capitant, Droit civil français, vol. 2/259-260. 4/58 - Critique of This Foreign Classification: It is clear from this that relative voidness in foreign law contains nothing of the meaning of voidness, neither much nor little; indeed, it has no trace of the reality of voidness. All it contains is the susceptibility to annulment (annulabilité) granted by law to one of the parties to safeguard his special right. And it is obvious that mere susceptibility to annulment is one thing, and voidness is another. Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Supports in Fiqh: It is not permissible to describe the contract here as void, even relatively, because a contract susceptible to annulment to protect a special right is a contract that is existing, complete in existence, valid in all respects, producing its effects. As for its susceptibility to annulment to protect one party and safeguard his special right, it only divests it of its binding force, and does not brand it with voidness at all. Do you not see that susceptibility to annulment of a binding contract may be established for one or both parties by stipulation, such as when one of the two contracting parties stipulates the right to cancel the sale for a certain period; then this right is established for him (1), and the sale becomes susceptible to cancellation by the will of the stipulator without being dependent on a judge's ruling of cancellation, yet the contract before its cancellation is considered valid in all respects. And it is not acceptable in legal logic to describe it with any color of voidness, and foreign law did not count this case as a form of relative voidness despite the susceptibility to annulment existing in the contract by virtue of the stipulation. So what difference is there between divesting the contract of its binding force by virtue of a stipulation, and divesting it by rule of law due to a presumed defect in the consent of one party, to give him the opportunity to decide his consent to the contract or reject it if he wishes? 5/58 - It is clear from what has preceded that voidness in correct expression is only one type: absolute voidness, and that the cases they call relative voidness are not at all from the meaning of voidness; rather they are a divestment of the contract's bindingness (luzum). Some of these cases, those arising from a deficiency of legal capacity, are considered in the view of our jurists as a form of suspension (tawaqquf) or non-enforceability; thus the contract is called suspended (mawquf), as in the sale of a discerning minor, for it is suspended pending the permission of his guardian or executor (ref.: sec. 5/38). Similarly, the contract of a coerced person in one juristic opinion is suspended in its effect pending his permission after the duress is removed (ref.: sec. 4/34 and 5/55). (1) This is called in our fiqh: option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart), as has been presented on many occasions (ref.: sec. 7/3, eighthly). In foreign legal terminology, this falls under what they call 'resolutory condition' (ref.: sec. 774? [؟].
Source pages p. 729

Commentary
The passage presents a comparative legal analysis of the classification of voidness (batalan). The author critiques the Western division into absolute voidness (batalan mutlaq) and relative voidness (batalan nisbi). He argues that what Western jurists call 'relative voidness' is not truly voidness: it is merely a loss of binding force (luzum), not a defect in the contract's existence. In Hanafi fiqh, such cases are dealt with under different categories. For example, contracts of a discerning minor (mumayyiz) or a person under duress (mukrah) are not void ab initio but are classified as 'mawquf' (suspended), pending ratification. The author uses the analogy of a conditional right to cancel (khiyar al-shart) to show that even in Western law, such a contract is considered valid until cancellation, yet they do not call it relative voidness. He concludes that true voidness is only one type: absolute voidness, which renders the contract legally non-existent from the start. This analysis clarifies the Hanafi position and refines the fiqh terminology by distinguishing between voidness (batalan), suspension (wuquf), and loss of binding force (salb al-luzum).

Key Terms
البطلان المطلق (al-baṭalān al-muṭlaq): العقد الذي لا ينعقد أصلاً في الواقع الاعتباري، فلا أثر له ولا يحتج به.
البطلان النسبي (al-baṭalān al-nisbī): في الفقه الأجنبي: قابلية العقد الصحيح للإبطال بناءً على طلب أحد الطرفين لحماية حقه الخاص. والمؤلف ينفي أن يكون هذا من البطلان في شيء.
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): حالة العقد الموقوف (mawquf) الذي يتوقف نفاده على إجازة ولي أو تصحيح لاحق، كبيع الصغير المميز.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, the concept of 'relative voidness' in Western law corresponds in Hanafi fiqh to:
A) Absolute voidness
B) Irregularity (fasad)
C) Suspension (tawaqquf) or loss of binding force
D) A valid contract with no issue

Key Takeaway
True voidness in Hanafi fiqh is only absolute voidness; cases labeled 'relative voidness' in Western law are actually forms of suspension or loss of binding force.

The Hanafi Critique of Relative Invalidity
Comparative analysis of invalidity concepts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why Hanafi fiqh rejects the concept of relative invalidity and explain the distinction between absolute invalidity, suspension, and removal of bindingness.

Core Idea
Only absolute invalidity is true invalidity; relative invalidity is a misnomer for suspension or non-bindingness.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa critiques the foreign legal concept of 'relative invalidity' (al-buṭlān al-nisbī). He argues that Hanafi fiqh possesses a more precise taxonomy: contracts are either absolutely invalid, suspended (mawqūf), or have their bindingness removed (salab al-luzūm). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly classifying defective contracts in Islamic law and issuing sound fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foreign concept being critiqued
Locate where the author discusses 'relative invalidity' (al-buṭlān al-nisbī) and note how foreign jurisprudence uses it to cover both non-enforceability and voidability.
Foreign law uses 'annulabilité' for contracts voidable due to defects.
2
Understand the Hanafi counter-argument using khiyār al-sharṭ
Examine the example of a conditional option to cancel (khiyār al-sharṭ). The contract is fully valid until cancellation, so labeling it 'invalid' is logically inconsistent.
This shows that voidability does not imply invalidity.
3
Distinguish between absolute invalidity and non-bindingness
Absolute invalidity (buṭlān muṭlaq) nullifies all effects from inception. Non-bindingness (salab al-luzūm) preserves validity but gives a party the right to rescind.
The difference is foundational for correct classification.
4
Recognize the two categories: suspension (tawaqquf) and removal of bindingness (salab al-luzūm)
Cases of capacity deficiency (e.g., minor) are suspended; cases of consent defects (e.g., duress) are non-binding. Both are not invalidity.
These correspond to what foreign law calls relative invalidity.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) for fatwa
A suspended contract requires ratification to become effective; a non-binding contract gives an immediate right to void. The mufti must apply the correct category.
Misclassification leads to erroneous rulings on contract validity.

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه

يجوز أن يوصف العقد هنا بالبطلان ولو نسبياً، لأن العقد القابل للإبطال صيانة لحق خاص هو عقد قائم كامل الوجود معتبر من كل الوجوه، منتج أحكامه. أما قابليته للإبطال حماية لأحد الطرفين وصيانة لحقه الخاص فإنما تسلبه قوته الإلزامية فقط، ولا تسمه بالبطلان قط. أفلا يرى أن قابلية الإبطال للعقد الملزم قد تثبت لأحد الطرفين أو لكليهما بالشرط، كما إذا اشترط أحد المتبايعين حق إلغاء البيع إلى مدة معينة، فإنه يثبت له هذا الحق (۱) ، ويكون البيع قابلاً للإلغاء بإرادة المشترط دون توقف على قضاء القاضي بالإلغاء، ومع ذلك يعتبر العقد قبل إلغائه صحيحاً من كل وجه . وليس مقبولاً في المنطق الحقوقي وصفه بأي لون ألوان البطلان ولم يعد الفقه الأجنبي هذه الحالة من صور البطلان النسبي رغم قابلية الإبطال القائمة في العقد بمقتضى الشرط.

فأي فرق بين سلب القوة الإلزامية عن العقد بمقتضى الشرط، وبين سلبها بحكم القانون لشائبة مفروضة تقديراً في رضا أحد الطرفين كي يفسح له المجال أن يقرر رضاه بالعقد أو يرفضه إن شاء.

٥٨/ ٥ - يتضح مما سلَفَ أنَّ البطلان بالتعبير الصحيح، لا يكون إلا نوعاً واحداً هو البطلان المطلق، وأن الحالات التي يسمونها البطلان النسبي، ليست من معنى البطلان في شيء، وإنما هي سلب للزوم العقد. فهذه الحالات ،بعضها، وهو الناشيء عن نقص الأهلية يعتبر في نظر فقهائنا من قبيل التوقف أو عدم النفاذ، فيسمي العقد معه موقوفاً، كما في بيع الصغير المميز فإنه موقوف على إجازة وليه أو وصيه (ر: ف ٥/٣٨) وكذا عقد المستكره في أحد الآراء الفقهية فإنه متوقف الأثر على إجازته له بعد زوال الإكراه عنه (ر: ف ٤/٣٤ و ٥/٥٥) .

(۱) وهذا يسمى في فقهنا : خيار الشرط) كما تقدّم في مناسبات عديدة (ر: ف ۷/۳ ثامناً وفي اصطلاح الفقه الأجنبي يدخل هذا فيما يسمونه «شرطاً فاسخاً» (ر: ف ٧٧٤) .

الفصل (٥٨) نظرية البطلان في الفقه الأجنبي

أما باقي الحالات الناشئة عن سائر عيوب الرضا فتعتبر من قبيل سلب اللزوم عن العقود اللازمة أي من قبيل التخيير الثابت في حالات عيوب الرضا للعاقد الذي شيب فيها رضاه.

والعقد في هذه الحالات يوصف بأنه مسلوب اللزوم، أي قابل للإبطال بإرادة الطرف المتضرر الذي شيب رضاه فيصبح هذا الطرف مخيراً بين الإبطال ،والإمضاء، كما قدمناه في بحث الإلزام من نظرية العقود (ف ٦/٤٠ - ١١/٤٠/) .

وقد قرر فقهاؤنا قديماً في حالات سلب اللزوم جميع الأحكام التي قررها الفقه الأجنبي الحديث فيما يسميه البطلان النسبي.

هذه

أما الفقه الأجنبي فقد خلط في ذلك بين عدم النفاذ وسلب اللزوم، ثم زج الحالتين في نظرية البطلان فأصبح التعبير بالبطلان النسبي عن الحالات يتجلى فيه خداع العناوين !! فإذا عرف القارىء ما تحته من يتبين فيه سوء التسمية واختلاط الفكرة.

حالات

وقد كان فقهاؤنا في هذه التقاسيم أدق نظراً وتمييزاً، وأصح لغة واصطلاحاً (۱).

٦/٥٨ - اختلاف ما يسمونه البطلان النسبي عن الفساد. بعد ما سَلَف بیانه نخلص إلى القول بأن نظرية الفساد في فقهنا الإسلامي ليس لها مقابل أصلاً في الفقه الأجنبي. فالبطلان النسبي عندهم

(1) يلحظ أن القانون المدني السوري تبعاً لأصله المصري الجديد، قد استعمل فيه لفظ قابلية الإبطال ترجمة حرفية لكلمة annulabilité بدلاً من البطلان النسبي رغم أن القانونين يعتمدان في الدرجة الأولى الفقه الفرنسي الذي تبنى في الأصل تلك التسمية القانونية الخاطئة .
وقد أشار الأستاذ جمال العطيفي في حواشيه على المذكرة الإيضاحية للأصل المصري تحت المواد / ۱۳۸ - ١٤١ إلى هذا العدول في التسمية عمداً في المشروع النهائي للقانون. فكأنما لوحظ ذلك الخطأ في اسم البطلان النسبي، فعدل عنه.
Chapter Seven: Theory of Legal Supports in Jurisprudence

It is permissible to describe the contract here as invalid, even if relatively, because a contract that is voidable to protect a specific right is an existing, complete contract, valid from all aspects, producing its effects. But its voidability to protect one party and safeguard his specific right only removes its binding force, and does not label it with invalidity at all. Does one not see that the voidability of a binding contract may be established for one party or both by condition, as when one of the two contracting parties stipulates the right to cancel the sale within a specified period, then this right is established for him, and the sale becomes cancellable by the will of the stipulator without waiting for a court judgment of cancellation, and yet the contract before its cancellation is considered valid from every aspect. And it is not acceptable in legal logic to describe it with any color of invalidity, and foreign jurisprudence did not count this case as a form of relative invalidity despite the voidability existing in the contract by virtue of the condition.

So what difference is there between removing the binding force from the contract by virtue of a condition, and removing it by law due to a presumptive defect in the consent of one party in order to give him room to decide his consent to the contract or reject it if he wishes?

58/5 - It is clear from the foregoing that invalidity, in correct expression, is only one type: absolute invalidity, and that the cases they call relative invalidity are not from the meaning of invalidity at all, but rather are removal of the bindingness of the contract. Some of these cases – those arising from deficiency of capacity – are considered in the view of our jurists as of the type of suspension or non-enforceability, so the contract with it is called suspended (mawquf), as in the sale of a discerning minor, for it is suspended upon the permission of his guardian or executor; and likewise the contract of the coerced person in one juristic opinion, for its effect is suspended upon his permission after the coercion ceases.

(1) This is called in our fiqh: Khiyar al-Shart (option of condition), as previously mentioned on many occasions. In the terminology of foreign jurisprudence, this falls under what they call a 'rescissory condition'.

Chapter (58): Theory of Invalidity in Foreign Jurisprudence

As for the remaining cases arising from other defects of consent, they are considered of the type of removal of bindingness from binding contracts, i.e., of the type of choice established in cases of defects of consent for the contractor whose consent was tainted.

And the contract in these cases is described as having its bindingness removed, i.e., voidable by the will of the injured party whose consent was tainted, so that this party becomes entitled to choose between voiding and ratification, as we have presented in the discussion of bindingness in the theory of contracts.

And our jurists have long established in cases of removal of bindingness all the rulings that modern foreign jurisprudence established under what it calls relative invalidity.

As for foreign jurisprudence, it confused in that between non-enforceability and removal of bindingness, then bundled the two cases into the theory of invalidity, so the expression 'relative invalidity' for those cases manifests a deception of titles!! So when the reader knows what underlies it, the misnomer and confusion of thought become apparent.

And our jurists in these classifications were more precise in observation and distinction, and more correct in language and terminology.

58/6 - The difference between what they call relative invalidity and corruption (fasād). After what has been explained, we conclude that the theory of corruption in our Islamic fiqh has no counterpart at all in foreign jurisprudence. For relative invalidity in their view...

(1) It is noted that the Syrian Civil Code, following its Egyptian original, has used the term 'voidability' as a literal translation of 'annulabilité' instead of 'relative invalidity', despite the fact that both codes rely primarily on French jurisprudence which originally adopted that erroneous legal nomenclature. Professor Jamal al-'Atifi indicated in his footnotes on the explanatory memorandum of the Egyptian original under Articles 138-141 this deliberate change of nomenclature in the final draft of the law. As if that error in the name 'relative invalidity' was noted, so it was amended.
Source pages p. 730

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal philosophy analysis of the concept of invalidity (būṭlān) in Hanafi fiqh versus Western jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by challenging the foreign legal term 'relative invalidity' (al-buṭlān al-nisbī). He argues that a contract which is 'voidable' (qābil lil-ibṭāl) to protect a specific right is actually a valid contract in all respects, except that its binding force (luzūm) has been removed. He provides the example of khiyār al-sharṭ (option of condition) where a party stipulates the right to cancel the sale within a period; the contract is fully valid until cancellation, so it is not invalidity.

He then categorizes cases that foreign law calls relative invalidity into two types in Hanafi fiqh: (1) cases arising from deficiency of capacity (like sale by a discerning minor) which are considered suspended (mawqūf) or non-enforceable (ghayr nāfidh), and (2) cases arising from defects of consent (other than capacity) which are considered removal of bindingness (salab al-luzūm), giving the affected party the option to void or confirm. The author emphasizes that Hanafi jurists have long treated these cases with precise terminology, while foreign law confuses them under 'relative invalidity'.

Finally, he distinguishes 'relative invalidity' from fasād (corruption) in Islamic law. Fasād is a unique concept in Islamic jurisprudence with no exact counterpart in Western law; it refers to a contract that is defective in a way that can be rectified, whereas relative invalidity in foreign law is essentially a right to rescind. The passage concludes with a footnote criticizing the Syrian Civil Code's adoption of the term 'qābiliyyat al-ibṭāl' (voidability) to avoid the misnomer 'relative invalidity'.

Key Terms
البطلان المطلق (al-buṭlān al-muṭlaq): Absolute invalidity: a contract that is void from its inception, producing no legal effects.
البطلان النسبي (al-buṭlān al-nisbī): Relative invalidity: a term used in foreign jurisprudence for contracts voidable at the option of one party; the author argues this is a misnomer.
سلب اللزوم (salab al-luzūm): Removal of bindingness: a contract that is valid but not binding; the party with the right can choose to uphold or rescind.
عدم النفاذ / التوقف (‘adam al-nāfidh / al-tawaqquf): Non-enforceability / suspension: a contract whose effectiveness depends on subsequent approval (e.g., minor's sale).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about a contract with a defect of consent (e.g., duress) in Hanafi fiqh?
A) It is absolutely invalid (bāṭil) and produces no effects.
B) It is corrupt (fāsid) and can be rectified.
C) It is suspended (mawqūf) until the duress ceases.
D) It is valid but non-binding (salab al-luzūm).

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh recognizes only absolute invalidity; all other defects are either suspension or non-bindingness, preserving the contract's validity.
