Hanafi Theory of Fasad vs. Relative Nullity
Comparing Hanafi fasad with foreign relative nullity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi concept of fasad (invalidity) and the foreign concept of relative nullity, and apply the distinction to determine the legal consequences of a fasid contract.

Core Idea
The Hanafi fasid contract is a third category between validity and voidness, not relative nullity.

This lesson explores a key distinction in Hanafi legal theory: the concept of fasad (invalidity) as a third category of contracts, separate from both valid (sahih) and void (batil). Imam al-Kasani explicitly states that the fasid is a separate category. Many modern jurists mistakenly equate fasad with the foreign doctrine of relative nullity. However, as al-Zarqa explains, they differ fundamentally in purpose and rulings. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying Hanafi contract law correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core concepts
First, locate the definitions of fasad and relative nullity in the passage. Note that fasad is a third rank, while relative nullity is a right to annul a valid contract.
Fasad is a protective support for the Shar'i system; relative nullity protects an individual right.
2
Compare legislative purpose
Read the section on ghaya tashri'iyya. The passage states that fasad aims to distinguish substantial from subsidiary violations of the contracting system. Relative nullity is not systemic but party-specific.
This is the most fundamental difference.
3
Contrast legal consequences
Examine the rulings: fasad cannot be ratified by parties, remains subject to rescission by either party or judge, and its effect is delayed until execution with consideration being equivalent value. For relative nullity, the contract can be ratified and effects are immediate.
Note the opposite outcomes.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a scenario of a contract with ambiguity (gharar fahish), determine whether it is fasid or void under Hanafi law, and whether it could be considered valid under relative nullity.
Use the criteria from the passage.

الباب السابع : نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية في الفقه
لا يقابل الفساد عندنا وإنما يقابل حالات سلب لزوم العقد كما أشرنا إليه . وهو يختلف عن الفساد في الغاية والأحكام:
أ - فمن حيث الغاية التشريعية يختلفان اختلافاً أساسياً، لأن نظرية الفساد إنما هي مؤيد حام للنظام الشرعي في التعاقد يُقصد به تقسيم طريق البطلان وإيجاد مرتبة ثالثة بينه وبين الصحة يقف عندها العقد المخالف النظامه، وذلك للتمييز بين المخالفة الجوهرية الموجبة للبطلان كفقدان ركن المخالفة الفرعية كجهالة مفضية إلى نزاع مشكل، على ما سلف بيانه .
قال الإمام الكاساني في «البدائع» ٢٩٩/٥:
الفاسد عندنا قسم آخر وراء الجائز والباطل» اهـ.
أما البطلان النسبي في الاصطلاح الأجنبي - أو سلب لزوم العقد في اصطلاحنا - فليس مرتبة ثالثة بين الصحة والبطلان، وإنما هو حق في إبطال العقد الصحيح ممنوح لأحد العاقدين حماية له من ضرر محتمل لم يتحقق رضاه به وهو موجود في فقهنا إلى جانب نظرية الفساد تحت اسم سلب اللزوم، كما بيّناه فهو مؤيد لحماية حق خاص، لا للنظام التشريعي العام كما في نظرية الفساد.
فالفساد بالجهالة المفسدة مثلاً لا يقصد به حماية أحد العاقدين، بل حماية نظام التعاقد الذي يوجب معلومية محل الالتزام وسائر النواحي التي يتوقف عليها تنفيذه ضمن حدود الإرادات المُنْشِئَةِ له .
ب - ومن حيث الأحكام قد رأينا أنَّ الفساد لا يزول بإجازة الطرفين عند فقهائنا، بل يبقى العقد مستحقاً للفسخ ويجوز لكل من الطرفين أن يتمسك بالفساد فيفسخ العقد، كما يفسخه القاضي أيضاً دون طلب.
وحكم العقد الفاسد أي أثره يتأخر فلا يثبت إلا بالتنفيذ. والعوض الواجب بالتنفيذ إنما هو العوض المعادل أي القيمة أو أجر المثل، لا العوض المسمى في العقد الفاسد كما تقدم ( ر ) ف ٢/٥٦ - ٣ و ٥/٥٦ رأينا .
أما البطلان النسبي في الفقه الأجنبي فنتائجه بعكس ذلك كله كما رأينا .
Chapter Seven: Theory of the Shar'i Supporting Means in Fiqh. According to us, fasad (invalidity) is not equivalent to [relative nullity]; rather it corresponds to cases of withdrawing binding effect (salb al-luzum) as we indicated. And it differs from fasad in purpose and rulings: (a) In terms of legislative purpose, they differ fundamentally, because the theory of fasad is a protective support for the Shar'i system of contracting, intended to divide the path of nullity and create a third rank between it and validity at which a contract violating the system stops, to distinguish between a substantial violation necessitating nullity, like loss of a pillar, and a subsidiary violation like ignorance leading to a problematic dispute, as previously explained. Imam al-Kasani said in al-Bada'i 5/299: 'For us, the invalid (fasid) is another category beyond the permissible and the void.' As for relative nullity in foreign terminology - or withdrawal of binding effect in our terminology - it is not a third rank between validity and nullity, but rather a right to annul a valid contract granted to one of the contracting parties to protect him from potential harm to which his consent was not realized. It exists in our fiqh alongside the theory of fasad under the name salb al-luzum, as we explained; it is a support for protecting a specific right, not for the general legislative system as in the theory of fasad. For example, fasad due to invalidating ignorance does not aim to protect one of the parties, but rather to protect the contracting system that requires certainty of the subject matter and all other aspects upon which its execution depends within the limits of the constitutive wills. (b) Regarding rulings, we have seen that fasad is not removed by the parties' ratification according to our jurists; rather the contract remains subject to rescission, and each party may insist on the fasad and rescind the contract, and the judge may also rescind it without request. The ruling of a fasid contract - i.e., its effect - is delayed and is only established by execution. The consideration due upon execution is the equivalent consideration, i.e., value or fair wage, not the named consideration in the fasid contract, as previously seen (see vol. 2/56-3 and 5/56). As for relative nullity in foreign jurisprudence, its consequences are the opposite of all that, as we have seen.
Source pages p. 732

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Aam compares the Hanafi theory of fasad (invalidity) with the foreign concept of relative nullity (al-batlan al-nisbi). The author begins by clarifying that Hanafi fasad is not equivalent to relative nullity; rather it corresponds to cases of 'salb al-luzum' (withdrawal of binding effect). He then highlights two key differences: first, the legislative purpose – the theory of fasad is a protective mechanism for the overall Shari‘ah system of contracting, creating a middle ground between absolute validity and absolute nullity to distinguish substantial defects (like missing a pillar) from subsidiary defects (like ambiguous ignorance). In contrast, relative nullity is only a right granted to one party to annul a valid contract to protect a specific interest. Second, the legal consequences: a fasid contract cannot be ratified by the parties; it remains subject to rescission at the option of either party or the judge. Its effects are delayed until execution, and the consideration is the equivalent (qimah or ujr al-mithl), not the named consideration. Relative nullity has opposite effects. The passage cites Imam al-Kasani's statement that the fasid is a separate category beyond the halal and batil. This illustrates the unique Hanafi tripartite division of contracts (sahih, fasid, batil) versus the bipartite division (sahih/batil) of other schools and modern civil law. The author emphasizes that fasad protects the general legislative system, while salb al-luzum protects individual rights. This distinction is crucial for understanding Hanafi contract theory.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasad): حالة العقد المخالف للنظام الشرعي مخالفة فرعية لا جوهرية، فيكون صحيحاً في الأصل غير لازم، ويبطل بالفسخ
البطلان النسبي (al-batlan al-nisbi): حق في إبطال عقد صحيح ممنوح لأحد العاقدين حماية له من ضرر محتمل
سلب اللزوم (salb al-luzum): حالة العقد الصحيح الذي لا يلزم أحد الطرفين حماية له من ضرر، وله حق فسخه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
ما الفرق بين الفساد عند الحنفية والبطلان النسبي في الفقه الأجنبي من حيث إجازة العقد؟
A) الفساد يزول بالإجازة، والبطلان النسبي لا يزول.
B) الفساد لا يزول بالإجازة، والبطلان النسبي يزول بالإجازة.
C) كليهما يزول بالإجازة.
D) كليهما لا يزول بالإجازة.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fasid contract is a distinct third category that protects the contracting system, whereas relative nullity protects a party's individual right.

Hanafi Fasad vs. Foreign Relative Nullity
Comparing fasad and relative nullity in purpose and effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast Hanafi fasad with foreign relative nullity in terms of purpose and legal effect, and apply the rule that fasad cannot be cured by consent of the parties.

Core Idea
Fasad protects the contracting system, not a party; it cannot be cured by consent.

In this comparative analysis, we examine the Islamic concept of fasad (irregularity) and its distinction from relative nullity in foreign jurisprudence. Understanding this difference is crucial for muftis when evaluating contracts that may be defective but not utterly void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Purpose of Fasad vs. Relative Nullity
Recognize that relative nullity protects a specific party's right, whereas fasad—like that due to uncertainty (jahl)—protects the entire system of contracting by ensuring clarity and definiteness.
2
Analyze the Effect of Fasad on Contract Validity
Note that in the Hanafi school, fasad is not removed by subsequent consent of the parties; the contract remains voidable and can be rescinded by either party or the judge.
3
Examine the Deferred Effect and Consideration
Understand that a fasid contract produces no immediate legal effects; upon execution, only equivalent consideration (qimah/ujr al-mithl) is due, not the named price.

سلب اللزوم، كما بيّناه فهو مؤيد لحماية حق ،خاص، لا للنظام التشريعي العام كما في نظرية الفساد.

Chapter (58): Theory of Nullity in Foreign Jurisprudence
It strips binding effect, as we have explained, so it supports the protection of a specific right, not the general legislative system as in the theory of fasad.
For example, fasad due to misleading uncertainty is not intended to protect one of the contracting parties, but to protect the system of contracting that requires the object of obligation and all other aspects upon which its execution depends to be known within the limits of the wills that created it.
B – In terms of rulings, we have seen that fasad is not removed by the consent of both parties according to our jurists; rather, the contract remains subject to rescission, and each party may rely on the fasad to rescind the contract, just as the judge may rescind it without request.
The ruling on a fasid contract, i.e., its effect, is deferred and is not established except upon execution. The consideration due upon execution is only the equivalent consideration, i.e., the value or fair wage, not the consideration named in the fasid contract, as previously mentioned (see vol. 2/56-3 and 5/56.
Source pages p. 733

Commentary
This passage is a comparative legal analysis contrasting the Islamic concept of fasad (irregularity) with relative nullity in foreign jurisprudence. The author first explains the purpose: relative nullity protects a specific party's right, whereas fasad—like that arising from uncertainty (jahl) that corrupts the contract—protects the entire contracting system by requiring clarity and definiteness. The second point addresses the effect: according to Hanafi jurists, fasad cannot be cured by subsequent consent (ijazah) of the parties—the contract remains voidable and any party or the judge may rescind it. Moreover, the contract produces no immediate legal effects; only upon actual execution does the counterpart become due, and it is the market value (qimah) or fair wage (ujr al-mithl), not the agreed price. This distinguishes fasad from relative nullity, which can often be ratified.

Key Terms
فساد (fasād): Irregularity: a contract that is not inherently void but defective, subject to rescission.
بطلان نسبي (buṭlān nisbī): Relative nullity in foreign law: voidable due to defect protect one party, curable by consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, can the parties consent to cure a fasid contract?
A) Yes, because the defect is only between them.
B) No, fasad does not cease by consent; it remains subject to rescission.
C) Yes, but only if the defect is uncertainty (jahl).
D) No, but the judge cannot rescind it.

Key Takeaway
Fasad in Hanafi law protects the contractual system and cannot be cured by party consent, distinguishing it from foreign relative nullity.

Introduction to Legal Capacity (Ahliyyah): Basis, Definition, and Its Role Across Fiqh
Outlines the chapter structure and defines the basis of ahliyyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition and types of ahliyyah and identify its role in contracts, worship, and criminal liability according to the Hanafi framework.

Core Idea
Legal capacity (ahliyyah) is the foundation for validity and obligation in all legal acts.

In this lesson, we begin the study of legal capacity (ahliyyah)—a fundamental concept that underpins all Islamic jurisprudence. The passage from al-Zarqa outlines the chapter structure and then dives into the basis of ahliyyah, showing how it is a condition for contracts, acts of worship, and criminal liability. You will learn the two types of capacity: for validity (ahliyyat al-wujub) and for obligation (ahliyyat al-ada'). Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti when assessing the legal effects of actions by minors, the insane, and other parties.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall chapter structure
Identify the four branches of the theory: definition & types, five life stages, obstacles, and guardianship. This structure provides a map for the entire discussion of ahliyyah.
This is a conceptual roadmap; it helps you see where each topic fits.
2
Grasp the basis of ahliyyah
Recognize that ahliyyah is a condition for the validity of contracts, judicial acts (claims, acknowledgments, testimonies), worship, and criminal liability. The passage reminds us that absence of capacity (e.g., insanity, non-discerning minority) leads to nullity.
Ahliyyah is not limited to contracts—it permeates all areas of fiqh.
3
Differentiate between the two types of ahliyyah in worship
Distinguish between capacity for an act to be valid (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity for it to be obligatory (ahliyyat al-ada'). A minor's prayer can be valid but not obligatory; an adult's prayer is both.
This distinction carries practical consequences for when compensation (qada) is required.
4
Apply the concept to a concrete scenario
Consider a non-discerning minor who performs a sale. According to the passage, such a contract is void because the minor lacks ahliyyah for transactional acts. Similarly, an insane person's testimony is invalid.
The ruling is batil (void), not merely fasid, because the condition of capacity is missing.

Chapter Eight: The Theory of Legal Capacity (Ahliyyah) and Guardianship (Wilayah)
Overall Plan of the Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship
1/59 - We have distributed the chapters of the theory of legal capacity and guardianship under four branches:
First branch: Includes Chapter 59 on the basis, definition, and types of legal capacity.
Second branch: Includes five chapters (60-64) on the five stages of human legal capacity according to the phases of his life: the fetal stage, then childhood, then discernment, then puberty, then sound judgment.
Third branch: Includes Chapter 65 on obstacles to legal capacity.
Fourth branch: Includes Chapter 66 on guardianship or legal agency. Then we conclude the eighth chapter with two important appendices: the first explains the criticisms of the traditional fiqh presentation of obstacles to legal capacity; the second clarifies the advantage of our scholarly focus on the theory of legal capacity in a way that solved its problems.

Chapter Fifty-Nine
The Basis of Legal Capacity, Its Definition and Types
First Section: The Basis of Legal Capacity
2/59 - We saw in the discussion of general conditions of formation in contract theory that among those conditions is the capacity of the contracting party to contract (Section 2/31).
And we saw in the discussion of nullity in the theory of legal effects that among the reasons for nullity of a contract is that the contractor lacks the necessary capacity for formation, such as the insane and the non-discerning minor (Section 2/52).
Acts and dispositions that involve validity and nullity, such as claims and all [actions] regarding rights before the judiciary, and such as acknowledgment of a right and testimony regarding a right, all of that requires that the person be capable of performing these acts and dispositions; otherwise they are invalid and disregarded, because one of the conditions of their validity is legal capacity.
Similarly, religious acts of worship such as prayer, fasting, and others, they depend in the person on two types of legal capacity:
- Capacity for these acts of worship to be valid from him, even if he is not obligated by them, for acts of worship may be concluded and valid from a non-obligated minor.
- Capacity for these acts of worship to become obligatory upon him, so he becomes obligated by them and responsible for abandoning them.
Similarly, penalties for crimes require for their entitlement, legally and by law, that
Source pages p. 734

Commentary
This passage opens the eighth chapter of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa al-Zarqa, dedicated to the theory of legal capacity (ahliyyah) and guardianship (wilayah). The author first presents an overall plan dividing the topic into four branches: (1) definition and basis of ahliyyah; (2) the five stages of human development (fetus, childhood, discernment, puberty, sound judgment); (3) obstacles to ahliyyah; (4) guardianship. Two appendices follow: one critiquing classical treatments of obstacles, another highlighting the author's own systematic approach. Then he begins Chapter 59 on the basis of ahliyyah, demonstrating its pivotal role by reminding us that ahliyyah is a condition for valid contracts (as seen earlier in contract theory), and its absence (insanity, non-discerning minority) causes nullity. He extends the necessity of ahliyyah beyond contracts to all legal acts: claims, acknowledgments, testimonies – all require capacity or they are void. He then categorizes ahliyyah in worship into two types: (a) capacity for validity (allowing an act to be sound even without obligation, as in a minor's valid prayer), and (b) capacity for obligation (making one responsible and sinful for omission). Finally, he notes that criminal liability also hinges on capacity. The passage establishes that ahliyyah is a universal condition for legal effect across all areas of Islamic law.

Key Terms
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity: the quality in a person that makes his acts and dispositions legally effective, either for validity (ahliyyat al-wujub) or for obligation (ahliyyat al-ada').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the contract of a non-discerning minor considered void?
A) Because he lacks the intention (niyyah) required for the contract.
B) Because he lacks the necessary legal capacity (ahliyyah) for the contract to be valid.
C) Because his guardian did not give permission.
D) Because he did not pay the price.

Key Takeaway
Legal capacity (ahliyyah) is a universal condition for the validity of all legal acts and for the obligation of duties, and it is divided into capacity for validity and capacity for obligation.

Definition and Basis of Legal Capacity (Ahliyyah)
Defining ahliyyah and analyzing its foundational elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ahliyyah, identify its two foundational elements (the Lawgiver's determination and human development), and explain why physical capacity is included.

Core Idea
Legal capacity is a divinely-given quality that enables a person to receive legal address, growing with physical and mental maturity.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa defines legal capacity (ahliyyah) as a quality the Lawgiver envisions in the person, making them a suitable locus for legislative address. He explains that this capacity develops gradually through stages of human development, and crucially, it requires both physical and mental ability—not just rationality. Understanding this definition is foundational for any mufti assessing who is obligated by shar‘i rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Notice the opening example of criminal liability: an insane person or minor is not punished because they lack ahliyyah. This shows why legal capacity is needed: to be eligible for responsibility.
2
Extract the two observations
From section 3/5, note that ahliyyah is based on (a) qualities required by the Lawgiver and (b) these qualities vary per act (e.g., accepting a gift vs. giving a gift).
This explains why different acts have different capacity thresholds.
3
Study the definition
Read the formal definition in section 4/59: 'a quality that the Lawgiver envisions in the person, making him a suitable locus for a legislative address.' Break it down: sifah (quality), yuqaddiruha al-Shari‘ (envisioned by the Lawgiver), mahall salih (suitable locus), khitab tashri‘i (legislative address).
The definition is general and inclusive of all types of ahliyyah.
4
Analyze the cumulative nature
In the analysis (5/59), ahliyyah is cumulative: it grows gradually through stages—first capacity to receive rights, then obligations, then transactions, then full responsibility at rushd. Each stage parallels physical and mental development.
This aligns with the Hanafi division into ahliyyah al-wujub and ahliyyah al-ada’.
5
Note the inclusion of physical capacity
The footnote and the text emphasize that ahliyyah includes physical ability, not just mental. Acts of worship like prayer and fasting require physical capacity, as does jihad. This corrects the misconception that capacity is purely cognitive.
This is a distinctive feature of Islamic legal capacity compared to some other systems.

Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship
The offender is eligible for the imposition of this penal liability upon him, as discipline and deterrence for others. If the offender does not have legal capacity for criminal liability, like the insane and the minor, then he does not deserve punishment. Thus we find legal capacity required in every step a person takes in his actions and dispositions whose results depend on specific qualities in the acting and disposing person.
During it:
3/5 - From these observations the basis of legal capacity becomes clear to us, and we see:
a) That legal capacity is based on the necessity of the person being characterized with the qualities that the Lawgiver deems necessary to exist in him so that legal rulings can be established in his regard, and so that dispositions can be valid from him and their results can follow.
b) And that these required qualities are in each subject according to what is appropriate for it.
And for some actions or dispositions, a legal capacity with limits different from what is required for other actions or dispositions may be stipulated in the person. Thus the legal capacity required in a person for the validity of his acceptance of a gift from another differs from the legal capacity required for the validity of his own gift and other voluntary donations (1).
So what is this legal capacity in its general meaning in legislative consideration and its terminology?
Then what are its types and effects? This is what we will see in the following:
Second Sub-Requirement: Definition of Legal Capacity
4/59 - Legal capacity is a characteristic of persons. In lexical usage it is used to mean worthiness and competency for a matter; it is said: so-and-so is worthy (ahl) of leadership, i.e., he is deserving of it, and so-and-so is worthy of great things, i.e., he is competent for them.
In juristic terminology, legal capacity in its comprehensive general concept can be defined as a quality that the Lawgiver envisions in the person, making him a suitable locus for a legislative address.
5/59 - Analysis of the Definition:
1- The legal capacities of persons are cumulative qualities because, according to the preceding explanation, they are the stages of human development, physical and mental (1). By this gradual development, the person first becomes prepared for the establishment of rights for him, then for the establishment of obligations upon him, then for the validity of some dispositions and transactions; then finally he becomes prepared for responsibility for violating everything that legislation imposes and for the commitments he takes on by his will and undertakes, as his legal capacity is completed by his reaching the stage of maturity (rushd).
Thus this human development, which is followed like a shadow by the gradual development of legal capacity, is essentially competency and fitness, i.e., a quality and potentiality in the person, as stated in the definition, growing and expanding gradually like his other innate talents and perceptions.
2- And this potentiality, it is up to the Lawgiver Himself to determine its estimation and specify its degrees according to the stages of development in the person, and his safety from defects; because the Lawgiver is the Ruler speaking to people with commands and prohibitions for their welfare and protection.
(1) Legal capacity in its general sense in Islamic jurisprudence is related to physical development, not only mental, because in Islam there are practical religious obligations that require physical ability alongside mental awareness; such as acts of worship of various kinds like prayer, fasting, and others, and other practical collective obligations like jihad, for example. So a religious obligation concerning any of that is not directed to anyone unless he possesses physical ability alongside the mental element to be eligible to bear obligations.
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa opens the chapter on legal capacity (ahliyyah) and guardianship (wilayah). The author first illustrates the practical importance of ahliyyah through the example of criminal liability: an insane person or a minor is not punishable because they lack capacity. He then extracts two foundational observations: (a) ahliyyah is based on the Lawgiver (al-Shari‘) requiring certain qualities in a person for legal rulings to apply and transactions to be valid; (b) these qualities vary according to the act—for example, accepting a gift requires only discernment (tamyiz), while giving a gift requires majority and rationality (rushd) because it involves financial loss. The author then defines ahliyyah in its general sense as 'a quality that the Lawgiver envisions in the person, making him a suitable locus for a legislative address.' This definition emphasizes that ahliyyah is a legal construct (sifah yuqaddiruha al-Shari‘) not merely a natural fact. In the analysis, he stresses that ahliyyah is cumulative (takamuliyyah), growing gradually with physical and mental development: first capacity to receive rights, then to bear obligations, then to perform transactions, and finally full legal responsibility at the age of rushd. Importantly, the author notes that in Islamic law, ahliyyah involves physical capacity too, not just mental—because acts of worship like prayer and fasting require physical ability, as does jihad. This corrects a common misconception that legal capacity is only cognitive. The passage sets the stage for a detailed discussion of types and effects of ahliyyah.

Key Terms
الأهلية (al-ahliyyah): صفة يقدرها الشارع في الشخص تجعله محلاً صالحاً لخطاب تشريعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A natural ability to understand law
B) A quality envisioned by the Lawgiver making a person suitable for legal address
C) The physical strength to perform obligations
D) The right to own property

Key Takeaway
Ahliyyah is a divinely-decreed, gradual quality that evolves with physical and mental growth, making a person capable of bearing legal address and responsibility.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Legal Capacity (Ahliyyah): Definition and Types
Defines legal capacity, analyzes its definition, and introduces its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define legal capacity (ahliyyah) as per Hanafi usul, analyze its definition into four components, and distinguish between ahliyyat al-wujub and ahliyyat al-ada'.

Core Idea
Legal capacity is a gradual quality determined by the Lawgiver, making a person fit for legislative address.

Legal capacity (ahliyyah) is a foundational concept in Hanafi legal theory. It answers the question: who can be addressed by the Lawgiver and be subject to rights, obligations, and legal actions? In this lesson, we unpack Zarqa's definition and see how capacity develops gradually from the fetus to the mature adult, and how it splits into two types: the capacity to have rights (ahliyyat al-wujub) and the capacity to exercise rights (ahliyyat al-ada').

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning
Start with the everyday sense of ahliyyah: worthiness and sufficiency. This grounds the technical term.
Linguistic meaning helps remember the core idea of fitness.
2
State the juristic definition
Memorize the exact definition: 'a quality that the Lawgiver measures in a person, making him a fit locus for a legislative address.' Break it into four parts.
The definition is the anchor for all further discussion.
3
Analyze the four components
Examine each part: (1) quality in the person, (2) measured by the Lawgiver, (3) fit locus, (4) legislative address. Understand why each is needed.
The indefinite 'legislative address' is key to include minimal capacity (fetus).
4
Understand the gradual nature
Capacity is not static; it develops with physical and mental maturity, from rights for the person to rights against, to valid actions, to full responsibility.
This explains why minors and the insane have limited capacity.
5
Distinguish the two types
Ahliyyat al-wujub enables a person to have rights and obligations; ahliyyat al-ada' enables valid performance of actions. Note their different applications.
A fetus has ahliyyat al-wujub but not ahliyyat al-ada'.

الفصل (٥٩) مبنى الأهلية وتعريفها وأنواعها
بمعنى الجدارة والكفاية لأمر من الأمور ؛ يقال؛ فلان أهل للرئاسة أي هو جدير بها، وفلان أهل للعظائم، أي هو كفي لها.
وفي الاصطلاح الفقهي يمكن تعريف الأهلية في مفهومها العام الشامل بأنها صفة يقدرها الشارع في الشخص تجعله محلاً صالحاً لخطاب تشريعي .
٥/٥٩ - تحليل التعريف :
۱ - أهليات الأشخاص صفات تكاملية لأنها بحسب الإيضاح السالف البيان هي مراحل التكامل الإنساني، جسماً وعقلا (۱). فبهذا التكامل التدريجي يتهيأ الشخص أولاً لثبوت الحقوق له، ثم لثبوت الحقوق عليه، ثم لصحة بعض التصرفات والمعاملات ؛ ثم في النهاية يتهيأ للمسؤولية عن الإخلال بكل ما يوجبه التشريع وعن الالتزامات التي يلتزمها بإرادته ويتعهد بها، إذ تكمل أهليته بوصوله إلى مرحلة الرشد.
فهذا التكامل الإنساني الذي يتبعه كالظل تكامل الأهلية التدريجي إنما هو كفاية ولياقة أي أنه صفة وقابلية في الشخص، كما جاء في التعريف، تنمو وتتسع تدريجياً كسائر مواهبه الفطرية ومداركه .
٢ - وهذه القابلية إنما يعود إلى الشارع نفسه أمر تقديرها وتعيين درجاتها تبعاً لمراحل التكامل في الشخص، ولسلامته من الآفات؛ لأن الشارع هو الحاكم المتكلم على الناس أمراً ونهياً بما يصلحهم ويصون حقوق أفرادهم وجماعتهم بتلك الأحكام التي يشرعها لهم. فإليه يعود تقدير كفاءة كل من الناس لكل من تلك الأحكام.
ولذا جاء في التعريف أنها: يقدرها الشارع».
٣ - والمراد بالخطاب التشريعي الحكم الشرعي، فهما مترادفان في اصطلاح علم أصول الفقه فما يشرعه الشارع، سواء أكان من قسم العبادات الدينية كالصلاة والصيام، أو من قسم الالتزامات والحقوق المدنية كوجوب تنفيذ العقود وضمان المتلفات، وثبوت ملكية الممتلكات عند وجود سبب من أسباب التملك، ووجوب النفقة بشرائطها بين الأزواج والأقارب، كل ذلك وأمثاله يسمى: أحكاماً شرعية، باعتبار أن الشارع قد حكم بها على الناس وأوجب تطبيقها بينهم ويسمى أيضاً خطاباً تشريعياً، باعتبار أن الشارع في تشريعه إنما يخاطب الناس بهذه الأحكام آمراً وناهياً، ويلزمهم بتنفيذها واحترامها (ر: أصول الفقه للخضري ص ٢١ الطبعة الأولى).
٤ - وإذا كانت أهلية الإنسان مراحل ودرجات بحسب تكامله وسلامته من الآفات الجسمية والعقلية كما أشرنا إليه آنفاً وهو في كل مرحلة أهل لبعض الأحكام أو التبعات دون بعض لذلك أوردنا في تعريف الأهلية بمفهومها العام لفظ الخطاب التشريعي بالتنكير ليشمل أدنى الأهليات لأقل الأحكام كأهلية الجنين على ما سنرى .
المطلب الثالث: أنواع الأهلية
٦/٥٩ - إنَّ الأهلية بمعناها العام الشامل المشروح تنقسم إلى نوعين أساسيين هما أهلية الوجوب، وأهلية الأداء.
وسنشرح فيما يلي هذين النوعين كما نقسم أفعال الناس إلى أنواع بحسب ارتباط كل نوع من أفعالهم بأحد نوعي الأهلية :
Chapter (59): The Basis of Legal Capacity, Its Definition, and Types
In the sense of worthiness and sufficiency for a matter; it is said: So-and-so is worthy of leadership, i.e., he is deserving of it, and so-and-so is worthy of great matters, i.e., he is sufficient for them.
In juristic terminology, legal capacity in its general comprehensive concept can be defined as: a quality that the Lawgiver measures in a person, making him a fit locus for a legislative address.
5/59 - Analysis of the Definition:
1 - The capacities of persons are cumulative qualities, because according to the previous explanation, they are stages of human development, physically and mentally. Through this gradual development, the person becomes prepared first for the establishment of rights for him, then for the establishment of rights against him, then for the validity of certain actions and transactions; then finally, he becomes prepared for responsibility for violating everything that legislation requires and for obligations he undertakes by his will and pledges, since his capacity is completed upon reaching the age of maturity.
This human development, which is followed like a shadow by the gradual development of capacity, is indeed sufficiency and fitness, meaning it is a quality and potentiality in the person, as stated in the definition, growing and expanding gradually like his other innate talents and perceptions.
2 - This potentiality is determined by the Lawgiver Himself in terms of its estimation and specification of its degrees according to the stages of development in the person and his freedom from defects; because the Lawgiver is the ruler who addresses people with commands and prohibitions for their benefit and to protect the rights of individuals and communities through those rulings He legislates for them. Thus, the estimation of each person's competence for each of those rulings is up to Him.
Hence, the definition states: 'the Lawgiver measures it.'
3 - What is meant by 'legislative address' is the legal ruling; they are synonymous in the terminology of the science of Legal Theory. Whatever the Lawgiver legislates, whether from the category of religious acts of worship like prayer and fasting, or from the category of obligations and civil rights like the obligation to execute contracts and compensate for damages, the establishment of ownership of properties when a cause of ownership exists, and the obligation of maintenance with its conditions between spouses and relatives, all that and its likes are called 'legal rulings,' considering that the Lawgiver has ruled them upon people and obligated their application among them. They are also called 'legislative address,' considering that the Lawgiver in His legislation addresses people with these rulings, commanding and forbidding, and obligates them to implement and respect them (see: Usul al-Fiqh by al-Khudari, p. 21, first edition).
4 - Since a person's capacity has stages and degrees according to his development and freedom from physical and mental defects, as we indicated earlier, and in each stage he is fit for some rulings or responsibilities but not others, we therefore used the term 'legislative address' in the definition of capacity in its general concept in an indefinite form to include the lowest capacities for the least rulings, such as the capacity of the fetus, as we will see.
Third Subsection: Types of Legal Capacity
6/59 - Legal capacity in its general comprehensive sense as explained is divided into two basic types: capacity to acquire rights (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity to exercise rights (ahliyyat al-ada').
We will explain these two types below, as we also divide people's actions into types according to the relationship of each type of action to one of the two types of capacity.
Source pages p. 737

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of legal capacity (ahliyyah) in Hanafi fiqh from the work Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It begins by providing the linguistic meaning of ahliyyah—worthiness and sufficiency—and then defines it juristically as a quality determined by the Lawgiver (the Shar'i) that makes a person a suitable subject for legislative address (khitab tashri'i). The definition is then analyzed in four points: (1) Ahliyyah is a cumulative quality that develops gradually alongside physical and mental human development, first enabling rights for a person, then rights against him, then validity of transactions, and finally full responsibility; (2) The Lawgiver alone determines the levels of capacity based on the person's development and freedom from defects; (3) 'Legislative address' means legal rulings (ahkam shar'iyyah), covering both acts of worship (ibadat) and civil obligations (mu'amalat); (4) Because capacity has degrees, the definition uses indefinite 'legislative address' to include minimal capacity like that of a fetus.

Finally, it states that ahliyyah divides into two fundamental types: ahliyyat al-wujub (capacity to have rights and obligations) and ahliyyat al-ada' (capacity to perform actions legally). The text promises to elaborate further on these types and their correlation with people's actions.

Key Terms
الأهلية (al-ahliyyah): صفة يقدرها الشارع في الشخص تجعله محلاً صالحاً لخطاب تشريعي
الخطاب التشريعي (al-khitab al-tashri'i): المراد به الحكم الشرعي، وهو ما يشرعه الشارع من أحكام سواء كانت عبادات أو معاملات
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): النوع الأول من أنواع الأهلية: القدرة على ثبوت الحقوق والواجبات للشخص وعليه
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada'): النوع الثاني: القدرة على القيام بالأفعال الشرعية كالعقود والتصرفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Legal capacity is a graded quality determined by the Lawgiver, enabling a person to be a subject of legislation, and it divides into capacity to have rights (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity to perform actions (ahliyyat al-ada').

Legal Capacity: Definition and Types
Defining legal capacity and introducing its division into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of legal capacity (ahliyya) and identify its division into capacity for obligations and capacity for performance.

Core Idea
Legal capacity is a graded attribute based on humanity; it divides into capacity for obligations and capacity for performance.

In this foundational passage from al-Zarqa's work, we explore the general theory of legal capacity (ahliyya). The author defines it as the Lawgiver's determination of a person's competence for legal rulings. He then introduces the two fundamental types—ahliyyat al-wujub and ahliyyat al-ada'—which serve as the framework for understanding who can bear rights and duties in Islamic law.

Grasp the definition

The indefinite form of 'khitab tashri'i' is intentional to include minimal capacity.
2
Recognize the basis
Capacity is not uniform; it has stages based on development and soundness. The definition uses indefinite form to cover even the fetus.
3
Identify the two types
The passage divides capacity into capacity for obligations (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada').
These two types will be elaborated in subsequent lessons.

ب - وإذا كان أهلاً فوق ذلك لترتب الالتزامات المالية عليه أيضاً فهو ذو أهلية وجوب كاملة كالصغير غير المميز والمجنون على ما يأتي تفصيله.

فأهلية الوجوب ذات عنصرين:
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. The rights of their individuals and groups through those rulings that the Lawgiver legislates for them. So to Him returns the estimation of the competence of each person for each of those rulings. Hence it came in the definition that: the Lawgiver determines it. - And what is meant by legislative address is the legal ruling; they are synonymous in the terminology of the science of Usul al-Fiqh. Whatever the Lawgiver legislates, whether from the category of religious acts of worship like prayer and fasting, or from the category of obligations and civil rights like the obligation to execute contracts, guarantee for damages, the establishment of ownership of properties when a cause of ownership exists, and the obligation of maintenance with its conditions between spouses and relatives, all that and similar examples are called: legal rulings, considering that the Lawgiver has ruled by them upon people and obligated their application among them. It is also called legislative address, considering that the Lawgiver, in His legislation, addresses people with these rulings as commander and prohibitor, and obliges them to implement and respect them (see: Usul al-Fiqh by al-Khudari, p. 21, first edition). 4 - And if a person's legal capacity has stages and degrees according to his completeness and soundness from physical and mental afflictions, as we referred to earlier, and in each stage he is capable of some rulings or responsibilities and not others, therefore we included in the definition of legal capacity in its general sense the word 'legislative address' in an indefinite form to include the lowest capacities for the least rulings, like the capacity of the fetus as we will see. The Third Section: Types of Legal Capacity. 6/59 - Indeed legal capacity in its explained general comprehensive sense is divided into two basic types: capacity for obligations (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada'). We will explain below these two types, as we also divide people's actions into types according to the connection of each type of their actions with one of the two types of capacity. Chapter (59): The Foundation of Legal Capacity, its Definition and Types. First: Capacity for Obligations. 7/59 - Capacity for obligations is: the person's suitability for imposition (ilzam) and commitment (iltizam). What is meant by imposition is the establishment of rights for him, such as his entitlement to the value of damaged property from his wealth from whoever damages it, and the transfer of ownership to him in what he buys or borrows, and the obligation of his maintenance upon others if he is poor and unable—his relatives. And what is meant by commitment is the establishment of rights upon him, such as his commitment to pay the price of what he buys, and the substitute for a loan from his wealth, and his commitment to maintain those whose maintenance is obligatory upon him among the poor and unable if he is wealthy, etc. So every capacity that the Lawgiver requires in a person for the emergence of a right for him or the imposition of an obligation upon him is a capacity for obligation. 8/59 - The Basis of Capacity for Obligations: This capacity for obligation in the view of Islamic law has as its basis the human attribute, and it has no connection to age, intellect, or maturity. Rather, every human being in any stage or condition, even the fetus and the insane, is considered to possess this capacity for obligations, but the capacity for obligations in him may be complete or deficient: A - If he is capable of the establishment of some rights only for him and is not capable of obligations being imposed upon him, then he has deficient capacity for obligation, like the fetus in its mother's womb before birth, as will be detailed. B - If he is capable beyond that of financial obligations being imposed upon him as well, then he has complete capacity for obligation, like the non-discerning minor and the insane, as will be detailed. 9/59 - Elements of Capacity for Obligations: Capacity for obligations has two elements:
Source pages p. 738

Commentary
This passage is a foundational discussion from al-Zarqa's work on the general theory of legal capacity (ahliyya). The author begins by explaining that the Lawgiver (Allah) determines the legal capacity of individuals. He equates 'legislative address' (khitab tashri'i) with 'legal ruling' (hukm shari'), covering both devotional acts and civil obligations. The key point in paragraph 4 is that capacity is graded; the definition uses 'legislative address' in an indefinite form to encompass even minimal capacity, such as that of the fetus. Then in Section Three, the author divides capacity into two fundamental types: capacity for obligations (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada'). This lesson focuses on the first type. For capacity for obligations, the definition involves suitability for having rights (ilzam) and duties (iltizam). The author gives examples: rights include receiving compensation for damages and having property transferred; duties include paying a price or providing maintenance. Importantly, the basis for this capacity is simply being human (al-sifa al-insaniyya), not age, intellect, or maturity. Thus even a fetus or an insane person has capacity for obligations, though it may be deficient (only rights, no duties) or complete (both rights and duties). The passage ends by mentioning that capacity for obligations has two elements, which likely refer to these two aspects.

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): The suitability of a person to have rights (ilzam) and duties (iltizam) imposed by the Lawgiver.
الخطاب التشريعي (al-khitab al-tashri'i): Synonymous with 'legal ruling' (hukm shari'), it is the address of the Lawgiver containing command or prohibition.
الإلزام والالتزام (al-ilzam wa al-iltizam): Ilzam: the imposition of rights for a person; iltizam: the imposition of duties upon a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Legal capacity is a graded attribute determined by the Lawgiver, fundamentally divided into capacity for obligations (based on humanity) and capacity for performance.

Capacity for Obligations: Meaning, Basis, and Types
Defining capacity for obligations and its basis in humanity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define capacity for obligations (ahliyyat al-wujub), identify its basis as the human attribute, and distinguish between complete and deficient capacity.

Core Idea
Capacity for obligations is based solely on being human; it is either complete (rights and duties) or deficient (only rights).

This passage delves into the first type of legal capacity. Al-Zarqa defines capacity for obligations as suitability for having rights (ilzam) and duties (iltizam). He stresses that its basis is the human attribute, independent of age or intellect. The passage then classifies it into complete and deficient, with examples.

Guided Path
1
Define capacity for obligations
Read the definition: suitability for ilzam (rights) and iltizam (duties). Note the examples given.
Examples: receiving compensation (right), paying price (duty).
2
Identify the basis (manat)
The text states the basis is the human attribute (al-sifa al-insaniyya), not age or intellect.
This means even fetus and insane have this capacity.
3
Distinguish complete vs deficient
Deficient: only rights (fetus). Complete: both rights and duties (non-discerning minor, insane).
Complete here refers to inclusion of both elements, not perfection.

Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. The rights of their individuals and groups through those rulings that the Lawgiver legislates for them. So to Him returns the estimation of the competence of each person for each of those rulings. Hence it came in the definition that: the Lawgiver determines it. - And what is meant by legislative address is the legal ruling; they are synonymous in the terminology of the science of Usul al-Fiqh. Whatever the Lawgiver legislates, whether from the category of religious acts of worship like prayer and fasting, or from the category of obligations and civil rights like the obligation to execute contracts, guarantee for damages, the establishment of ownership of properties when a cause of ownership exists, and the obligation of maintenance with its conditions between spouses and relatives, all that and similar examples are called: legal rulings, considering that the Lawgiver has ruled by them upon people and obligated their application among them. It is also called legislative address, considering that the Lawgiver, in His legislation, addresses people with these rulings as commander and prohibitor, and obliges them to implement and respect them (see: Usul al-Fiqh by al-Khudari, p. 21, first edition). 4 - And if a person's legal capacity has stages and degrees according to his completeness and soundness from physical and mental afflictions, as we referred to earlier, and in each stage he is capable of some rulings or responsibilities and not others, therefore we included in the definition of legal capacity in its general sense the word 'legislative address' in an indefinite form to include the lowest capacities for the least rulings, like the capacity of the fetus as we will see. The Third Section: Types of Legal Capacity. 6/59 - Indeed legal capacity in its explained general comprehensive sense is divided into two basic types: capacity for obligations (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada'). We will explain below these two types, as we also divide people's actions into types according to the connection of each type of their actions with one of the two types of capacity. Chapter (59): The Foundation of Legal Capacity, its Definition and Types. First: Capacity for Obligations. 7/59 - Capacity for obligations is: the person's suitability for imposition (ilzam) and commitment (iltizam). What is meant by imposition is the establishment of rights for him, such as his entitlement to the value of damaged property from his wealth from whoever damages it, and the transfer of ownership to him in what he buys or borrows, and the obligation of his maintenance upon others if he is poor and unable—his relatives. And what is meant by commitment is the establishment of rights upon him, such as his commitment to pay the price of what he buys, and the substitute for a loan from his wealth, and his commitment to maintain those whose maintenance is obligatory upon him among the poor and unable if he is wealthy, etc. So every capacity that the Lawgiver requires in a person for the emergence of a right for him or the imposition of an obligation upon him is a capacity for obligation. 8/59 - The Basis of Capacity for Obligations: This capacity for obligation in the view of Islamic law has as its basis the human attribute, and it has no connection to age, intellect, or maturity. Rather, every human being in any stage or condition, even the fetus and the insane, is considered to possess this capacity for obligations, but the capacity for obligations in him may be complete or deficient: A - If he is capable of the establishment of some rights only for him and is not capable of obligations being imposed upon him, then he has deficient capacity for obligation, like the fetus in its mother's womb before birth, as will be detailed. B - If he is capable beyond that of financial obligations being imposed upon him as well, then he has complete capacity for obligation, like the non-discerning minor and the insane, as will be detailed. 9/59 - Elements of Capacity for Obligations: Capacity for obligations has two elements:

Commentary
This passage is a foundational discussion from al-Zarqa's work on the general theory of legal capacity (ahliyya). The author begins by explaining that the Lawgiver (Allah) determines the legal capacity of individuals. He equates 'legislative address' (khitab tashri'i) with 'legal ruling' (hukm shari'), covering both devotional acts and civil obligations. The key point in paragraph 4 is that capacity is graded; the definition uses 'legislative address' in an indefinite form to encompass even minimal capacity, such as that of the fetus. Then in Section Three, the author divides capacity into two fundamental types: capacity for obligations (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada'). This lesson focuses on the first type. For capacity for obligations, the definition involves suitability for having rights (ilzam) and duties (iltizam). The author gives examples: rights include receiving compensation for damages and having property transferred; duties include paying a price or providing maintenance. Importantly, the basis for this capacity is simply being human (al-sifa al-insaniyya), not age, intellect, or maturity. Thus even a fetus or an insane person has capacity for obligations, though it may be deficient (only rights, no duties) or complete (both rights and duties). The passage ends by mentioning that capacity for obligations has two elements, which likely refer to these two aspects.

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): The suitability of a person to have rights (ilzam) and duties (iltizam) imposed by the Lawgiver.
الخطاب التشريعي (al-khitab al-tashri'i): Synonymous with 'legal ruling' (hukm shari'), it is the address of the Lawgiver containing command or prohibition.
الإلزام والالتزام (al-ilzam wa al-iltizam): Ilzam: the imposition of rights for a person; iltizam: the imposition of duties upon a person.

Ahlīyat al-Wujūb: Capacity for Rights and Obligations in Hanafi Jurisprudence
Defining ahlīyat al-wujūb and its types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between deficient and complete ahlīyat al-wujūb and explain why it is based on humanity alone, not intellect or age.

Core Idea
Ahlīyat al-wujūb is inherent to every human being, even the fetus, based solely on humanity—not age or intellect.

This passage introduces the foundational Hanafi concept of legal capacity (ahliyya). Before a mufti can assess any transaction or obligation, he must determine the capacity of the person involved. The text distinguishes two types: ahlīyat al-wujūb (capacity for rights and duties) and ahlīyat al-adā' (capacity to execute actions). We will focus on the first, which is universal to all humans.

Guided Path
1
Define ahlīyat al-wujūb
Locate the definition in paragraph 7/59. Note that it covers both having rights (imposition) and bearing obligations (commitment). Identify examples given for each.
Imposition = rights for him; commitment = obligations upon him.
2
Identify the criterion (manāt)
Read paragraph 8/59. The sole criterion is the quality of humanity (al-ṣifah al-insāniyyah). Age, intellect, and discernment are irrelevant. Confirm that even a fetus and insane person possess it.
This is a key Hanafi principle: humanity suffices for capacity for rights.
3
Differentiate deficient vs. complete
From paragraph 8/59, note two subcategories: deficient (only rights, no obligations, e.g., fetus) and complete (rights and obligations, e.g., non-discerning minor, insane). Understand the practical consequence: the fetus can inherit but owes no debts.
Complete = both rights and financial obligations.
4
Analyze the two elements of ahlīyat al-wujūb
Paragraph 9/59 breaks capacity into two elements: creditor element (qualifies person to have rights) and debtor element (qualifies person to have obligations). The second element completes the capacity and entails the dhimmah.
Deficient capacity lacks the debtor element.
5
Understand the concept of dhimmah
Paragraph 10/59 explains that the debtor element requires a notional repository called al-dhimmah, a legal vessel for debts and obligations. This is central to Hanafi legal theory and is used in phrases like 'so-and-so owes in his dhimmah'.
Dhimmah is similar to legal personality in Western law.

١٠/٥٩ - أهلية الوجوب، والذمة :
ان ذلك العنصر الثاني في أهلية الوجوب، وهو العنصر الذي يؤهل الشخص للمديونية، يستتبع وجود شيء آخر يقدّر شرعاً في شخصية الإنسان يسمى: «الذمة» (۱) .
وهذه الذمة هي كوعاء اعتباري يقدّر تكونه في الشخص لتثبت فيه الديون وسائر الالتزامات التي تترتب عليه لأنه متى أصبح الشخص قابلاً للمديونية احتيج إلى تقدير مقرّ اعتباري في شخصه لاستقرار الديون التي عليه فهذا المقر المقدَّر هو الذمة، وهو المقصود في مثل قولهم: إنَّ لفلان في ذمة فلان مبلغ كذا من المال .
وسنرى في الجزء التالي الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية تفصيل نظرية الذمة، والسبب التشريعي الداعي إلى تقديرها في الشخص، ومقارنتها بنظرية الذمة المالية في الفقة الأجنبي.
ثانياً : أهلية الأداء
١١/٥٩ - وأما أهلية الأداء فهي : صلاحية الشخص لممارسة الأعمال
التي يتوقف اعتبارها الشرعي على العقل .
(۱) الذمة : بكسر الذال المعجمة وتشديد الميم، جمعها ذمم.
Chapter (59): The Foundation of Capacity, Its Definition, and Its Types
First: Ahlīyat al-Wujūb (Capacity for Obligation)
7/59 - Ahlīyat al-Wujūb is: the suitability of a person for imposition and obligation. What is meant by imposition is the establishment of rights for him, such as his entitlement to the value of his damaged property from whoever damages it, and the transfer of ownership to him of what he buys or borrows, and the obligation of his maintenance upon others if he is a poor incapable person—his relatives. And what is meant by obligation is the establishment of rights upon him, such as his commitment to pay the price of what he buys, and the repayment of a loan from his wealth, and his commitment to maintain those whose maintenance is obligatory upon him from the poor and incapable if he is wealthy, etc.
So every capacity that the Lawgiver requires in a person for the purpose of a right arising for him or an obligation being imposed upon him is Ahlīyat al-Wujūb.
8/59 - The Criterion for Ahlīyat al-Wujūb:
This Ahlīyat al-Wujūb, in the view of Islamic law, its criterion is the quality of humanity, and it has no relation to age, intellect, or sound judgment. Rather, every human being in whatever stage or state, even the fetus and the insane, is considered to possess this Ahlīyat al-Wujūb, but Ahlīyat al-Wujūb in him may be complete or deficient:
a - If he is suitable only for the establishment of some rights for him and not suitable for the imposition of obligations upon him, then he has a deficient Ahlīyat al-Wujūb, like the fetus in its mother's womb before birth, as will be detailed later.
b - If he is suitable, beyond that, for the imposition of financial obligations upon him as well, then he has a complete Ahlīyat al-Wujūb, like the non-discerning minor and the insane, as will be detailed later.
9/59 - Elements of Ahlīyat al-Wujūb:
Ahlīyat al-Wujūb has two elements:
a - The first is the element that qualifies for debtorship—that is, imposition. It qualifies the person to be a creditor, such that rights are established for him, even if he is not suited for a right to be established against him. An example is the capacity of the fetus in its mother's womb, which is a deficient capacity as we explained.
b - The second is the element that qualifies for debtorship as well—that is, obligation. It qualifies the person to be a debtor as he is a creditor, so that rights can be established for him and against him by their legitimate causes. With this second element, the Ahlīyat al-Wujūb in the person becomes complete. An example is the capacity of a human after being born alive, as we will see.
10/59 - Ahlīyat al-Wujūb and the Dhimmah:
Indeed, that second element in Ahlīyat al-Wujūb, which is the element that qualifies the person for debtorship, entails the existence of another thing that is legally presumed in the human personality called: "al-Dhimmah" (the legal capacity/obligation-bearing entity) (1).
And this Dhimmah is like a notional vessel that is presumed to be formed in the person to establish therein debts and all obligations that are imposed upon him, because when a person becomes suitable for debtorship, there is a need to presume a notional repository in his personality for settling the debts that are upon him. So this presumed repository is the Dhimmah, and it is what is intended in statements like: "So-and-so has in the Dhimmah of so-and-so such-and-such an amount of money."
And we will see in the following third part of this fiqh series the detailed theory of the Dhimmah, the legislative reason for presuming it in the person, and its comparison with the theory of financial Dhimmah in foreign jurisprudence.
Second: Ahlīyat al-Adā' (Capacity for Execution)
11/59 - As for Ahlīyat al-Adā', it is: the suitability of the person to practice actions whose legal consideration depends on intellect.
(1) Al-Dhimmah: with a kasrah on the dhal (مُعجمة) and tashdid on the mim, its plural is dhimam.
Source pages p. 739

Commentary
This passage is a legal theory exposition on the concept of capacity (ahliyya) in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by defining Ahlīyat al-Wujūb (capacity for obligation) as the suitability of a person to have rights established for him (imposition) and obligations imposed upon him (commitment). Examples clarify each: imposition includes entitlement to damages, ownership transfer, and maintenance rights; commitment includes paying for purchases, repaying loans, and providing for dependents. The criterion for this capacity is merely being human, irrespective of age, intellect, or reason. Even a fetus and an insane person possess it, but it may be deficient (only rights, no obligations, as for the fetus) or complete (rights and obligations, as for a minor without discernment or the insane). The two elements of Ahlīyat al-Wujūb are: the qualification to be a creditor (rights for him) and the qualification to be a debtor (obligations upon him). The latter implies the existence of the 'Dhimmah', a notional receptacle for debts and obligations, analogous to the legal entity in foreign law. The passage then introduces Ahlīyat al-Adā' (capacity for execution), which is the suitability to perform actions whose legal effect depends on intellect. This sets the stage for further discussion on mental capacity and discerning minors. The footnote clarifies the pronunciation of 'Dhimmah'.

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب (ahlīyat al-wujūb): The suitability of a person to have rights established for him and/or obligations imposed upon him, based solely on humanity.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): A presumed notional repository in the human person for debts and obligations, entailed by the second element of ahlīyat al-wujūb.
أهلية الأداء (ahlīyat al-adā'): The suitability of a person to perform actions whose legal validity depends on intellect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following has a COMPLETE ahlīyat al-wujūb according to the passage?
A) A fetus in the womb
B) A non-discerning minor
C) A deceased person
D) An unconsenting adult

Key Takeaway
The criterion for capacity for obligation (ahlīyat al-wujūb) is simply being human; it is independent of intellect or age, and the dhimmah is the notional vessel for obligations that arises from the debtor element of this capacity.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

8 min
Types of Legal Capacity: Ahliyyat al-Wujub, Ahliyyat al-Ada', and Dhimmah
Definition and distinction of legal capacities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two components of ahliyyat al-wujub, define dhimmah, and distinguish ahliyyat al-wujub from ahliyyat al-ada' in terms of their basis and onset.

Core Idea
Legal capacity is twofold: capacity for rights (inherent) and capacity for acts (acquired).

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aamm by Mustafa al-Zarqa lays the foundational distinction between two types of legal capacity: ahliyyat al-wujub (capacity for rights and obligations) and ahliyyat al-ada' (capacity for performance). Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti to correctly assess the legal validity of acts performed by minors, the insane, and others with limited capacity. The author also introduces the crucial concept of dhimmah, the notional vessel that holds a person's debts and obligations.

Guided Path
1
Deconstruct ahliyyat al-wujub
Identify that it has two components: one qualifies a person to be a creditor (having rights) and one qualifies him to be a debtor (bearing obligations). Note that the first exists even in the fetus, but the second only appears after birth.
The creditor element is incomplete on its own; both are needed for full capacity for rights and obligations.
2
Define dhimmah
Understand dhimmah as a notional receptacle or vessel that the Lawgiver presumes in a person to hold debts and obligations. Once a person becomes capable of debtorship (second element of ahliyyat al-wujub), dhimmah is needed to settle obligations.
Dhimmah is often used in expressions like 'in his dhimmah' meaning he is liable.
3
Define ahliyyat al-ada'
This is the capacity to perform acts whose legal validity depends on reason and intention. It is not present at birth; it begins when a child reaches discernment (tamyiz) and becomes complete at puberty.
Acts of worship and civil transactions require this capacity.
4
Contrast the two capacities
Ahliyyat al-wujub is inherent in human existence and never absent (though it can be incomplete, as in a fetus). Ahliyyat al-ada' is absent before tamyiz and is limited until puberty. The first is about rights and obligations, the second about performing acts with legal effect.
A person may have full ahliyyat al-wujub but no ahliyyat al-ada' (e.g., a newborn).
5
Apply the distinction to examples
Consider a fetus: it has incomplete ahliyyat al-wujub (rights only, no obligations) and no ahliyyat al-ada'. A newborn: complete ahliyyat al-wujub (both creditor and debtor) but no ahliyyat al-ada'. A discerning minor: complete ahliyyat al-wujub and limited ahliyyat al-ada'.
These distinctions determine the validity of contracts, gifts, and worship by minors.

الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية
أ - أولهما العنصر المؤهّل للدائنيه، أي الإلزام. وهو يؤهل الشخص لأن يكون دائناً، بحيث تثبت الحقوق له، وإن كان غير صالح لأن يثبت عليه حق ما ومثاله أهلية الجنين في بطن أمه وهي أهلية ناقصة كما بينا . ب - وثانيهما: العنصر المؤهل للمديونية أيضاً، أي الالتزام وهو يؤهل الشخص لأن يكون مديناً كما يكون دائناً، فيمكن أن تثبت الحقوق له وعليه بأسبابها المشروعة وبهذا العنصر الثاني تكمل أهلية الوجوب في الشخص. ومثاله أهلية الإنسان بعد ولادته حياً كما سنرى.
١٠/٥٩ - أهلية الوجوب، والذمة ان ذلك العنصر الثاني في أهلية الوجوب، وهو العنصر الذي يؤهل الشخص للمديونية، يستتبع وجود شيء آخر يقدّر شرعاً في شخصية الإنسان يسمى: «الذمة» (۱) .
وهذه الذمة هي كوعاء اعتباري يقدّر تكونه في الشخص لتثبت فيه الديون وسائر الالتزامات التي تترتب عليه لأنه متى أصبح الشخص قابلاً للمديونية احتيج إلى تقدير مقرّ اعتباري في شخصه لاستقرار الديون التي عليه فهذا المقر المقدَّر هو الذمة، وهو المقصود في مثل قولهم: إنَّ لفلان في ذمة فلان مبلغ كذا من المال .
وسنرى في الجزء التالي الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية تفصيل نظرية الذمة، والسبب التشريعي الداعي إلى تقديرها في الشخص، ومقارنتها بنظرية الذمة المالية في الفقة الأجنبي.
ثانياً : أهلية الأداء
١١/٥٩ - وأما أهلية الأداء فهي : صلاحية الشخص لممارسة الأعمال التي يتوقف اعتبارها الشرعي على العقل .
(۱) الذمة : بكسر الذال المعجمة وتشديد الميم، جمعها ذمم.
٧٨٦
الفصل (٥٩) مبنى الأهلية وتعريفها وأنواعها
وقد قدمنا في بحث البطلان من نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية (ف ٣/٥١) أن الأفعال الحسية ذات الآثار الاعتبارية تتوقف آثارها ونتائجها الشرعية على وجودها الاعتباري في نظر الشارع ولا يكفي وجودها الحسّي لترتب هذه ذلك جميع التصرفات المدنية.
الآثار. ومن
فهذه الأفعال كلها سواء أكانت عبادات دينية كالصلاة والصيام، أو كانت تصرفات مدنية كالعقود لا يمكن إسباغ هذا الاعتبار عليها من الشارع مالم يكن لدى فاعلها من التعقل والإدراك نصيب يكون به قادراً على أن يفهم نتائجها إجمالاً، أي أن يتوافر لديه أدنى حد كاف من الفهم لتكون ممارسته لها عن قصد صحيح معتبر .
١٢/٥٩ - يتضح من ذلك أن أهلية الأداء هذه لا وجود لها في الطفل قبل أن يصير مميزاً قادراً على فهم الخطاب التشريعي إجمالاً، وعلى القيام ببعض الأعباء (ر) رسالة الأهلية وعوارضها للأستاذ الشيخ أحمد إبراهيم بك ص/٤).
وهذا على خلاف الحال في أهلية الوجوب فإنها لا يعتريها العدم في الشخص أصلاً، لما قدمنا من أن هذه الأهلية ملازمة للصفة الإنسانية في الوجود، حتى إنها لتثبت للجنين في بطن أمه وإن كانت فيه ناقصة. وذلك لأنها ليست أساساً لممارسة الأفعال وتحمل مسؤولياتها ونتائجها، بل هي أساس لثبوت الحقوق للأشخاص وعليهم . والحمل في بطن أمه قد اتفقت الشرائع الإلهية والوضعية على إثبات بعض الحقوق له. فهو إذن متمتع بأهلية وجوب ولو ناقصة كما تقدم.
أما أهلية الأداء فإنها أساس لممارسة الأعمال والتصرفات الشرعية كما أوضحنا . وهذه الأعمال والتصرفات تعتمد قصد الفاعل وإرادته، فلا بد في ممارستها من عنصر التمييز والتعقل.
فتبدأ أهلية الأداء في الإنسان متى أصبح مميزاً، ولكنها تكون فيه قاصرة كما سيأتي بيانه. ثم تتم بتمام قدرته جسماً وعقلاً، وذلك ببلوغه ثم
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. A) First: The element qualifying for creditorship, i.e., obligation. It qualifies a person to be a creditor, so that rights are established for him, even if he is not fit for a right to be established against him. Its example is the capacity of the fetus in its mother's womb, which is incomplete capacity as we explained. B) Second: The element qualifying for debtorship as well, i.e., commitment. It qualifies a person to be a debtor just as he is a creditor, so that rights can be established for him and against him by their lawful causes. With this second element, the capacity for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) is completed in a person. Its example is the capacity of a person after being born alive, as we will see. 10/59: Capacity for obligation and the dhimmah. That second element in capacity for obligation, which is the element that qualifies a person for debtorship, entails the existence of something else that is legally presumed in a person's personality called 'dhimmah' (1). This dhimmah is like a notional vessel presumed to exist in a person to establish debts and all other obligations that are incumbent upon him. Because once a person becomes capable of debtorship, it is necessary to presume a notional repository in his personality to settle the debts against him. This presumed repository is the dhimmah, and it is what is meant in statements like: 'So-and-so has such-and-such amount of money in the dhimmah of so-and-so.' We will see in the third part of this fiqh series the details of the theory of dhimmah, the legislative reason prompting its presumption in a person, and its comparison with the theory of financial liability in foreign jurisprudence. Secondly: Capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada'). 11/59: As for capacity for performance, it is: the person's suitability to perform acts whose legal validity depends on reason. (1) Dhimmah: with a kasra on the dhal, tashdid on the mim, plural dhimam. 786. Section (59) Basis of capacity, its definition, and its types. We have already presented in the discussion of nullity from the theory of legal supports (F 3/51) that physical acts with legal effects depend for their effects and legal consequences on their legal existence in the view of the Lawgiver, and their physical existence is not sufficient for these effects to follow. Of these are all civil transactions. All these acts, whether they are religious acts of worship like prayer and fasting, or civil transactions like contracts, cannot receive this legal recognition from the Lawgiver unless the doer has a share of intellect and comprehension enabling him to understand their consequences in general, i.e., he possesses a sufficient minimum of understanding so that his practice is based on sound and recognized intention. 12/59: It becomes clear from this that this capacity for performance does not exist in a child before he becomes discerning (mumayyiz) and able to understand legislative discourse in general, and to undertake some responsibilities (ref: Risalah al-Ahliyyah wa 'Awaridiha by Prof. Shaykh Ahmad Ibrahim Bek, p. 4). This is contrary to the case of capacity for obligation, for it is never absent from a person at all, because as we have said, this capacity is inherent to the human condition in existence, so much so that it is established for the fetus in its mother's womb, albeit incomplete. That is because it is not a basis for performing acts and bearing their responsibilities and consequences, but rather a basis for the establishment of rights for persons and against them. The fetus in its mother's womb, divine and positive legal systems have agreed on establishing certain rights for it. So it enjoys a capacity for obligation, even if incomplete, as mentioned. As for capacity for performance, it is the basis for performing legal acts and dispositions as we explained. These acts and dispositions depend on the doer's intention and will, so the element of discernment and reason is necessary in their performance. Thus, capacity for performance begins in a person when he becomes discerning, but it is limited in him as will be explained later. Then it is completed with his complete physical and mental ability, which occurs at his puberty, then...
Source pages p. 740

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aamm explains the foundational concepts of legal capacity (ahliyyah) in Hanafi jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, breaks down ahliyyat al-wujub (capacity for rights and obligations) into two elements: one that qualifies a person to be a creditor (having rights) and another that qualifies him to be a debtor (bearing obligations). The first element is present even in the fetus, making him capable of having rights but not obligations. The second element appears after birth, completing the capacity for obligation. The author then introduces the concept of dhimmah, a notional receptacle in a person wherein debts and obligations reside. This is a key term in Hanafi fiqh: when we say 'so-and-so owes so much in his dhimmah,' it means the debt is established as a liability on his person. The passage then transitions to ahliyyat al-ada' (capacity for performance), which is the ability to perform acts that the Lawgiver considers valid based on reason. Unlike ahliyyat al-wujub, which is inherent to human existence and never absent (even for the fetus), ahliyyat al-ada' begins only when a child attains discernment (tamyiz) and can understand legal discourse in general. Even then, it is limited until puberty, when it becomes complete. The author emphasizes that acts of worship and civil transactions require the doer to have sufficient understanding and intention for their legal validity. This distinction is crucial for Hanafi muftis when determining the validity of contracts, acts of worship, and other legal acts performed by minors or those with mental incapacity.

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): Capacity for rights and obligations; the suitability of a person to have rights established for him and obligations upon him.
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada'): Capacity for performance; the suitability of a person to perform acts whose legal validity depends on reason.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): A notional vessel in a person's personality that holds debts and obligations; the legal subject of obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa, when does ahliyyat al-ada' begin?
A) At birth.
B) At puberty.
C) At discernment (tamyiz).
D) At the age of 15.

Key Takeaway
Ahliyyat al-wujub is inherent and never absent; ahliyyat al-ada' begins at tamyiz and requires reason and intention.

Basis, Definition and Types of Legal Capacity
Defining legal capacity and distinguishing its two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between capacity of obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada) and explain their basis in Hanafi legal theory.

Core Idea
Capacity of obligation is inherent in every human; capacity of performance requires discernment and is the basis for valid legal acts.

Legal capacity in Islamic law is not monolithic. The Sharī‘ah distinguishes between the capacity to hold rights and duties (obligation capacity) and the capacity to perform acts that produce legal effects (performance capacity). This lesson unpacks that distinction using the Hanafi framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of capacity
Scan the passage for the two terms: 'ahliyyat al-wujub' and 'ahliyyat al-ada'. Note that the author contrasts their foundations.
The passage explicitly compares them: capacity of obligation is never absent, while performance capacity depends on discernment.
2
Define each capacity from the text
Extract the definition: obligation capacity is the basis for rights and duties; performance capacity is the basis for performing valid legal acts.
Capacity of obligation attaches to every human being, even a fetus. Performance capacity requires understanding and intent.
3
Identify the legal basis (dalīl) for each
Obligation capacity is derived from personhood (al-ṣifa al-insāniyya). Performance capacity is justified by the need for qaṣd (intent) and tamyīz (discernment) for acts to have legal effect.
The author uses a talīl (reasoning) approach: acts require legal existence, which depends on the doer's comprehension.
4
Apply to examples – fetus and child
A fetus has deficient obligation capacity (e.g., inheritance rights). A non-discerning child has no performance capacity. A discerning child has deficient performance capacity.
The text states: fetus has obligation capacity; child before tamyīz has no performance capacity.
5
Determine practical consequences
Obligation capacity triggers rights like inheritance and debts. Performance capacity determines validity of contracts, worship, and other legal acts.
A mufti uses this to rule on a minor's transactions: a discerning minor may make valid gifts but contracts require guardian approval.
