الفصل (٥٩) مبنى الأهلية وتعريفها وأنواعها
وقد قدمنا في بحث البطلان من نظرية المؤيدات الشرعية (ف ٣/٥١) أن الأفعال الحسية ذات الآثار الاعتبارية تتوقف آثارها ونتائجها الشرعية على وجودها الاعتباري في نظر الشارع ولا يكفي وجودها الحسّي لترتب هذه الآثار. ومن هذه جميع التصرفات المدنية.
فهذه الأفعال كلها سواء أكانت عبادات دينية كالصلاة والصيام، أو كانت تصرفات مدنية كالعقود لا يمكن إسباغ هذا الاعتبار عليها من الشارع مالم يكن لدى فاعلها من التعقل والإدراك نصيب يكون به قادراً على أن يفهم نتائجها إجمالاً، أي أن يتوافر لديه أدنى حد كاف من الفهم لتكون ممارسته لها عن قصد صحيح معتبر .
يتضح من ذلك أن أهلية الأداء هذه لا وجود لها في الطفل قبل أن يصير مميزاً قادراً على فهم الخطاب التشريعي إجمالاً، وعلى القيام ببعض الأعباء.
وهذا على خلاف الحال في أهلية الوجوب فإنها لا يعتريها العدم في الشخص أصلاً، لما قدمنا من أن هذه الأهلية ملازمة للصفة الإنسانية في الوجود، حتى إنها لتثبت للجنين في بطن أمه وإن كانت فيه ناقصة. وذلك لأنها ليست أساساً لممارسة الأفعال وتحمل مسؤولياتها ونتائجها، بل هي أساس لثبوت الحقوق للأشخاص وعليهم. والحمل في بطن أمه قد اتفقت الشرائع الإلهية والوضعية على إثبات بعض الحقوق له. فهو إذن متمتع بأهلية وجوب ولو ناقصة كما تقدم.
أما أهلية الأداء فإنها أساس لممارسة الأعمال والتصرفات الشرعية كما أوضحنا. وهذه الأعمال والتصرفات تعتمد قصد الفاعل وإرادته، فلا بد في ممارستها من عنصر التمييز والتعقل.
فتبدأ أهلية الأداء في الإنسان متى أصبح مميزاً، ولكنها تكون فيه قاصرة كما سيأتي بيانه. ثم تتم بتمام قدرته جسماً وعقلاً، وذلك ببلوغه ثم رشده، فيحمل عندئذ جميع التكاليف الشرعية ويمارس جميع الحقوق.
Chapter (59): Basis of Legal Capacity, Its Definition and Types. We have previously presented in the discussion of invalidity from the theory of legal sanctions (section 3/51) that physical actions having legal effects depend for their effects and legal consequences on their legal existence in the view of the Lawgiver, and their physical existence is not sufficient for these effects to attach. From these are all civil transactions. Thus all these actions, whether they are religious acts of worship like prayer and fasting, or civil transactions like contracts, cannot receive this attribution from the Lawgiver unless the doer has a share of intellect and comprehension enabling him to understand their consequences in general, i.e., that he possesses a sufficient minimum of understanding so that his practice of them is based on a correct and considered intent. From this it becomes clear that this capacity of performance does not exist in a child before he becomes discerning (mumayyiz), capable of understanding the legislative address in general, and of undertaking some obligations. This is contrary to the case of capacity of obligation, for it is never absent from a person at all, because we have stated that this capacity is inseparable from the human quality in existence, so much so that it is established for a fetus in its mother's womb, although it is deficient in it. This is because it is not a basis for performing actions and bearing their responsibilities and consequences, but rather a basis for the establishment of rights for and against persons. The fetus in its mother's womb has had some rights established for it by divine and positive laws. It thus possesses a capacity of obligation, albeit deficient, as previously mentioned. As for capacity of performance, it is the basis for performing legal acts and transactions, as we have explained. These acts and transactions depend on the doer's intent and will, so the element of discernment and intellect is necessary in performing them. Thus capacity of performance begins in a person when he becomes discerning, but it is deficient in him as will be explained later. Then it becomes complete with his complete physical and mental ability, which occurs with his puberty and then his sound judgment (rushd). At that time he bears all legal obligations and exercises all rights.
Source pages p. 741

Commentary
This passage is a foundational legal philosophy discussion on the basis, definition, and types of legal capacity (ahliyyah) in Islamic jurisprudence. The author begins by linking legal capacity to the concept of legal existence of actions: physical acts have no legal effect unless the Lawgiver attributes legal significance to them, and this attribution requires the doer to have a minimum level of intellect and comprehension. This principle applies to both acts of worship and civil transactions. The passage then distinguishes between two fundamental types of capacity: capacity of obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada). Capacity of obligation is inherent in every human being from conception, even in the fetus, because it is the basis for rights and obligations without requiring action. Capacity of performance, however, is the basis for validly performing legal acts and depends on the doer's discernment (tamyiz) and intent. It begins with discernment (generally around age 7 in Hanafi fiqh) but is initially deficient, becoming fully complete only with maturity (bulugh) and sound judgment (rushd). The passage emphasizes that the Lawgiver's recognition of legal effects requires a combination of physical existence of the act and the mental capacity of the agent.

Key Terms
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada): The capacity to legally perform actions and transactions validly, requiring discernment and intent.
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): The capacity to acquire rights and bear obligations, inherent in every human being.
تمييز (tamyiz): Discernment: the mental stage where a child understands the general nature of legal address.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada) begin in a human being?
A) At birth
B) At the moment of conception
C) When the person becomes discerning (mumayyiz)
D) Only at the age of majority (18)

Key Takeaway
Legal capacity in Hanafi fiqh has two dimensions: obligation capacity (inherent in every human) and performance capacity (acquired with discernment and perfected at puberty and sound judgment).

Classification of Actions by Legal Capacity
Classification of actions into two types based on capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify actions into those requiring only capacity of obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) and those requiring capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada), and apply this classification to determine legal effects for various actors.

Core Idea
Some legal effects follow mere physical acts; others require intent and comprehension.

This passage introduces a foundational classification in Hanafi legal theory: actions are divided based on the type of legal capacity they require. Understanding this division is essential for determining liability for harm, the validity of contracts, and the legal status of acts by minors or the insane. The author explains that some actions, like causing damage, trigger legal consequences regardless of the doer's mental state, while others, like contracts, depend on the doer having intellect and comprehension. This distinction shapes many practical rulings in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the first part of the passage to note the two types of actions: those not requiring intellect (aql) and those requiring intellect and understanding. The first type connects effects to acts materially; the second connects effects to intentions.
The key Arabic terms are 'أفعال لا يشترط فيها العقل' and 'أفعال يشترط فيها العقل والتفهم'.
2
Understand the capacity basis
For the first type, only capacity of obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) is needed, which is present from birth. For the second, capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada) is required, which depends on discernment and maturity.
Capacity of obligation allows bearing financial consequences; capacity of performance allows valid legal acts.
3
Apply to examples
Consider the given examples: an insane person or child damaging property is liable (first type). A non-discerning child's contracts and transactions are invalid (second type).
The footnote clarifies that civil transactions (تصرفات مدنية) include both creating and executing obligations.
4
Distinguish the two types in practice
Practice identifying which type a given action belongs to. For instance, theft involves intent, so it might require capacity of performance; but liability for accidental damage does not.
Intent matters for criminal liability but not necessarily for civil liability for damage.

ثالثاً : تقسيم الأفعال بالنسبة إلى الأهليات
بناء على ما تقدّم تنقسم الأفعال التي تصدر عن الإنسان وتترتب عليها آثار ونتائج شرعية من حيث علاقتها بالأهلية إلى نوعين :
١ - أفعال لا يشترط في فاعلها العقل، بل يرتبط فيها الأثر بمجرد الفعل ارتباطاً مادياً محضاً كارتباط المسببات بأسبابها الطبيعية. وذلك كالفعل الضار من حيث سببيته الشرعية لضمان الضرر الحاصل به .
وأمثال هذه الأفعال تعتمد في الفاعل أهلية الوجوب فقط لتحمل نتائجها المالية .
وهذه الأهلية تصير كاملة في الإنسان منذ ولادته، وهو بها صالح للالتزامات عند تحقق أسبابها كما تقدم.
فلو أتلف المجنون أو الطفل غير المميز مالاً لغيره، أو أحدث فيه عيباً فإنه يضمنه أي يصبح ملتزماً بتعويض ما أتلف أو عيب.
٢ - وأفعال يشترط في فاعلها العقل والتفهم كي يعتد بها وتترتب عليها آثارها ونتائجها الشرعية، لأنّ لنتائجها ارتباطاً بالمقاصد والإرادات وواضح أنه لا اعتبار للإرادة والقصد بلا عقل وإدراك . وأمثال هذه الأفعال جميعاً تعتمد أهلية الأداء في الفاعل لكي يعتد بها، من حيث أن لنتائجها ذلك الارتباط بالمقاصد والعقل.
فمن هذا القبيل جميع العقود، وسائر التصرفات المدنية من قولية وفعلية كتسلم المبيع أو قبض الثمن، فإنهما لا يصحان من الطفل غير المميز وكإحراز المباحات العامة، فإن الطفل غير المميز لا يملك ما يقع.
(١) المراد بالتصرفات المدنية التصرفات التي شرعت إما لإنشاء حق أو التزام كما في العقود وإحراز المباحات؛ وإما لتنفيذ التزام كما في تسليم المبيع وقبض الثمن.
Third: Division of Actions with Respect to Legal Capacities. Based on what has preceded, actions that issue from a person and entail legal effects and consequences are divided, with respect to their relation to capacity, into two types: 1 - Actions for which the doer's intellect is not a condition, but rather the effect is tied to the mere act through a purely material connection, like the connection of effects to their natural causes. This includes harmful acts in terms of their legal causality for liability for the resulting harm. Such actions require in the doer only the capacity of obligation to bear their financial consequences. This capacity becomes complete in a person from his birth, and he is thereby capable of incurring obligations when their causes are realized, as previously mentioned. So if an insane person or a non-discerning child destroys someone else's property, or causes a defect in it, he is liable for it, i.e., he becomes obliged to compensate for what he destroyed or the defect. 2 - Actions that require the doer's intellect and understanding in order to be considered and for their legal effects and consequences to follow, because their consequences are connected to intentions and wills, and it is clear that will and intent are not considered without intellect and comprehension. All such actions depend on the capacity of performance in the doer in order to be considered, because their consequences have this connection to intentions and intellect. Examples include all contracts and other civil transactions, whether verbal or physical, like receiving the sold item or grasping the price, for they are not valid from a non-discerning child, and also like appropriation of public mubahat, for a non-discerning child does not own what falls into his hand. (1) By 'civil transactions' is meant transactions legislated either to create a right or obligation, as in contracts and appropriation of mubahat, or to execute an obligation, as in delivery of the sold item and taking possession of the price.
Source pages p. 742

Commentary
This passage is a classification of actions in relation to legal capacity (ahliyyah). The author divides actions into two types based on the kind of capacity required for legal effects. The first type does not require the doer to have intellect (aql) or discernment; the legal effect follows from the physical act itself, like causality in nature. For these actions, only capacity of obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) is necessary, which every human possesses from birth. The example given is liability for damage: if an insane person or a non-discerning child destroys property, they are liable to compensate because the act itself caused harm. This ruling reflects the Hanafi principle that liability for torts (damages) does not require intent. The second type requires the doer to have intellect and understanding, because the effects depend on intentions and will. These actions require capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada). Examples include all contracts, delivery of sold items, and appropriation of ownerless objects (mubahat). Such acts are not valid from a non-discerning child. The footnote clarifies the meaning of 'civil transactions' (تصرفات مدنية) as transactions that create rights/obligations (like contracts) or execute existing obligations (like delivery of goods).

Key Terms
أفعال لا يشترط فيها العقل (af'al la yushtarat fiha al-aql): Actions whose legal effect follows from the mere physical act, regardless of the doer's mental state, like torts causing damage.
أفعال يشترط فيها العقل والتفهم (af'al yushtarat fiha al-aql wa al-tafahhum): Actions that require the doer to have intellect and comprehension for their effects to follow, as they depend on intention.
تصرفات مدنية (tasarrufat madaniyyah): Legal transactions that either create rights/obligations (e.g., contracts) or execute existing obligations (e.g., delivery).
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): The capacity to bear rights and obligations, present from birth.
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada): The capacity to perform valid legal acts, requiring intellect and discernment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions requires the doer to have capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada)?
A) A sleepwalker accidentally breaking a vase.
B) An insane person destroying someone's property.
C) A non-discerning child receiving a gift.
D) A sane adult selling his car.

Key Takeaway
Actions in Islamic law are classified by capacity: some require only capacity of obligation (tort liability), others require capacity of performance (contracts and transactions).

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
8 min
Actions Based on Capacity: Material vs. Intent-Required
Explains two types of actions based on required legal capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actions requiring only capacity of obligation and those requiring capacity of performance, and apply this distinction to concrete cases involving minors and the insane.

Core Idea
Tort liability attaches to the act itself; worship requires intent.

In Islamic legal theory, not all actions depend on the doer's mental state. Some legal effects follow purely from the physical act, while others require conscious intention. This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm lays out the foundational division based on two types of legal capacity: Ahlīyat al-Wujūb (capacity of obligation) and Ahlīyat al-Adā' (capacity of performance). Understanding this taxonomy is essential for a mufti to correctly assign rulings in cases involving minors, the insane, and those with diminished capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The text divides actions into those where the legal effect is linked to the mere physical act (material connection) and those where the effect depends on the doer's reason and understanding (intent-based).
Material acts: e.g., causing harm. Intent-based acts: e.g., contracts, worship.
2
Specify the required capacity for each
Material acts require only Ahlīyat al-Wujūb (capacity of obligation) which exists from birth. Intent-based acts require Ahlīyat al-Adā' (capacity of performance) which depends on reason and discernment.
Ahlīyat al-Wujūb is complete at birth; Ahlīyat al-Adā' is complete only at maturity.
3
Apply the classification to examples
Consider a madman destroying property: he is liable despite lack of intent (material act). A non-discerning child's contract is invalid because it requires capacity of performance.
The text gives explicit examples: madman/non-discerning child for material; contracts/worship for intent-based.
4
Recognize the underlying rationale
The difference hinges on whether the legal effect is tied to the act itself (causal connection) or to the doer's intention. Material acts are like natural causes; intent-based acts require a conscious agent.
This rationale is expressed by the author as 'ارتباطاً بالمقاصد والإرادات'.

Source Text

وأمثال هذه الأفعال تعتمد في الفاعل أهلية الوجوب فقط لتحمل نتائجها المالية .
وهذه الأهلية تصير كاملة في الإنسان منذ ولادته، وهو بها صالح للالتزامات عند تحقق أسبابها كما تقدم.

۲ - وأفعال يشترط في فاعلها العقل والتفهم كي يعتد بها وتترتب عليها آثارها ونتائجها الشرعية، لأنّ لنتائجها ارتباطاً بالمقاصد والإرادات وواضح أنه لا اعتبار للإرادة والقصد بلا عقل وإدراك . وأمثال هذه الأفعال جميعاً تعتمد أهلية الأداء في الفاعل لكي يعتد بها، من حيث أن لنتائجها ذلك الارتباط بالمقاصد والعقل.
فمن هذا القبيل جميع العقود، وسائر التصرفات المدنية (١) من قولية وفعلية كتسلم المبيع أو قبض الثمن، فإنهما لا يصحان من الطفل غير المميز وكإحراز المباحات العامة، فإن الطفل غير المميز لا يملك ما يقع في يده منها(۱)، إلخ.

ب - ومنها ما يكفي فيه أهلية أداء قاصرة بأن يكون الفاعل مميزاً، أي واعياً كما في العبادات الدينية وبعض العقود والتصرفات المدنية؛ فإذا فعلها الصغير المميز تصح منه .

(۱) بخلاف الصغير المميز، فإنه يملك ما يحرزه من المباحات عن قصد كالكبير الراشد ر الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم في أحكام الصبيان ١٤٥/٢).
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. His maturity, then he bears all legal obligations and exercises all rights. Third: Division of actions in relation to capacities.
13/59 - Based on what preceded, actions emanating from humans upon which legal effects and consequences depend, in terms of their relation to capacity, are divided into two types:
1- Actions in which the doer's reason is not required, but the effect is linked to the mere act in a purely material connection, like the connection of effects to their natural causes. For example, the harmful act in terms of its legal causality for liability for the resulting harm.
Such actions depend on the doer having capacity of obligation only, to bear their financial consequences.
This capacity becomes complete in a human from his birth, and by it he is fit for obligations when their causes occur, as discussed.
So if a madman or a non-discerning child destroys another's property, or causes a defect in it, he is liable for it, i.e., he becomes obligated to compensate what he destroyed or damaged.
2- Actions in which the doer's reason and understanding are required so that they are considered valid and their effects and legal consequences follow, because their consequences connect to intentions and wills, and it is clear that will and intent have no consideration without reason and perception. Such actions all depend on capacity of performance in the doer to be considered valid, since their consequences have that connection to intentions and reason.
Of this kind are all contracts and all other legal dispositions (1) whether verbal or physical, like taking delivery of the sold item or receiving the price, for they are not valid from a non-discerning child; and like acquisition of public permissible things, for a non-discerning child does not own what falls into his hands from them (1), etc.
And others.
Also of this kind are all religious acts of worship: prayer, fasting, and pilgrimage.
Then these actions, in terms of the degree of capacity required for them, are of two types:
a- Some require complete capacity of performance, i.e., the doer must be adult and mature, such as financial donations like gift, endowment, and similar, because they are pure financial loss for the doer, so for validity of his performing them, he must enjoy his complete capacity, as a precaution to safeguard his wealth and rights.
b- Some suffice with deficient capacity of performance, i.e., the doer is discerning (aware), as in religious worship and some contracts and legal dispositions; so if a discerning minor does them, they are valid from him.
Detail of that in the following sections 60-64.
(1) Contrary to the discerning minor, for he owns what he acquires of permissible things intentionally, like the adult mature [scholar]. Refer to al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir by Ibn Nujaym, ruling on minors, 2/145.
Source pages p. 742

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm is a theoretical exposition of legal capacity (ahlīyah) in Islamic law. It begins by dividing actions into two categories based on the type of capacity required. The first category comprises actions where the legal effect attaches to the mere physical act, irrespective of the doer's mental state. The example given is causing harm: if a madman or a non-discerning child destroys property, they are liable for compensation. This is because liability here is based on the act itself, not on intent, and requires only capacity of obligation (Ahlīyat al-Wujūb) which every human possesses from birth. The second category consists of actions that require intention and understanding, such as contracts, legal dispositions, and acts of worship. These require capacity of performance (Ahlīyat al-Adā'), which depends on reason and discretion. The passage then further subdivides these actions: some need complete capacity of performance (i.e., adulthood and maturity) like donations that are purely lossy, while others only need deficient capacity (discernment) like worship and certain contracts. The author references Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir for the rule that a discerning minor owns what he intentionally acquires from public property. The reasoning here is based on the connection between legal effects and human intent, a foundational concept in Hanafi legal theory.

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب (Ahlīyat al-Wujūb): The legal capacity to have rights and obligations, present from birth, enabling liability for torts and debts.
أهلية الأداء (Ahlīyat al-Adā'): The capacity to perform legally valid acts, requiring reason and intention; its complete form is at maturity, deficient form at discernment.
مميز (Mumayyiz): A child who has reached an age of understanding and can discriminate between beneficial and harmful acts, but is not yet adult.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a madman liable for damage to another's property despite lacking intent?
A) Because he has capacity of performance.
B) Because liability attaches to the material act, which requires only capacity of obligation.
C) Because Islamic law presumes intent in all actions.
D) Because his guardian is responsible.

Key Takeaway
Actions divide into material (requiring only capacity of obligation) and intent-based (requiring capacity of performance); this distinction determines liability and validity.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner

Degrees of Capacity of Performance: Complete vs. Deficient
Explains subdivision of performance-based actions by degree of capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between actions requiring complete capacity of performance and those requiring only deficient capacity, and apply this to categorize financial donations versus worship.

Core Idea
Donations require full maturity; worship requires only discernment.

The previous division established that some actions require capacity of performance. But not all such actions demand the same degree. This passage further classifies performance-based acts: some require complete capacity (adulthood and maturity) like gifts and endowments, while others suffice with deficient capacity (discernment) like prayer, fasting, and certain contracts. The mufti must consult this hierarchy to determine the validity of acts by minors.

Guided Path
1
Recall the second category
Actions that require capacity of performance are subdivided into two subcategories based on the degree of capacity needed.
This is the 'ثم إن هذه الأفعال' paragraph.
2
Identify the two subcategories
Complete capacity of performance (كمال أهلية الأداء) is needed for acts that are pure financial loss (tabarru'at). Deficient capacity (أهلية أداء قاصرة) suffices for acts of worship and some contracts.
Complete capacity requires the doer to be adult (بالغ) and mature (راشد).
3
Note the examples given
Complete capacity: gift (هبة), endowment (وقف). Deficient capacity: prayer, fasting, pilgrimage, and some legal dispositions.
The text specifies that donating is a 'خسارة مالية محضة'.
4
Understand the rationale
Donations are detrimental to the doer's wealth, so full capacity is needed as a precaution (احتياطاً). Worship is beneficial and requires only awareness.
The principle of ihtiyat (precaution) in safeguarding wealth is invoked.

Commentary

It begins by dividing actions into two categories based on the type of capacity required. The reasoning here is based on the connection between legal effects and human intent, a foundational concept in Hanafi legal theory.

Key Terms
أهلية الأداء (Ahlīyat al-Adā'): The capacity to perform legally valid acts, requiring reason and intention; its complete form is at maturity, deficient form at discernment.
مميز (Mumayyiz): A child who has reached an age of understanding and can discriminate between beneficial and harmful acts, but is not yet adult.
بالغ راشد (Bāligh Rāshid): An adult who has reached legal majority and is mature in financial matters; required for valid donations.

Checkpoint
Why does a gift require complete capacity of performance rather than deficient?
A) Because it is an act of worship.
B) Because it is a pure financial loss for the doer, requiring precaution.
C) Because gifts are contracts and all contracts require complete capacity.
D) Because only adults can own property.

Key Takeaway
Within actions requiring capacity of performance, donations need complete capacity (adulthood), while worship and some contracts require only deficient capacity (discernment).

Stages of Legal Capacity: Milestones Not Gradual Change
Introduction to the five stages and the milestone principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five stages of human development that determine legal capacity in Hanafi fiqh and explain why capacity changes only at these milestones.

Core Idea
Legal capacity does not grow gradually; it jumps at five fixed milestones: fetus, childhood, discernment, puberty, and rashd.

This passage lays the foundation for understanding legal personality in Hanafi fiqh. The author introduces the concept that capacity (ahliyyah) passes through distinct phases, each with its own legal implications. Importantly, he explains why Shari'ah does not recognize every minor increment in maturity—only the major milestones matter. This insight is essential for analyzing issues of guardianship, contracts by minors, and criminal responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five stages
List the five stages from the text: fetus (ijtinan), childhood (tufulah), discernment (tamyeez), puberty (bulugh), and rational maturity (rushd).
These stages correspond to specific ages in Hanafi fiqh (e.g., tamyeez typically at 7, bulugh at 15 or physical signs).
2
Understand the two types of ahliyyah
Recognize that the passage mentions deficient capacity for rights (ahliyyat wujub naqisah) at the beginning and complete capacity for performance (ahliyyat ada kamilah) at the end. Each stage maps to a level of ahliyyah.
The legal definition dissector below elaborates on ahliyyat wujub naqisah.
3
Recognize the milestone principle
Notice that capacity does not increase gradually month by month; it only changes at the five major milestones. The author explicitly states that minor differences are not legally considered.
The rationale is to avoid disruption in transactions (mu'amalat).
4
Analyze the rationale
The author argues that if capacity changed too rapidly, it would disrupt transactions. Stability in legal dealings requires clear, identifiable thresholds.
This is a practical consideration rooted in maslahah (public interest).
5
Apply the principle
Consider a hypothetical: a 6-year-old with exceptional intelligence and a 10-year-old with average intelligence. According to this passage, both are still in the childhood phase (before tamyeez), so they have the same deficient capacity for rights only.
This illustrates that capacity is based on fixed stages, not individual intellectual variation.

مراحل أهلية الإنسان بحسب أطوار حياته

١/٦٠ - يتبين مما أسلفنا بيانه أنَّ لأهلية الإنسان مراحل تمر بها في طريقها إلى التكامل بحسب أطوار حياته : منذ اجتنانه (۱) حتى احتلامه فرشده. فالأهلية ترافق حياته كلها وتمر في التكامل بأطوار تساير أطواره فتبدأ أهلية وجوب ناقصة، ثم تنتهي إلى أهلية أداء كاملة لأن مراحل الأهلية مستمدة أولاً من إنسانيته وما يتبعها من حقوق ثم من مواهبه العقلية وما تورثه من استعدادات : لكن يلحظ في هذا المقام أن الإنسان إذا كانت ملكاته العقلية ومواهبه تزداد مع نمو جسمه سنة فسنة وشهراً فشهراً، فإنّ أهليته التي يمنحه إياها الشرع لا تتسع دائرتها كذلك سنة فسنة وشهراً فشهراً، وإنما تتسع وتنتقل من طور إلى طور في المراحل والمحطات الأساسية من أطوار حياة الإنسان. ذلك لأن التنقل والتبدل السريع في الأهلية وخصائصها ليس مستحسناً لأنه تضطرب به المعاملات ،فوجب بالنسبة إلى الأهليات، أن لا يعتد بالفوارق اليسيرة في التكامل المستمر الذي تسير فيه مواهب الإنسان الناشيء، وأن لا تنتقل أهليته من حد إلى حد مختلف عنه في الخصائص إلا في المحطات الرئيسية من أطوار نشأته.
٢/٦٠ - ومن ثم تقسم نشأة الإنسان إلى خمسة أطوار أساسية : طور الاجتنان وطور الطفولة وطور التمييز، وطور البلوغ، وطور الرشد.
ومراحل التكامل في أهلية الإنسان توزع على هذه الأطوار،
دلائل أحكام الشريعة .
(۱) أي تكوينه جنيناً في بطن أمه (ر : القاموس المحيط، مادة «عقل»).
Book Eight: The theory of capacity and guardianship.
Stages of the capacity of a human according to the phases of his life.
A preliminary to the following chapters 60-64.
1/60 - It becomes clear from what we have previously explained that the capacity of a human has stages through which it passes on its way to completion according to the phases of his life: from his being a fetus (1) until his puberty and his rational maturity. So capacity accompanies his entire life and passes in completion through phases that parallel his phases. It begins as deficient capacity for rights (ahliyyat wujub naqisah), then ends with complete capacity for performance (ahliyyat ada kamilah). Because the stages of capacity are derived firstly from his humanity and the rights that follow from it, and then from his intellectual faculties and the dispositions they bequeath. But it is noted in this regard that if a person's intellectual faculties and talents increase with the growth of his body year by year and month by month, his capacity that the Shari'ah grants him does not expand its scope likewise year by year and month by month; rather, it expands and moves from one phase to another in the major stages and milestones of the phases of a human's life. That is because the rapid transition and change in capacity and its characteristics is not considered good because it would disrupt transactions. Therefore, it is necessary with respect to capacities that minor differences in the continuous development that the talents of a growing human undergo are not considered, and that his capacity does not move from one limit to another different from it in characteristics except at the major milestones of the stages of his growth.
2/60 - Consequently, the growth of a human is divided into five basic phases: the phase of being a fetus, the phase of childhood, the phase of discernment (tamyeez), the phase of puberty, and the phase of rational maturity (rushd). And the stages of completion in human capacity are distributed over these phases.
Evidence of the rulings of the Shari'ah.
(1) i.e., his formation as a fetus in his mother's womb (see: al-Qamus al-Muhit, entry 'aql).
Source pages p. 744

Commentary
This passage is a foundational exposition of the theory of legal capacity (ahliyyah) in Hanafi fiqh. The author explains that capacity goes through stages parallel to human development, starting from deficient capacity for rights (ahliyyat wujub naqisah) as a fetus, and culminating in complete capacity for performance (ahliyyat ada kamilah) after puberty and rational maturity. Importantly, the author emphasizes that capacity does not increase gradually like physical growth; instead, Shari'ah designates specific milestones (fetus, childhood, discernment, puberty, rashd) at which the legal status changes. The reason given is that if capacity changed too frequently, it would disrupt transactions (mu'amalat). Therefore, minor increments in maturity are not legally recognized until a major milestone is reached. The five stages are listed: 1) fetus (ijtinan), 2) childhood (tufulah), 3) discernment (tamyeez), 4) puberty (bulugh), 5) rational maturity (rushd). Each stage corresponds to a particular level of ahliyyah. This framework is crucial for understanding issues such as the validity of contracts by minors, guardianship, and criminal responsibility.

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب الناقصة (ahliyyat al-wujub al-naqisah): The initial stage of capacity for rights, established from the moment of conception, but incomplete because the individual cannot exercise those rights independently.
أهلية الأداء الكاملة (ahliyyat al-ada al-kamilah): The full capacity to perform legal acts, attained at puberty (bulugh) with rational maturity (rushd).
مراحل الأهلية (marahil al-ahliyyah): The distinct stages of legal capacity corresponding to the five major life phases: fetus, childhood, discernment, puberty, and rashd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does legal capacity not change gradually with every month of growth?
A) Because it would be too difficult to determine
B) Because it would disrupt transactions
C) Because the Shari'ah has no evidence for gradualism
D) Because minors cannot handle complexity

Key Takeaway
Legal capacity in Hanafi fiqh is determined by fixed life milestones, not by gradual intellectual growth, to ensure stability in transactions.

The Fetal Stage of Legal Capacity: Incomplete Obligatory Capacity
Defining the fetal stage of legal capacity and its partial obligatory capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define incomplete obligatory capacity (ahliyyah wujub naqisah), identify its scope, and apply it to the fetus in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The fetus has legal capacity for rights but not obligations.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of legal capacity (ahliyyah) in Hanafi legal theory, focusing on its earliest stage: the fetal stage. We will explore how the Shariah grants the unborn child a limited capacity to acquire necessary rights, while exempting it from any duties. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rules of inheritance, bequests, and lineage.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Five Stages of Legal Capacity
Recognize that human legal capacity progresses through five major stages: fetal, childhood, discernment, puberty, and maturity. Each stage corresponds to a different type of ahliyyah.
The fetus is the first stage.
2
Define Incomplete Obligatory Capacity
Identify that the fetus possesses 'ahliyyah wujub naqisah' – a capacity to be obligated (i.e., to receive rights) but not to assume obligations.
This is distinct from performance capacity (ahliyyah ada').
3
Explain the Rationale (Ta'lil)
Understand the jurists' reasoning: the fetus is partly dependent on the mother, yet has independent life. Hence, only essential rights are established.
The example of selling a pregnant animal shows the fetus is implicitly included.
4
Identify Practical Implications
Apply the concept: the fetus can inherit, receive bequests, and have lineage established, but is not liable for debts or duties.
If stillborn, rights are voided retroactively.

Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship
Stages of a Person's Legal Capacity According to the Phases of His Life
Preliminary to the following chapters 60-64
1/60 - It becomes clear from what we have previously explained that a person's legal capacity has stages that it passes through on its path to perfection according to the phases of his life: from his being a fetus (1) until his adolescence and then his maturity. So legal capacity accompanies his entire life and passes in perfection through phases that parallel his phases, so it begins as incomplete obligatory capacity, then ends as complete performance capacity because the stages of legal capacity are derived first from his humanity and the rights that follow from it, then from his intellectual gifts and the aptitudes they bequeath. But it is noted in this regard that if a person's intellectual faculties and gifts increase with the growth of his body year by year and month by month, then his capacity granted to him by the Law does not expand its scope likewise year by year and month by month, but rather expands and transfers from one stage to another in the major phases and milestones of a person's life stages. That is because rapid transition and change in legal capacity and its characteristics is not considered desirable because transactions would be disturbed by it. So it was necessary, with respect to legal capacities, not to take into account the slight differences in the continuous perfection in which the gifts of the developing person proceed, and that his legal capacity should not transfer from one limit to another different from it in characteristics except at the major milestones of his developmental stages.
2/60 - Hence, a person's development is divided into five basic stages: the fetal stage, the childhood stage, the stage of discernment, the stage of puberty, and the stage of maturity.
And the stages of perfection in a person's legal capacity are distributed over these stages.
Evidence of the rulings of the Sharia.
(1) i.e., his formation as a fetus in his mother's womb (see: Al-Qamoos Al-Muheet, entry 'aql').

Chapter Sixty
The First Stage of Legal Capacity: The Fetal Stage
3/60 - This stage is in which a person is a fetus, i.e., a pregnancy in his mother's womb. It extends from conception to birth.
In this stage, jurisprudence has established for the fetus in its mother's womb an incomplete obligatory capacity that makes it capable of being obligated only (i.e., receiving obligations) without assuming obligations (i.e., being liable). So some necessary rights are established for it, and nothing is established against it.
The jurists and legal theorists explain this by saying that the fetus does not have an independent existence from every aspect, but rather resembles being a part of its mother, as if it were one of her organs attached to her. Therefore, if a pregnant animal is sold, its fetus is included in the sale without explicit mention. And from another aspect, it can be considered independent of its mother because it has its own life, and it is preparing to separate from her after a while and become an independent human being (2).
Thus, the capacity of the fetus was limited to necessary rights, especially since it may not be born alive, so there is no need to expand in granting it rights and obligating it.
(1) The jurists and legal theorists in this context use the term 'pregnancy' (al-haml) and do not use the term 'fetus' (al-ijtinan).
We have preferred to revive this derived word directly from 'janin' (fetus) and to use it in its place here because it has the merit of good indication of the natural stage with utmost brevity. It is better than the term 'pregnancy' which means the fetus itself, not its stage.
(2) See: Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi, 2394; Sharh al-Manar li Ibn Malik, p. 334; and the end of Qa'ida 84 from Qawa'id Ibn Rajab al-Hanbali, p. 177.
Source pages p. 744

Commentary
This passage presents the foundational theory of legal capacity (ahliyyah) in Hanafi fiqh and usul, specifically the first stage: the fetal stage (tawr al-ijtinan). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by explaining that legal capacity progresses through stages mirroring human development, starting with incomplete obligatory capacity (ahliyyah wujub naqisah) and culminating in complete performance capacity (ahliyyah ada' kamilah). He emphasizes that this progression does not occur gradually day by day but jumps at major milestones to avoid transactional instability.

The passage then divides human development into five stages: fetal, childhood, discernment, puberty, and maturity. The first stage is the fetus (janin/haml). During this stage, the Law grants the fetus an incomplete obligatory capacity: it can acquire rights (e.g., inheritance, bequest, lineage) but cannot be held liable. The jurists justify this by noting the fetus's dual nature: it is partly dependent on the mother (like an organ) but also has an independent life. An illustrative Hanafi example is the sale of a pregnant animal—the unborn offspring is included in the sale implicitly. Because the fetus may not survive birth, its capacity is limited to essential rights only.

The author also makes a terminological point: while classical jurists use 'haml' (pregnancy) to refer to the fetus, he prefers 'ijtinan' (from janin) as it more precisely denotes the stage rather than the entity itself.

Key Terms
أهلية وجوب ناقصة (ahliyyah wujub naqisah): Incomplete capacity to acquire rights without corresponding obligations; applied to the fetus.
اجتنان (ijtinan): The fetal stage of human development, from conception to birth.
حمل (haml): The fetus in the womb; also used to denote pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What type of legal capacity does the fetus (haml) possess according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Complete performance capacity (ahliyyah ada' kamilah)
B) Incomplete obligatory capacity (ahliyyah wujub naqisah)
C) No legal capacity whatsoever
D) Complete obligatory capacity (ahliyyah wujub kamilah)

Key Takeaway
The fetus in Hanafi law possesses incomplete obligatory capacity: it can receive rights (like inheritance) but bears no obligations.

The Legal Capacity of the Fetus: Four Essential Rights
Exposition of the fetus's legal capacity and the four established rights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four rights established for the fetus in Hanafi fiqh, explain why ownership is pending on live birth, and contrast the Hanafi view on dhimma with the Hanbali position.

Core Idea
The fetus has limited legal capacity: four rights are established but ownership is pending until live birth.

In this lesson, we explore the unique legal status of the fetus (al-haml) in Hanafi fiqh. Drawing from Mustafa al-Zarqa's discussion on legal capacity, we will examine the four rights universally recognized for the fetus and the critical distinction between rights and obligations. Understanding this concept is essential for correctly applying inheritance, bequest, and endowment rules in cases involving an unborn child.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four rights
Read the passage listing the four rights: lineage (nasab), inheritance (irth), bequest (wasiyyah), and endowment (waqf). Understand that these are agreed upon by all madhhabs.
These rights are necessary for the fetus's social and financial protection.
2
Understand pending ownership
The text states that ownership (milkiyyah) for inheritance, bequest, and endowment is not effective but 'pending' (mutawaqqif) on live birth. If stillborn, these rights revert.
This is a Hanafi concept; other schools may differ.
3
Analyze the absence of dhimma
The Hanafi school holds that the fetus has no dhimma (capacity for obligations). Therefore, it cannot be liable for debts or prices, e.g., a purchase made for it is invalid.
This is the key reason for pending ownership.
4
Compare with Hanbali view
The Hanbali school considers the fetus's ownership effective from the time of cause (e.g., death of inheritor) and grants it a dhimma, allowing use of its wealth for maintenance.
The practical difference: immediate vs deferred use.

الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية بالتزامات تكون كلها عرضة للانتقاض إذا لم يكتب له استمرار الحياة حتى الولادة .
٤/٦٠ - الحقوق الضرورية التي تثبت للجنين :
وقد اتفقت المذاهب الاجتهادية على أهلية الحمل لحقوق أربعة تثبت له، وهي:
1- النسب من أبيه وأمه ومن يتصل بهم بواسطتهما.
2- الإرث ممن يموت من مورثيه لأنه متفرّع عن النسب. فيوقف للحمل من تركة مورثه أكبر النصيبين على تقدير كون الحمل ذكراً أو أنثى (1).
3- استحقاق ما يوصى له به .
4- استحقاق ما يوقف عليه.
لأن كلا من الوصية والوقف يجوز شرعاً لمن سيوجد، فيجوز لحمل معين موجود بطريق الأولوية .
ولا أهلية للحمل في استحقاق ما سوى هذه الحقوق الأربعة : فلا تجوز الهبة له ولو اشترى له أبوه أو غيره شيئاً لا يملكه الحمل (2) لعدم : الضرورة، ولأن الشراء له سيلزمه بالثمن وهو غير أهل للالتزام (3).
(1) اختلفت الاجتهادات في مقدار ما يوقف للحمل من ميراث مورثه: فبعضها يوجب أن يوقف له نصيب أربعة أشخاص لاحتمال تعدده؛ وبعضها يرى وقف نصيب اثنين. والراجح في الاجتهاد الحنفي أنه يوقف للحمل أكبر نصيب واحد على تقدير كونه ذكراً أو أنثى، لأن تعدده نادر. فإذا جاء أكثر من واحد يرجع على الورثة بحصته (ر: السراجية وشرحها، فصل الحمل). ومثال كون حصة الحمل الأنثى أكثر من الذكر مالو مات الشخص عن زوجة وأب وأم وبنت وعن زوجة ابن حبلي. فعلى تقدير كون الحمل هنا أنثى تكون حصتها سدس التركة؛ وعلى تقدير كونه ذكراً تكون حصته هي الباقي بعد ذوي الفروض، لأنه العصبات، وإن الباقي في هذه الصورة أقل من السدس. (2) ر: كشف الأسرار شرح أصول فخر الإسلام البزدوي ٤/ ٢٣٩ ، وحاشية الحموي : على الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم الفن الثالث ص/ ٢٠٢).
وقد اتفقت المذاهب الاجتهادية على أهلية الحمل لحقوق أربعة تثبت له، وهي: 1- النسب ...

الفصل (٦٠) الطور الأول للأهلية : طور الاجتنان
الضرورة، ولأن الشراء له سيلزمه بالثمن وهو غير أهل للالتزام (1).
٦٠ ٥ - ويلاحظ أن ما يحجز للجنين من الميراث أو الوصية أو غلة الوقف ليس للجنين فيه ملكية نافذة بل متوقفة على ولادته حي (2). فإذا ولد ميتاً لا يثبت له شيء من تلك الحقوق المتوقفة، فترد غلة الوقف الموقوف عليه إلى سواه من المستحقين ويردّ الميراث المحجوز لأجله إلى ورثة مورثه الآخرين ويردُّ ما أوصي له به إلى ورثة الموصي.
وإذا ولد الجنين حياً ولو تقدير (3) تثبت ملكيته في هذه الحقوق مستندة إلى وقت سببها السابق، أي بأثر رجعي.
(1) ومن ثم يصرّح فقهاؤنا أن الحمل لا ذمة له (ر: كشف الأسرار شرح أصول فخر الإسلام البزدوي (٢٣٧/٤)، وذلك لأن الذمة وعاء اعتباري لاستقرار الحقوق التي على الشخص كما أسلفنا الإشارة إليه ( ر ) ف (٥٩(١٠) فلا حاجة إلى تقديرها فيه قبل أن أهلاً للالتزامات. وسيأتي لذلك مزيد إيضاح في بحث نظرية الذمة من الجزء من هذه السلسلة الفقهية .
(2) وفي الاجتهاد الحنبلي يُعتبر ملك الجنين نافذاً في الميراث منذ وفاة مورثه . وقد فَرّعوا على ذلك أن المرأة الحامل وأقارب حملها المستحقين للنفقة ينفق عليهم من ميراث الحمل وسائر أمواله إن كان له مال وهذا منقول عن الإمام أحمد نفسه (ر: القواعد لابن رجب الحنبلي تحت القاعدة / ٨٤/ ص / ١٨١ الطبعة الأولى بمطبعة الصدق بالقاهرة) . وهذا يفيد أيضاً أن للحمل في الاجتهاد الحنبلي أهلية للالتزام وذمة .
(3) تكون حياة الحمل تقديرية إذا أسقط بجناية على أمه كما لو ضرب أحد امرأة حبلى فأسقطت من الضرب جنيناً ميتاً، فإن هذا الجنين يعتبر حياً حكماً، وأنه مات بفعل الجاني. فيلتزم الجاني بدية الجنين التي تسمى: (غُرّة وقدرها خمسمائة درهم تساوي خمسين ديناراً كل عشرة بدينار تعطى لورثة الجنين كما يثبت حق الجنين في ميراث مورثه الذي مات قبلاً، فيعطى نصيب الجنين من الميراث لورثته هو ولا يعاد لورثة مورثه (ر )) الدر المختار فصل الجنين من كتاب الديات ٣٧٧/٥ -(٣٧٨). وزنة الدينار مثقال من الذهب، (وزنة المثقال اليوم ٤,٢٥ غراماً). ويعتبر هذا المعيار في جميع الوجائب المالية من دينية ومدنية ( ر ) رد المحتار باب زكاة المال ٢٩/٢).
Chapter Eight: The Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship with obligations that are all subject to annulment if continued life until birth is not written for him. 4/60 - The necessary rights that are established for the fetus: The jurisprudential schools have agreed on the legal capacity of the fetus for four rights that are established for it, namely: 1- Lineage from his father and mother and those connected to them through them. 2- Inheritance from whoever dies among his inheritors because it is derived from lineage. So from the estate of his inheritor, the greater of the two shares is stopped for the fetus on the assumption that the fetus is male or female (1). 3- Entitlement to what is bequeathed to him. 4- Entitlement to what is endowed for him. Because both bequest and endowment are legally permissible for one who will exist, so it is permissible for an existing specific fetus by way of priority. And the fetus has no capacity for entitlement to other than these four rights: so gift to him is not permissible, and even if his father or someone else buys something for him, the fetus does not own it (2) due to the absence of necessity, and because the purchase would obligate him with the price and he is not qualified for obligation (3). (1) The jurisprudential opinions differed regarding the amount to be stopped for the fetus from the inheritance of his inheritor: some require stopping the share of four persons due to the possibility of multiplicity; some see stopping the share of two. The preponderant in Hanafi jurisprudence is that the greater single share is stopped for the fetus on the assumption of it being male or female, because its multiplicity is rare. If more than one comes, they revert to the heirs for their share. And an example of the female share being greater than the male: if a person dies leaving a wife, father, mother, daughter, and a pregnant daughter-in-law. On the assumption that the fetus here is female, her share is one-sixth of the estate; on the assumption it is male, his share is the remainder after the fixed shares, because he is an agnatic heir, and the remainder in this case is less than one-sixth. (2) See: Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi 4/239, and Hashiyat al-Hamawi on al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir of Ibn Nujaym, al-Fann al-Thalith p. 202. Chapter (60) The first stage of legal capacity: The stage of concealment. Necessity, and because the purchase for him would obligate him with the price and he is not qualified for obligation (1). 60/5 - And it is noted that what is reserved for the fetus from inheritance or bequest or revenue of endowment does not constitute effective ownership for the fetus but is pending on his being born alive (2). If he is born dead, none of those pending rights are established, so the revenue of the endowment reserved for him is returned to other entitled recipients, the inheritance reserved for him is returned to the other heirs of his inheritor, and what was bequeathed to him is returned to the testator's heirs. And if the fetus is born alive, even hypothetically (3), his ownership in these rights is established dating back to the time of their prior cause, i.e., retroactively. (1) Hence our jurists state: 'The fetus has no dhimma (legal capacity for obligations)' (see: Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi 4/237), because dhimma is a notional vessel for the establishment of rights due from a person, as we have previously indicated, so there is no need to presume it in him before he is qualified for obligations. Further clarification will come in the discussion of the theory of dhimma in the part from this jurisprudential series. (2) In Hanbali jurisprudence, the ownership of the fetus is considered effective in inheritance from the time of death of his inheritor. They derived that the pregnant woman and the relatives of her fetus entitled to maintenance are provided for from the inheritance of the fetus and his other wealth if he has wealth, and this is narrated from Imam Ahmad himself. This also indicates that the fetus in Hanbali jurisprudence has legal capacity for obligations and a dhimma. (3) The life of the fetus is hypothetical if it is miscarried due to an assault on its mother, such as if someone strikes a pregnant woman and she miscarries a dead fetus from the blow, then this fetus is considered alive by legal fiction, and that it died by the act of the perpetrator. So the perpetrator is liable for the blood money (diya) of the fetus called 'ghurra' amounting to five hundred dirhams equal to fifty dinars, each ten dirhams per dinar, given to the heirs of the fetus, and the right of the fetus to inheritance from his inheritor who died earlier is established, so the fetus's share of the inheritance is given to his own heirs and is not returned to the inheritor's heirs (see: al-Durr al-Mukhtar, chapter on the fetus from the Book of Diyat 5/377-378). The weight of a dinar is a mithqal of gold (the weight of a mithqal today is 4.25 grams). This standard is considered in all financial obligations, both religious and civil (see: Radd al-Muhtar, chapter on Zakat of Wealth 2/29).
Source pages p. 746

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the legal capacity of the fetus (al-haml) in Hanafi fiqh, from the chapter on legal capacity and guardianship. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, systematically presents the four rights universally recognized for the fetus by all madhhabs: lineage, inheritance, bequest, and endowment. These rights are necessary for the fetus even before birth, but with a key limitation: ownership is pending (mutawaqqif) on live birth, not effective immediately. The Hanafi school distinguishes between rights (for which the fetus has partial capacity) and obligations (for which it has no capacity, lacking a dhimma). The passage also contrasts the Hanafi position with the Hanbali view, which grants the fetus full ownership from the moment of inheritance. The author then explains the practical implications: if the fetus is stillborn, the reserved rights revert; if born alive (even hypothetically, as in the case of a miscarriage due to assault), ownership retroactively attaches. This theory of pending ownership is a cornerstone of Hanafi inheritance and endowment law, ensuring justice for potential heirs while preventing premature claims. The footnote provides a detailed example of how to calculate the reserved share in inheritance, showing the preference for a single larger share to account for the possibility of a male or female heir, with the understanding that multiple births are rare.

Key Terms
حمل (haml): The fetus from conception until birth.
ذمة (dhimma): A notional vessel for the establishment of rights due from a person; the fetus has no dhimma until birth.
ملكية متوقفة (milkiyyah mutawaqqifah): Ownership that is not effective immediately but becomes so upon fulfillment of a condition (live birth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to the share of inheritance reserved for a fetus if the fetus is stillborn?
A) It goes to the fetus's heirs.
B) It is divided among the other living heirs of the original inheritor.
C) It is given to the state.
D) It is kept for the next pregnancy.

Key Takeaway
The fetus has limited legal capacity: four rights are established but ownership is pending on live birth; obligations are not imposed due to the absence of dhimma.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
15 min
Childhood Stage: Capacity for Obligations vs. Capacity for Performance
Exposition of childhood stage and its legal effects on capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between capacity for obligations and capacity for performance in the childhood stage and apply the Hanafi rule that all constructive acts of a non-discerning child are void.

Core Idea
A non-discerning child has full capacity for obligations but zero capacity for performance — every constructive act of his is void.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's legal introduction defines the second stage of legal capacity: childhood. It separates the child's ability to have rights and duties from his ability to act independently. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining the validity of a child's transactions and torts.

Guided Path
1
Define the childhood stage and discernment
Read the opening definition: childhood begins at birth and ends when the child becomes discerning (mumayyiz). Discernment means understanding the legislative address in general, including religious acts and civil transactions.
The non-discerning child (ghayr mumayyiz) may have natural perception but lacks comprehension of legal acts.
2
Distinguish capacity for obligations from capacity for performance
Identify the two types of capacity: ahliyyat al-wujub (capacity to have rights and obligations) and ahliyyat al-ada' (capacity to perform acts requiring reason). At birth, the former becomes complete, but the latter remains absent until discernment.
Capacity for obligations has two elements: creditor-ability and debtor-ability.
3
Identify the legal consequences for the non-discerning child
The passage lists three categories: (a) words are void (contracts, acceptance of gifts, etc.); (b) actions are invalid (possession of property, religious acts); (c) torts are not punishable (even murder does not bar inheritance).
Exception: intentional destruction of another's property may create civil liability (footnote in source).
4
Apply the rule to concrete scenarios
Take the examples from the passage: a child's acceptance of a gift is void; if a seller delivers goods to the child, the risk remains with the seller; a child who kills his testator still inherits.
These illustrate that performance capacity is entirely absent.
5
Understand that capacity for obligations is complete from birth
The child can own property acquired on his behalf (by guardian or through inheritance), and others can have claims against his estate. This capacity was incomplete in the fetal stage.
Reference to earlier section: the fetus had limited capacity for obligations.

Source Text

الفصل الحادي والستون
الطور الثاني للأهلية : طور الطفولة
١/٦١ - ويمتد هذا الطور في الإنسان من حين أن تلده أمه حتى مميزاً . والمراد بتمييزه أن يصير له وعي وإدراك يفهم به الخطاب التشريعي إجمالاً، فيدرك معاني الأعمال الدينية والمعاملات المدنية، ويفهم نتائج هذه المعاملات في تبادل الحقوق والالتزامات ولو بصورة بسيطة مجملة، فيعرف فرق البيع عن الشراء، ويفهم تعادل القيم وتفاوتها.
فالطفل في طور طفولته قبل أن يصل إلى هذا الوعي والتمييز يعتبر ويسمى : غير مميز وإن كان له إدراك وتمييز في كثير من الأمور الطبيعية . ٢/٦١ - إن مما لا ريب فيه أنَّ خروج الجنين إلى الدنيا بالولادة ينتقل فيه الإنسان من طَوْر إلى طور أساسيين في حياته وقابلياته : فهو ينتقل من طور هو فيه كجزء تابع لأمه في الحياة إلى طور هو فيه ذو وجود مستقل وقابليات طبيعية لم تكن له من قبل.
وبذلك وجب أن تتسع أهليته وتدخل في مرحلة أكمل. ولكنها إنما يمكن أن تتسع في نطاق أهلية الوجوب ولا يمكن أن تمتد إلى دائرة أهلية الأداء، لأنَّ الوعي مفقود فيه، فيظل الطفل فاقداً في هذا الطور أهلية الأداء، لأنَّ أهلية الأداء إنما تؤهله لأعمال دينية أو مدنية تعتمد التعقل والطفل لا يعي هذه الأعمال أصلاً في مبدأ هذا الطور، ولا يفهمها فهماً كافياً في أواخره .
٣/٦١ - ولفقدان أهلية الأداء في الطفل غير المميز لا يعتد بشيء من التصرفات الإنشائية يباشره بنفسه من أقوال أو أفعال.
أ - فأقواله كلها هدر لا يترتب عليها حكم؛ وعقوده باطلة، لأن عبارته غير معتبرة شرعاً في إيجاب أو قبول أو إقرار أو إبراء أو غير ذلك. حتى إنه لو كان تصرفه نفعاً محضاً له كقبوله الهبة أو الصدقة لا يصح منه، بل ينوب عنه نائبه الشرعي من وليّ أو وصيّ في مباشرة جميع العقود والتصرفات المدنية التي يحتاج إليها . (ر ) الأحوال الشخصية لقدري باشا م/ ٤٨٣).
ب - وأفعاله كذلك سواء أكانت دينية كالصلاة والصيام، أو مدنية كقبض المبيع أو الوديعة أو القرض، كلها غير معتبرة. فلو اشترى له وليه أو وصيه شيئاً فسلمه البائع إلى الطفل لا يعتبر تسلمه: فلو هلك في يد الطفل يهلك على حساب البائع كما لو هلك عند البائع قبل التسليم (ر: الأحوال الشخصية أيضاً م/ ٤۸۸)(۱) .
وكذلك ما لو غَصَب مال الطفل غاصب، ثم رده إلى الطفل نفسه، فإِنَّ الغاصب لا يبرأ عن ضمان قيمة المال إذا هَلَكَ، لأن تسلم الطفل لماله باطل غير معتبر . ج - وإذا جَنَى الطفل جناية ولو قتلاً لا يعتبر فعله إجراماً، فلا يستحق العقوبة، حتى إنه لو كان القتيل مورثه لا يحرم الطفل القاتل من ميراثه لسقوط المؤاخذة عنه (۲)
٤/٦١ - أما أهلية الوجوب فتكمل في الطفل بعنصريها(۳) منذ ولادته فيصبح أهلاً لثبوت الحقوق والالتزامات له ،وعليه، بعد أن كانت هذه الأهلية لديه في طور اجتنانه قبل ولادته ناقصة كما تقدم. (ر: ف ٣/٦٠). وعلى هذا الأساس يملك الطفل منذ ولادته ما يشترى له أو يوهب
(1) بخلاف ما لو أتلف الطفل من تلقاء نفسه مال غيره دون تسليم أو تسليط من المالك ، فإنه عندئذ يضمنه كما سنرى لأنه جناية على مال الغير.
(۲) فالجناية لا تكون سبباً شرعياً لاستحقاق العقوبة إلا بما فيها من صفة الإجرام الذي تمرد على النظام العام. ومن شرائط اتصاف الجاني بصفة الإجرام تمام عقله، وذلك بالبلوغ، لأن العقل أساس المؤاخذة العقابية على عدم الطاعة.
(۳) وهما قابلية الدائنية وقابلية المديونية، أو الإلزام والالتزام (ر) ف ٩/٥٩).
Chapter Sixty-One: The Second Stage of Capacity: The Stage of Childhood. 1/61 - This stage extends in a human from the moment his mother gives birth to him until he becomes discerning. What is meant by his discernment is that he acquires awareness and comprehension by which he understands the legislative address in general, so he perceives the meanings of religious acts and civil transactions, and understands the consequences of these transactions in the exchange of rights and obligations, even in a simple and summary manner, so he knows the difference between sale and purchase, and understands equality and inequality of values. Thus, the child in his childhood stage before reaching this awareness and discernment is considered and called: non-discerning, even if he has perception and discernment in many natural matters. 2/61 - There is no doubt that the exit of the fetus into the world by birth transfers the human from one stage to a basic stage in his life and capabilities: he moves from a stage where he is like a part dependent on his mother in life to a stage where he has independent existence and natural capabilities he did not have before. Consequently, his capacity must expand and enter a more complete phase. But it can only expand within the scope of capacity for obligations and cannot extend to the sphere of capacity for performance, because awareness is lacking in him, so the child remains lacking in this stage the capacity for performance, because capacity for performance qualifies him for religious or civil acts that depend on reason, and the child does not comprehend these acts at all at the beginning of this stage, nor does he understand them sufficiently at its end. 3/61 - Due to the lack of capacity for performance in the non-discerning child, no constructive disposals that he performs himself by words or actions are considered. A - All his words are of no consequence and no ruling follows from them; his contracts are void, because his statement is not legally considered in offer, acceptance, acknowledgment, discharge, or otherwise. Even if his disposal is purely beneficial to him, like his acceptance of a gift or charity, it is not valid from him; rather, his legal representative, whether a guardian or executor, acts on his behalf in all contracts and civil disposals that he needs. (Reference: Personal Status by Qadri Pasha, Article 483). B - And his actions likewise, whether religious like prayer and fasting, or civil like taking possession of a sold item, a deposit, or a loan, all are not considered. If his guardian or executor buys something for him and the seller delivers it to the child, his taking possession is not considered: if it perishes in the child's hand, it perishes at the seller's expense, just as if it perished with the seller before delivery. (Reference: Personal Status also, Article 488) (1). Likewise, if someone usurps the child's property and then returns it to the child himself, the usurper is not released from liability for the value of the property if it perishes, because the child's taking possession of his property is void and not considered. C - And if the child commits a tort, even murder, his act is not considered a crime, so he does not deserve punishment; even if the victim is his testator, the child killer is not deprived of his inheritance due to the removal of accountability from him (2). 4/61 - As for the capacity for obligations, it is completed in the child with its two elements (3) from his birth, so he becomes eligible for the establishment of rights and obligations for him and upon him, after this capacity was incomplete in him during the fetal stage before birth, as previously mentioned. (Reference: Section 3/60). On this basis, the child owns from his birth what is purchased or gifted to him. (1) Unlike if the child himself destroys another's property without delivery or authorization from the owner, then he is liable for it, as we will see, because it is a tort against another's property. (2) For an offense is not a legal cause for deserving punishment except by the element of criminality that rebels against public order. Among the conditions for the offender to be characterized with criminality is complete intellect, which is attained by puberty, because intellect is the basis for punitive accountability for disobedience. (3) They are: creditor-ability and debtor-ability, or imposing obligation and being obligated. (Reference: Section 9/59).
Source pages p. 748

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the second stage of legal capacity (أهلية) in Islamic jurisprudence: the stage of childhood (طور الطفولة). The author defines the stage as extending from birth until the child becomes discerning (مميز). Discernment is defined as the acquisition of awareness and comprehension enabling the child to understand legislative address in general, including religious acts and civil transactions, albeit in a simple way. The child before this level is called 'non-discerning' (غير مميز), even if he has natural perception. 

The passage then explains the transition at birth: the child moves from being a part of his mother to an independent existence, necessitating an expansion of his capacity. However, this expansion is only in the capacity for obligations (أهلية الوجوب), not in the capacity for performance (أهلية الأداء) because awareness is lacking. Performance capacity requires reason (تعقل), which the child lacks especially at the beginning of this stage.

Because the non-discerning child lacks performance capacity, all his constructive disposals (تصرفات إنشائية) are void. The author details three categories: (a) his words are null, including contracts, even if purely beneficial like accepting a gift; (b) his actions are not considered, such as taking possession of purchased items; and (c) his torts are not punishable, as criminal intent requires complete intellect (puberty). A key ruling: if a child commits murder of his testator, he still inherits because accountability is removed.

The passage concludes that the capacity for obligations is complete from birth, making the child eligible to own property acquired on his behalf. This aligns with the Hanafi view that a child can be a creditor or debtor from birth.

Key Terms
غير مميز (ghayr mumayyiz): a child who has not yet attained the ability to understand legislative address in general, even if he has natural perception
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada'): legal capacity to perform acts that depend on reason, including religious duties and civil transactions
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): capacity to have rights and obligations, which is complete in a child from birth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a non-discerning child (غير مميز) accepts a donation (هبة), what is the ruling?
A) The acceptance is valid because it is purely beneficial.
B) The acceptance is valid if the child understands the concept of gift.
C) The acceptance is void; the guardian must accept on his behalf.
D) The acceptance is valid only if witnessed.

Key Takeaway
A non-discerning child has full capacity for obligations but zero capacity for performance — all his constructive acts are void, and his torts are not criminally punishable, though civil liability for direct destruction of property may still apply.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
8 min
Child's Statements and Actions: Void and Invalid
Ruling on child's statements and actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a child's statements and actions are legally void and apply the rule to determine when a guardian must act.

Core Idea
A child's words have no legal weight; only the guardian's action counts.

This lesson examines the Hanafi principle that a child's legal statements (e.g., contracts, acknowledgments) and actions (religious or civil) are null unless performed by a guardian. The rationale is the child's incomplete intellectual capacity, which prevents binding intent. Understanding this foundation is critical for muftis advising families on children's transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on statements
The passage states that the child's statements are 'hader' (null) and contracts are 'batil' (void). This means no legal effect arises from anything the child says or agrees to.
This includes acceptance of gifts, acknowledgment of debts, and releases.
2
Understand the rationale
The child's expression is 'ghayr mu'tabara' (not legally considered) because he lacks full understanding (aql) required for binding intent.
This is based on the principle that legal capacity for actions (ahliyyat al-ada') is incomplete.
3
Apply to examples of actions
Religious acts like prayer and civil acts like taking delivery of goods are invalid if performed by the child alone. For instance, if a seller delivers to the child, it is as if delivery did not occur.
The guardian must perform all transactions on the child's behalf.
4
Contrast with criminal liability
Although the child is not criminally punished (no hadd or tazir), he may still be financially liable for damages, as the passage later explains. This lesson focuses on the absence of criminal punishment.
The child's act is not considered criminal (ijram) due to lack of intent.

أ - فأقواله كلها هدر لا يترتب عليها حكم؛ وعقوده باطلة، لأن عبارته غير معتبرة شرعاً في إيجاب أو قبول أو إقرار أو إبراء أو غير ذلك. حتى إنه لو كان تصرفه نفعاً محضاً له كقبوله الهبة أو الصدقة لا يصح منه، بل ينوب عنه نائبه الشرعي من وليّ أو وصيّ في مباشرة جميع العقود والتصرفات المدنية التي يحتاج إليها . (ر ) الأحوال الشخصية لقدري باشا م/ ٤٨٣).
ب - وأفعاله كذلك سواء أكانت دينية كالصلاة والصيام، أو مدنية كقبض المبيع أو الوديعة أو القرض، كلها غير معتبرة. فلو اشترى له وليه أو وصيه شيئاً فسلمه البائع إلى الطفل لا يعتبر تسلمه: فلو هلك في يد الطفل يهلك على حساب البائع كما لو هلك عند البائع قبل التسليم (ر: الأحوال الشخصية أيضاً م/ ٤۸۸)(۱) .
وكذلك ما لو غَصَب مال الطفل غاصب، ثم رده إلى الطفل نفسه، فإِنَّ الغاصب لا يبرأ عن ضمان قيمة المال إذا هَلَكَ، لأن تسلم الطفل لماله باطل غير معتبر . ج - وإذا جَنَى الطفل جناية ولو قتلاً لا يعتبر فعله إجراماً، فلا يستحق العقوبة، حتى إنه لو كان القتيل مورثه لا يحرم الطفل القاتل من ميراثه لسقوط المؤاخذة عنه (۲)
٤/٦١ - أما أهلية الوجوب فتكمل في الطفل بعنصريها(۳) منذ ولادته فيصبح أهلاً لثبوت الحقوق والالتزامات له ،وعليه، بعد أن كانت هذه الأهلية لديه في طور اجتنانه قبل ولادته ناقصة كما تقدم. (ر: ف ٣/٦٠). وعلى هذا الأساس يملك الطفل منذ ولادته ما يشترى له أو يوهب (۱) بخلاف ما لو أتلف الطفل من تلقاء نفسه مال غيره دون تسليم أو تسليط من المالك ، فإنه عندئذ يضمنه كما سنرى لأنه جناية على مال الغير. (۲) فالجناية لا تكون سبباً شرعياً لاستحقاق العقوبة إلا بما فيها من صفة الإجرام الذي تمرد على النظام العام. ومن شرائط اتصاف الجاني بصفة الإجرام تمام عقله، وذلك بالبلوغ، لأن العقل أساس المؤاخذة العقابية على عدم الطاعة. (۳) وهما قابلية الدائنية وقابلية المديونية، أو الإلزام والالتزام (ر) ف ٩/٥٩).
