Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. B - The Principle of Discrimination. 2/62 - This discrimination has no fixed age in human life or natural sign, but may come early or late depending on the child's nature, intelligence, and mental abilities. It is known only by its effect, namely the balance appearing in the child's thinking and actions. This effect does not appear suddenly but gradually, preceded by signs of reasoning that approach it like beads of a necklace, each resembling the one before and after, because each is slightly larger than the one before and slightly smaller than the one after, but the difference between two distant ones appears great. Thus the beginnings of this stage resemble the end of the preceding childhood stage. If the transition from childhood were made conditional on the appearance of the effects of discrimination itself, the criterion would be unregulated and the boundaries of the new capacity unclear. Therefore, the wisdom of legislation required considering an appropriate age as the beginning of the stage of discrimination. Thus the jurists considered that the completion of the seventh year, in normal healthy condition, is the beginning of the stage of discrimination and the new capacity that follows (1). C - Limits of the Capacity of the Discerning Minor. 3/62 - It is clear from the above that when the minor reaches the age of discrimination, his readiness and abilities are in an intermediate position between the non-discerning child and the mature adult. This position requires that he be allowed to act in a restricted rather than absolute manner, because on one hand he needs practice in exercising actions for which he has sound awareness, and on the other hand he also needs protection for his rights from his expected misconduct and errors, i.e., protection from himself, because he is still a minor whose intellect has not reached maturity. Therefore, the jurists of Shari'ah, based on the proofs of its principles and rulings, decided that the discerning minor in this stage has a deficient performance capacity (ahliyyat ada' qasira), meaning his capacity for financial transactions. Capacity is inferred from its types and limits from the legislative rulings built upon it, as the building is inferred from the foundation. D - Religious Performance Capacity and Civil Performance Capacity. 4/62 - Considering what the jurists and usuliyyun have decided for the discerning minor necessitates dividing performance capacity into two parts: First: Capacity for worship (ahliyyat al-ta'abbud), which qualifies a person to perform prescribed acts of worship so that they are considered valid from him. We call this here religious performance capacity. Second: Capacity for transaction (ahliyyat al-tasarruf), which qualifies him for financial dealings and legal acts, verbal and physical, such as selling and buying, taking and giving. We call this civil performance capacity. A - As for religious performance capacity or capacity for worship, it is established for the minor fully from the beginning of the stage of discrimination. By virtue of it, acts of worship that are a mix of physical and mental work, like prayer and fasting, are valid from him by consensus of ijtihad, even though they are not obligatory upon him. The fact that these religious duties are not imposed on him - despite the completeness of capacity for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) in him since birth and the completeness of religious performance capacity with discrimination - is because capacity is only...
Source pages p. 754

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am explains the third stage of legal capacity (ahliyyah): the stage of discrimination (tamyiz). The author begins by noting that tamyiz has no fixed age or natural sign; it varies with each child's intellectual development. However, to ensure legal certainty, the jurists adopted the completion of the seventh year as the presumed beginning of tamyiz in a normal healthy child, citing the hadith ordering children to pray at age seven. This is a default rule, not an absolute one. After establishing tamyiz, the text explains that the discerning minor has a deficient performance capacity (ahliyyat ada' qasira): he is allowed to act in financial matters but under guardianship to protect his interests. Finally, the author divides performance capacity into two types: religious (capacity for worship, such as prayer and fasting, which are valid from the minor even if not obligatory) and civil (capacity for legal transactions like sale and purchase). The religious capacity is complete from the start of tamyiz, while the civil capacity is deficient. The passage thus illustrates the gradual development of legal personality in Islamic law.

Key Terms
التمييز (al-tamyiz): The stage where a child begins to understand and act with some reason and discernment, marked by the jurists as the completion of seven years in normal cases.
أهلية الأداء القاصرة (ahliyyat al-ada' al-qasira): Deficient performance capacity: the discerning minor can perform legal acts (financial transactions) but they are subject to guardianship and not fully binding.
أهلية التعبد (ahliyyat al-ta'abbud): Religious performance capacity: the ability to perform acts of worship validly, even if not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) At the completion of the fifth year
B) At the completion of the seventh year
C) At the first appearance of rational speech
D) At puberty

Key Takeaway
Tamyiz is the stage of discrimination legally presumed at seven, granting full religious capacity but deficient civil capacity subject to guardianship.

Types of Legal Capacity: Religious and Civil, and the Rule for Pure Financial Harm
Classifies capacity of performance and rules on minor's harmful transactions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between religious and civil capacity of performance, and apply the rule that pure financial harm dispositions by a discerning minor are void.

Core Idea
A discerning minor's acts of worship are valid but not obligatory; his gifts are void because they cause pure financial harm.

This lesson explores the Hanafi theory of legal capacity, focusing on the stage of discernment (tamyeez). You will learn how capacity of performance is divided into religious and civil, and how this classification determines the validity of a minor's acts. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the correct rulings in transactions involving minors.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of capacity
Identify religious capacity (for worship) and civil capacity (for financial transactions). Note their different scopes and completeness at the stage of discernment.
Religious capacity is complete; civil capacity is limited (qasirah).
2
Analyze the ruling on religious acts
Understand that acts of worship (prayer, fasting) are valid from a discerning minor by consensus, even though they are not obligatory until puberty. Capacity qualifies, it does not impose.
Taklif requires additional conditions like puberty.
3
Understand why civil capacity is limited
Civil capacity is limited to protect the minor from his own inexperience and lack of knowledge. This limitation prevents him from making harmful financial decisions.
Protection of property is key.
4
Apply the rule for pure financial harm
Dispositions that are pure financial harm (dharar mali mahd) such as gifts, charity, endowments, loans, and guarantees are void (batil). No guardian can authorize them.
Includes divorce according to some Hanafi jurists.

Chapter (62): The Third Stage of Legal Capacity: The Stage of Discernment
Also to protect his rights from his bad actions and expected mistakes, i.e., to protect him from himself, because he is still a minor and his intellect has not reached maturity. Accordingly, the jurists of Sharia, based on the evidences of its rules and rulings, decided that the discerning minor in this stage has a limited capacity of performance, meaning by that his capacity for financial disposition.
And capacity is inferred from the legislative rulings built upon it, just as the building is inferred from the foundation.
D - Religious Capacity of Performance and Civil Capacity of Performance
4/62 - Consideration of what the jurists and usul scholars have established for the discerning minor of rulings necessitates dividing the capacity of performance into two types:
First: The capacity for worship, which qualifies the person to perform prescribed acts of worship so that they are considered valid from him. We call this here religious capacity of performance.
Second: The capacity for disposition, which qualifies him for financial transactions and legal dispositions, verbal and physical, such as buying and selling, taking and giving. We call this civil capacity of performance.
A - As for religious capacity of performance, or the capacity for worship, it is established for the minor completely from the beginning of the stage of discernment. By virtue of it, acts of worship that are a mixture of physical and intellectual work, such as prayer and fasting, are valid from him by consensus of the ijtihads, even if they are not obligatory upon him.
And the non-imposition of these religious duties upon him - despite the completeness of the capacity of obligation in him since his birth and the completeness of religious capacity of performance by discernment - is because capacity merely qualifies and does not impose. Imposition is a charge (taklif) and it depends on its cause and all its Sharia conditions. The first of its conditions is puberty, by which his body's endurance and intellect's completion are achieved. Thus, a person is not charged with any practical obligations before puberty.
B - As for civil capacity of performance, or the capacity for disposition, it is also established for the minor from the beginning of this stage, but in it it is limited, not complete. This limitation is to protect his rights and safeguard his property, due to his lack of experience in financial transactions and lack of knowledge of people's conditions.
Division of the Discerning Minor's Dispositions
According to the Limitation of Civil Capacity of Performance in Him:
5/62 - By virtue of this limitation in the civil capacity of performance in the discerning minor, the jurists divided his legal dispositions in terms of their validity and consequences into three types:
6/62 - The First Type: Dispositions that are pure financial harm with respect to the minor.
That includes donations of all kinds, such as gift, charity, endowment, loan, or others.
This type, the minor has no power to do it, and no one - guardian, executor, or judge - has the power to authorize it for him or do it on his behalf. If it occurs, it is void, to protect the minor's rights due to the limitation of his capacity.
And of this kind is the guarantee by the discerning minor of a debt owed by another; his guarantee is not valid because it contains the meaning of donation by undertaking the payment of a debt on another's behalf.
And the jurists have counted among this type the divorce by the minor of his wife if he is married.
Source pages p. 755

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical exposition on the theory of legal capacity (ahliyyah) in Hanafi fiqh, specifically the third stage: the age of discernment (tamyeez). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that the discerning minor (saghir mumayyiz) possesses a limited capacity of performance (ahliyyat ada' qasirah) for financial transactions, based on the need to protect his rights from his own inexperience. The text then divides capacity of performance into two types: religious (diniyyah) and civil (madaniyyah). Religious capacity, which qualifies the minor to perform acts of worship like prayer and fasting, is fully established from the onset of discernment. Consequently, these acts are valid if performed, even though they are not obligatory until puberty. The author clarifies that capacity merely enables (tu'ahhil) but does not impose (la tujib); obligation (taklif) requires additional conditions, primarily puberty. Civil capacity, which covers financial transactions and legal acts, is also established but is limited (qasirah) to protect the minor's property due to his lack of experience. Based on this limitation, the jurists classify the minor's legal dispositions into three types. The passage describes the first type: dispositions that are pure financial harm (dharar mali mahd) to the minor, such as gifts, charity, endowments, loans, and guarantees (kafalah). These are entirely invalid (batil) because the minor cannot perform them, and no guardian can authorize them. Interestingly, divorce (talaq) is included in this category by some jurists, treating it as a harmful disposition, even though it is not financial—this reflects the Hanafi view that a minor's divorce is ineffective.

Key Terms
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada'): The capacity to perform legal acts that produce their intended legal effects; divided here into religious and civil.
الصغير المميز (al-saghir al-mumayyiz): A minor who has reached the age of discernment (tamyeez), typically around age 7, and can understand the nature of transactions and worship.
ضرر مالي محض (dharar mali mahd): Pure financial harm, i.e., a transaction that provides no benefit to the minor and only diminishes his wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid if approved by his guardian
B) Void (batil) because it is pure financial harm
C) Suspended (mawquf) until puberty
D) Valid but the minor can revoke it later

Key Takeaway
A discerning minor's acts of worship are valid but not obligatory; his pure financial harm dispositions are void, and no one can authorize them.

Pure Financial Harm: Discerning Minor's Void Donations
Classifying the first type of minor's transactions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify transactions that constitute pure financial harm for a discerning minor and state that they are void (bāṭil) in the Hanafi school, including the exception for a judge lending orphan property.

Core Idea
A discerning minor's donations are void because they cause pure financial harm—the law protects his wealth.

The Hanafi jurists classify a discerning minor's transactions based on financial impact. The first type includes all dispositions that are pure financial harm, such as gifts, charity, and surety. These are void to safeguard the minor's property. However, there is a notable exception: a judge may lend the orphan's money to protect it from loss, turning a nominal donation into a guaranteed loan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic
Look for transactions that benefit others but harm the minor financially with no benefit to him.
The harm is exclusive to the minor.
2
List the examples given
The text mentions gifts, charity, waqf, loan for use, surety for a debt, and even divorce by a married minor.
All are void regardless of guardian's permission.
3
Note the exception
A judge lending orphan property is excepted because it protects the property from loss; the loan is guaranteed.
Only the judge may do this, not guardians.
4
Understand the rationale
The deficiency in capacity is to protect the minor. Any transaction causing pure financial harm is void ab initio.

الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية تؤهل ولا توجب فالإيجاب تكليف وهو يتوقف على سببه وسائر شرائطه الشرعية. وإن أول شرائطه البلوغ الذي يتم به احتمال جسمه واكتمال عقله، فلا يكون الشخص مكلفاً بشيء من الواجبات العملية قبل البلوغ. ب - وأما أهلية الأداء المدنية، أو أهلية التصرف فإنها أيضاً تثبت للصغير من أول هذا الطور، لكنها فيه قاصرة غير كاملة، وقصورها هذا لحماية حقوقه وصيانة أمواله، نظراً لقلة خبرته في التصرف بالمال، وعدم معرفته بأحوال الناس. تقسیم تصرفات الصغير المميز بحسب قصور أهلية الأداء المدنية فيه : ٥/٦٢ - وبمقتضى هذا القصور في أهلية الأداء المدنية لدى الصغير المميز قسم الفقهاء تصرفاته الحقوقية من حيث اعتبارها ونتائجها إلى ثلاثة أنواع : الصغير . ٦/٦٢ - النوع الأول: التصرفات التي هي ضرر مالي محض في حق وذلك كالتبرعات بجميع أنواعها من هبة أو صدقة، أو وقف، أو إعارة، أو غيرها. فهذا النوع لا يملك الصغير فعله، ولا يملك أحد من وليّ أو وصي أو قاض أن يجيزه له أو يفعله عنه. فإذا وقع كان باطلاً حماية لحقوق الصغير بمقتضى قصور أهليته . ومن هذا القبيل كفالة الصغير المميز ديناً على غيره، فإن كفالته لا تصح، لما فيها من معنى التبرع بالتزام أداء الدين عن غيره. وقد عد الفقهاء من هذا النوع طلاق الصغير لزوجته إن كان مزوجاً، فإن طلاقه باطل، لأنه يخسر به المهر ومنفعة الزوجية (1) : وقد استثنوا من هذا النوع إقراض القاضي مال اليتيم، وإن كان في القرض معنى التبرع ابتداءاً، فإنه يصون مال اليتيم من الضياع، وهو خير من الإيداع، لأن الوديعة إذا هلكت دون تعد من الوديع عليها أو تقصير في حفظها تهلك أمانة غير مضمونة بخلاف القرض فإنه مضمون على المقترض في جميع الأحوال وبسلطة القاضي إذا تولى هو الإقراض لا يخشى على مال القرض ( ر ) رد المحتار، الباب الأول من كتاب القضاء .(٣٤١/٤ ٧/٦٢ - واختلفت الاجتهادات في حكم وصية الصغير المميز: فأجاز الإمام الشافعي وفقهاء المدينة وصيَّته في وجوه البر. واستدلوا من جهة النظر بأنها خير لا يلحق الصغير منها ضرر في ماله، لأنها إنما بعد وفاته واستدلوا من جهة الأثر بما روي عن عمر بن الخطاب الله عنه أنه أجاز وصية صغير يافع. تنفذ رضي وحكم أبو حنيفة وأصحابه ببطلانها لإضرارها بحقوق ورثة الصغير. والوصية إنما شرعت للكبير المكلف ليتدارك بثوابها ما فاته من تقصير في أحد (1) الظاهر أن بطلان طلاق الصغير لا يمنع جواز دعوى طلب التفريق التي تقام من الزوجين على الآخر بمقتضى المادة / ۱۱۲ / من قانون الأحوال الشخصية الجديد لدينا، إذا ادعت الزوجة الكبيرة على زوجها الصغير بطلب التفريق، لأن هذا التفريق مخالعة جبرية أو تطليق جبري بسلطان القضاء عند عدم إمكان الإصلاح بين الزوجين. والقانون قد جعل طلب التفريق حقاً لكل من الزوجين، ولم يشرط في أحد منهما شرائط لصحة الادعاء به فيرجع في ذلك إلى القواعد العامة والقواعد تقضي بان ممارسة الحق تتوقف على أهلية صاحبه المدعي لا على أهلية الطرف الآخر المدعى عليه. فإذا كانت الزوجة كبيرة فالظاهر أن دعواها بالتفريق على زوجها الصغير القاصر وعة قانوناً وهذا كما لو كان الزوج مجنوناً، فإن جنونه لا يمنع زوجته من طلب التفريق بمقتضى أحكام القانون ومن المقرر شرعاً وقانوناً أن المجنون في حكم الصغير غير المميز كما صرحت به المادة /۹۷۹/ من المجلة، والمادة / ٤٧/ من القانون المدني الجديد الذي حل محلها. مسموعة
Chapter Eight: The Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. It qualifies but does not obligate; obligation is a charge (taklif) and it depends on its cause and all its legal conditions. The first of its conditions is puberty, by which the body's tolerance and the intellect's completion are achieved, so a person is not charged with any of the practical obligations before puberty. B - As for civil capacity for execution, or capacity for disposition, it is also established for the minor from the beginning of this stage, but in it is deficient and incomplete, and this deficiency is to protect his rights and safeguard his wealth, due to his lack of experience in financial disposition and his lack of knowledge of people's conditions. Division of the discerning minor's dispositions according to the deficiency of civil capacity for execution in him: 5/62 - According to this deficiency in civil capacity for execution in the discerning minor, the jurists divided his legal dispositions in terms of their validity and consequences into three types: the minor. 6/62 - The first type: dispositions that are pure financial harm to the minor. That is like donations of all kinds: gift, charity, waqf, loan for use, or others. This type the minor is not qualified to do, nor is anyone – guardian, executor, or judge – allowed to authorize it for him or do it on his behalf. If it occurs, it is void to protect the minor's rights due to the deficiency of his capacity. Of this type is the discerning minor's suretyship for a debt owed by another, for his suretyship is invalid because it contains the meaning of a donation by undertaking to pay the debt on behalf of another. The jurists have counted among this type the divorce by a discerning minor of his wife if he is married, for his divorce is invalid because he loses the dowry and the benefit of marriage (1). And they have excepted from this type the judge's lending of the orphan's property, even though lending initially has the meaning of donation, because it protects the orphan's property from loss, and it is better than deposit, because if the deposit perishes without transgression by the depositary or negligence in its preservation, it perishes as an unguaranteed trust, unlike a loan, which is guaranteed by the borrower in all circumstances; and with the judge's authority when he undertakes the lending, there is no fear for the loan property. (Radd al-Muhtar, Book of Judiciary, vol. 4 p. 341). 7/62 - The juristic opinions differed on the ruling of the discerning minor's will: Imam al-Shafi'i and the jurists of Medina permitted his will for charitable purposes. They argued from the perspective of reason that it is good and no harm to the minor's wealth, because it is only after his death; and they argued from hadith with what was reported from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he validated the will of a young adolescent. And Abu Hanifa and his companions ruled its invalidity because it harms the rights of the minor's heirs. The will is only legislated for the major who is accountable to compensate through its reward for whatever shortcoming in (text breaks off). (1) It appears that the invalidity of the minor's divorce does not prevent the permissibility of filing a suit for dissolution (tafriq) brought by either spouse against the other under Article 112 of our new Personal Status Law, if the adult wife sues for dissolution on her minor husband – because this dissolution is a compulsory khul' or a compulsory judicial divorce by the authority of the judiciary when reconciliation between the spouses is impossible. The law has made the right to request dissolution a right for each spouse, and it did not stipulate in either of them conditions for the validity of the claim; so reference is made to the general rules. The rules dictate that the exercise of a right depends on the capacity of its claimant, not on the capacity of the other party (the defendant). If the wife is an adult, it appears that her claim for dissolution against her minor, incompetent husband is admissible legally. This is like if the husband is insane, for his insanity does not prevent his wife from seeking dissolution under the provisions of the law; and it is established legally and Lawfully that the insane is considered like a nondiscerning minor, as stated in Article 979 of the Majalla and Article 47 of the new Civil Code that replaced it.
Source pages p. 756

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the classification of a discerning minor's transactions. The author divides the third stage of legal capacity (the stage of tamyiz) into three types based on whether the transaction is harmful, beneficial, or neutral. The first type is transactions that are pure financial harm to the minor, such as gifts, charity, waqf, and suretyship. These are void because the minor's capacity is deficient for protection. An exception is made for a judge lending orphan's money, which though nominally a donation, is beneficial as it safeguards the orphan's property. The passage then presents a dispute about the validity of a discerning minor's will. The Shafi'is and Medinan jurists permit it, relying on the precedent of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab. The Hanafis (Abu Hanifa and his companions) rule it void because it harms the heirs. The footnote discusses a modern application: the invalidity of a minor's divorce does not bar an adult wife from filing for judicial separation, as capacity is required of the claimant, not the defendant.

Key Terms
صغير مميز (saghir mumayyiz): A minor who has reached the age of discrimination (tamyiz), capable of understanding transactions but lacking full maturity
ضرر مالي محض (darar mali mahd): Transactions that are exclusively harmful to the minor's financial interests, such as donations
إقراض القاضي (iqrāḍ al-qāḍī): The judge's lending of orphan property as an exception to protect the orphan's wealth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a discerning minor donating a gift (hiba)?
A) Valid with guardian's permission
B) Valid if for a good cause
C) Void, regardless of permission
D) Suspended until adulthood

Key Takeaway
A discerning minor's donations are void to protect his wealth; only a judge may lend orphan money as an exception.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Discerning Minor's Will: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute over the validity of a discerning minor's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that a discerning minor's will is void and evaluate the Shafi'i argument based on 'Umar's precedent.

Core Idea
A discerning minor's will is void in the Hanafi school because it harms the heirs; Shafi'is permit it citing 'Umar's validation.

The passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute over the validity of a discerning minor's will (wasiyya). The Hanafis rule it void due to harm to the heirs, while the Shafi'is and Medinan jurists permit it, arguing that it only takes effect after death and citing the practice of Caliph 'Umar. This lesson unpacks the proofs and the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa and his companions hold that a discerning minor's will is invalid (bāṭil) because it harms the heirs' rights.
The will is legislated for the adult accountable (mukallaf).
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i and the Medinan jurists permit a discerning minor's will for charitable purposes.
They argue it is only after death, so no harm to the minor's wealth.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Rational proof: No harm since it takes effect after death. Textual proof: Report that 'Umar ibn al-Khattab validated a young adolescent's will.
The hadith is a key evidence.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that the will harms the heirs and that 'Umar's action may be specific or not broadly applicable. (The passage is truncated but implies the Hanafi response.)
The default rule is that only a mukallaf can make a will.
5
Determine the practical consequence
A discerning minor's will is void in Hanafi law, so the property goes to the heirs. In Shafi'i law, it is valid and must be executed.
This affects inheritance distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Liability of a Non-Permitted Discerning Minor: Dispute and Preponderant View
Dispute on liability of a non-permitted discerning minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on liability of a non-permitted discerning minor who destroys property received under a void contract and identify the preponderant view.

Core Idea
A non-permitted minor is not liable for destroying property because the owner's delivery caused the loss.

This passage introduces an internal Hanafi dispute that hinges on whether the minor's act of destruction is a pure tort or a consequence of a void contract. Understanding the reasoning behind each position illuminates how the school distinguishes between tort and contractual liability, and how permission (idhn) affects a minor's capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the issue
Read the passage and identify the three Hanafi imams involved: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The issue: a discerning minor (mumayyiz) who is not permitted by his guardian to trade (ghayr ma'dhun) enters a contract (purchase, borrowing, etc.), receives property, and then destroys it. Does he guarantee (tadmin) the value?
The minor has discernment (tamyiz) but lacks permission (idhn).
2
State Abu Yusuf's position and his proof
Abu Yusuf holds that the minor is liable. His proof: since the contract is void, it is disregarded; only the act of destruction remains, which is a tort. The minor is capable of tort liability even if non-discerning, so he must guarantee.
He treats the case as purely about tort (itlaf).
3
State Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's position and their proof
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the minor is not liable. Their proof: the act of destruction is not independent because the owner voluntarily handed the property to the minor, exposing it to risk. Thus, the liability would arise only from the contract, which the minor cannot undertake. Therefore, the owner bears the loss.
They treat the case as contractual in nature.
4
Identify the preponderant view and its basis
The passage states that the preponderant (rajih) position in the Hanafi school is non-liability. The tarjih is based on the reasoning that the owner's delivery negates the tort, and the minor lacks capacity for contractual guarantee.
This is the mufta bihi position for fatwa.
5
Contrast with the permitted minor
If the minor is permitted (ma'dhun), there is consensus that he is liable. This shows that permission transforms the nature of the transaction: the permitted minor can bear contractual liability.
The key variable is idhn (permission).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٢/٦٢ ـ وهنا تعترضنا مسألة خلافية مهمة وطريفة، الصغير المميز إذا لم يكن مأذوناً وتعاقد مع غيره على شيء وتسلمه منه على سبيل الشراء أو الاقتراض أو الاستعارة أو الإيداع مثلاً، ثم أتلفه الصغير فهل يضمنه أو لا يضمنه؟ ذهب أبو يوسف صاحب أبي حنيفة إلى التضمين نظراً إلى أن العقد من الصغير غير المأذون إذا لم ينفذ عليه فقد بقي فعل الإتلاف، وهو بذاته يوجب على الصغير ضمان ما يتلف ولو كان طفلاً غير مميز كما تقدم ر ف ٦/٦١. فالقضية هنا قضية ضمان فعل والصغير أهل لذلك؛ وليست قضية ضمان عقد. وذهب أبو حنيفة وصاحبه الآخر محمد بن الحسن الشيباني إلى عدم التضمين بحجة أن فعل الإتلاف هنا ساقط الاعتبار، لأنه بتسليط من الذي سلّم المال إلى صغير غير مأذون فعرضه للتلف، ولم يأخذه الصغير تلقاء نفسه فلم يبق إلا الضمان الواجب بالعقد. والصغير غير المأذون ليس بأهل لالتزام ضمان العقود. وهذا هو الرأي الراجح في المذهب الحنفي. • أما لو كان الصغير مأذوناً فهو ضامن بالاتفاق (ر: الأحوال الشخصية لقدري باشا م / ٤٨٧ - ٤٨٨ ، والدر المختار ورد المحتار كتاب الحجر ٥/ ۹۲ ، وآخر كتاب المأذون ١١٢/٥). وهذا حكم يستوي فيه الصغير المميز غير المأذون والصغير غير المميز كما تقدم لأن كليهما محجور (ر) ف ٣/٦١ و ٦/٦١).
12/62 - Here a significant and interesting disputed issue confronts us: If a discerning minor who is not permitted contracts with another person for something and receives it from him by way of purchase, borrowing, loan, or deposit, and then the minor destroys it, does he guarantee it or not? Abu Yusuf, the companion of Abu Hanifa, went to liability (tadmin) because the contract from the non-permitted minor, if not executed, leaves the act of destruction, and that act by itself obligates the minor to guarantee what he destroys even if he were a non-discerning child, as previously mentioned in 6/61. So the issue here is an issue of guarantee of an action, and the minor is capable of that; it is not an issue of guarantee of a contract. Abu Hanifa and his other companion Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani went to non-liability (adam al-tadmin) on the grounds that the act of destruction here is disregarded because it was by the permission of the one who handed the property to the non-permitted minor, thus exposing it to destruction, and the minor did not take it on his own. So nothing remains except the guarantee necessitated by the contract. And the non-permitted minor is not capable of undertaking guarantee of contracts. And this is the preponderant view in the Hanafi school. • But if the minor is permitted, he is liable by agreement (see: al-Ahwal al-Shakhsiyya by Qadri Pasha, article 487-488, and al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar, Kitab al-Hajr, 5/92, and end of Kitab al-Ma'dhun, 5/112). And this ruling is the same for the discerning non-permitted minor and the non-discerning minor as previously mentioned because both are interdicted (see 3/61 and 6/61).
Source pages p. 761

Commentary
This passage presents an internal Hanafi dispute regarding the liability of a discerning minor who is not permitted to trade (ghayr ma'dhun). The issue arises when such a minor enters a contract (e.g., purchase, borrowing) and subsequently destroys the property he received. Abu Yusuf argues that since the contract is void (not binding), the minor's act of destruction remains as a pure tort, and the minor is liable for damages just as any non-discerning child would be. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, argue that the act of destruction is not actionable because the property was entrusted to the minor by the owner voluntarily, who knowingly gave it to an interdicted minor. Therefore, the owner's act of delivery caused the loss, and the minor's liability would only arise from the contract, which is invalid due to lack of capacity. The preponderant position (rajih) in the Hanafi school is non-liability for the non-permitted minor. However, if the minor is permitted (ma'dhun), he is liable by consensus. This distinction highlights the significance of permission (idhn) in determining the minor's capacity to bear contractual obligations.

Key Terms
صغير مميز (saghir mumayyiz): A minor who has reached the age of discernment (tamyiz), typically around seven years old, capable of understanding the basic nature of transactions but still under interdiction.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A minor who has been granted explicit or implicit permission by his legal guardian to engage in trade (tijarah).
تضمين (tadmin): Liability to guarantee or compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the preponderant Hanafi view on the liability of a non-permitted discerning minor who destroys property received under a void contract?
A) The minor is liable because he committed a tort.
B) The minor is liable because the contract is void, so the act stands alone.
C) The minor is not liable because the owner voluntarily gave the property to a minor who cannot undertake contracts.
D) The minor is not liable because he is not discerning.

Key Takeaway
The liability of a non-permitted discerning minor for destroying property received under a void contract is a matter of internal Hanafi dispute, with the preponderant view being non-liability due to the owner's delivery.
